I sat in my apartment with the radiator clearing its throat and I built the scene the way I'd build a sit-down: anticipated her opening, anticipated three of her likely escalations, drafted my responses, drafted the de-escalations under the responses, identified the moment she would cry — there's always a moment, you can usually call it to the half-hour — and pre-positioned the thing I'd say into the crying, the thing that lands softest in a wet face. I even, God help me, considered the lighting. The reflex came up the back of my throat like heat — *meet her somewhere with a window behind you, side-lit, never overhead, overhead reads as interrogation* — and I let it, because I have stopped being able to tell my reflexes from my judgment and on a good day I tell myself that's just expertise.

I have prepared for every version of Delphine except the one I'm about to get, which is the one where she isn't a client. I forgot to prepare for that. You can't prep a person who refuses to want anything you have.

She is ten minutes late, which I take, at first, as a kind of gift — late is human, late is data, late tells you something — and then she crosses the street toward me and I understand within four steps that she is not late because of traffic or nerves or strategy. She is late because she was somewhere that mattered and it ran long, and she has come straight from it, and it is on her like a smell. I'd find out later it was a smell. I'd find out later it was the exact smell.

"Mona," she says.

That's it. That's the whole opening. Not *Mona* with a hook in it, not *Mona* loaded, not the *Mona* I'd drafted four responses to. Just my name, said by a person who has clearly said it to herself a number of times over fifteen years and used up all the charge in it, so that now it's only a word that means me.

"Delphine," I say, and I stand, and there's the small terrible choreography of two people deciding the distance, and she decides it — she hugs me, briefly, the way you'd steady someone on a train, and steps back before I've finished arranging my arms, so that I'm left doing the back half of a hug into air, which is, I will realize, the entire afternoon in one gesture.

---

She's a hospice chaplain.

I want to put that down plainly because it landed on me plainly, in the coffee place, over the ceramic plate of pastries neither of us touched, and I have spent my life making sure things don't land plainly, so when one does I owe it the plainness.

She sits with the dying. That's the job. Not the doctor, not the family, not the one with the chart or the morphine or the last name. The one who comes and sits. People are leaving the world and she is the person in the chair. She told me this the way you'd tell someone what bus you take — no weight on it, no bid for the reaction I could feel myself getting ready to have — and I sat there with my professional face on, the one that's interested and warm and slightly impressed in a way that flatters the speaker, and somewhere under the professional face the real one was doing math I didn't authorize.

Because here is what I do. I'll say it cleanly, once, because she made me see it clean. I manufacture, for money, the appearance of a thing happening in a room. A man feels nothing and I make a stranger at a bus stop believe she has witnessed a soul. That's the whole trade — the counterfeit of presence, retailed at four hundred dollars an hour, the fake version of being-with-someone-in-the-worst-moment, scored for the appearance of honesty, clipped, shipped, billed. And my sister, the one whose worst moment I authored, does the real version. For almost nothing. All day. She walks into the actual worst moment, the unfixable one, the one with no comeback retainer and no second helping, and she does not bring a script, because there is no script, because the whole point — she said this, not as a thesis, just as a fact about her Tuesdays — *the whole point is you don't bring anything, you just don't leave.*

"That sounds hard," I say, which is a sentence I have personally trained sixteen people to deliver with the correct dampness, and hearing it come out of my own mouth at her is one of the worse moments of my professional life.

"It's the least hard thing I've ever done," she says. "It's the only thing I've ever done where I don't have to figure out what to say." She picks a crumb off the untouched pastry and looks at it instead of me, kindly, which I'll learn is how she does mercy — she gives you somewhere else to put your eyes. "You'd hate it. There's no technique. You just have to be there with the truth of it and not improve it."

*Not improve it.* I file that. I file it the way you file a thing you have no business filing, the way I filed Garrett's room, and I hate that I'm filing it even as I file it, and I file it anyway. The professional reflex does not turn off because the person across the table is the one person it should turn off for. If anything it turns up. It is the only language I'm fully fluent in and I have come to the one place it doesn't translate, and like every monoglot abroad my instinct is to say it louder.

---

Theo comes to meet us partway through, because of course he does, because she texted him *we're here* and he was three blocks over.

He's a carpenter. I'd half-hoped for something I could be quietly superior about — a wellness brand, a podcast, a man who says *journey* — and instead he's a carpenter, actual sawdust, a guy who builds the kind of stairs that don't clear their throats, and he's got that specific solidity of a person who makes things that have to bear weight or kill someone, which is a moral category I respect more than my whole industry. He shakes my hand. The hand is a hand that's been used. He says, "I've heard a lot about you," and there is not one grain of edge in it, no subtext, no managed surface, and I stand there having no idea what to do with a sentence that means only what it says, like a customs officer handed a document with nothing hidden in the lining.

"All of it bad, I hope," I say, because the joke is the only door I've got and I will, it turns out, keep opening it all afternoon and walking into the same wall.

"Some of it," he says, easily, "was funny," and squeezes Delphine's shoulder and says he'll let us, and goes — and she watches him go with a face that has nothing to sell, a face just watching a man it likes walk away to give it room, and I think: that's a Duchenne marker. That's the real *orbicularis oculi*, the one Sloan can spend a career failing to coach. My sister's face does for free, about a carpenter, the thing I bill plutocrats by the hour to fake.

I don't say that. I do, however, hear myself say, "He seems lovely," in a register I recognize from green rooms, the register where you've decided someone is beneath comment and are commenting anyway, and Delphine catches it. Of course she catches it. She does the worst thing anyone has done to me in years.

She lets it be true.

"He is," she says. "He's the most boring kind of good person there is. I waited my whole life for boring." And she smiles, not at me, just out at the street, and there's no point underneath it for me to grab, no hook, no wound I can fit my hand into. I came armed for a fight, and a fight I could have done something with — a fight has structure, a fight has a shape I can manage, a fight wants something, and I am very, very good at finding what a thing wants. She doesn't want the fight. She doesn't seem, terribly, to want anything from me at all, and I realize, sitting there with my cold expensive coffee, that I do not have a single tool that works on a person who has stopped needing me to be sorry.

---

I make my move around the second cup, because I've timed it, because there's a window where the small talk has warmed the room enough that a real thing can land without clattering, and I have built my career inside that window, and I step into it the way I step into everything, on the balls of my feet.

"Delphine," I say. "I want to say something. About — about the funeral. About what I said at your dad's funeral. I've wanted to say it for a long time, and I've never — " and here I do the thing, the real thing, the thing I am best in the country at, I let my voice find the small catch on *never*, not a big one, a hairline, the half-beat that reads as a person whose composure is costing them, and I feel it land in my own chest with the heavy click of a sentence built right, and I think, with the part of me that is always, always watching the panel: *that's good. That's clean. That'll move her.*

She looks at me.

She lets me get to the end of it. That's the thing. She doesn't interrupt, she doesn't escalate, she doesn't give me the cry I'd called for the half-hour, she just — listens, with the whole still attention of a woman whose job is being in a chair while someone says the hardest thing they've got, and when I'm done, when I've delivered the apology I'd drafted on a bench across a river, the good one, the one with the catch in exactly the right place, she's quiet for a second, and then she says, gently, the way you'd tell someone they've got something on their face:

"You've gotten really good."

And I — 

I don't have a response drafted for that. I drafted for tears and for fury and for the door and for a long cold silence I could fill. I did not draft for *you've gotten really good,* because it is not a counter-move, it is not in the game at all, it is a woman stepping outside the ring and turning the lights on so I can see I've been shadowboxing.

"That's not — " I start.

"It's okay," she says. "I'm not being mean. You really have. I could feel the place where your voice was going to go before it went there, and then it went there, and it was — " she does a small gesture, a flat hand, level, *just so* — "it was really good. You've spent fifteen years getting good at exactly that, haven't you. That little — " and she does it, she does the catch, on a word, a tiny dry imitation of the thing I'd done, perfect, devastating, my own technique handed back to me across a ceramic plate, "—that."

I am the most fluent woman alive. I make my living finding the one sentence. And I sit in a coffee shop that is sincerely a chain and I cannot find a single one, because every sentence I reach for, I can hear her hearing the place where it's going to go before it goes there, and a sentence you can hear being heard isn't a sentence, it's a tell.

"I'm not performing," I say, which is the single least true thing a performer can say, and which I hear leave my mouth already wearing makeup, and she lets that be true too. She just waits. She is so comfortable in a silence. It's the worst weapon I've ever come up against and she isn't even holding it like a weapon, she's just resting in it, the way you'd rest in a chair, the way she rests in chairs professionally beside the dying, who also cannot perform, who are also past it, and I understand with a slow horror that to my sister I am, right now, no harder to sit with than any of them. I am just one more person in a worse moment than they'll admit, saying it wrong, and she has all afternoon.

---

"I didn't bring you out here for that," she says, finally, when she's decided I've had enough.

"For what."

"For the apology." She says the word the way you'd handle something that's gone off — not cruelly, just at arm's length, a thing you don't want too near the food. "I know what an apology is. I see a lot of them, in my work, you'd be surprised, the dying are full of them, and the families — God, the families. Everybody's got a speech. I sit through speeches all day." She looks at me, and now she does the thing that scares me more than fury would have, more than the door, she looks at me with something I'd have to call tenderness if I were going to be honest, which I'm trying, for once, to be. "I didn't ask you here for a speech, Mona. You're the best speech-writer there is. I've got no use for the thing you're best at. That's not — that's not where you and I have a problem."

"Then where do we have a problem," I say, and it comes out smaller than I meant it, with no catch in it at all, the first ungarnished sentence I've managed all day, and she notices that too, and something in her face opens half an inch, like a door cracked to see who's there.

"That's better," she says, softly. "That one. More of that one." And then, before I can do anything with the opening, before I can identify what just worked and reach for it again — and she sees me start to, she sees the closer in me crouch to repeat the move — she stands. She gathers her bag. She does it without hurry and without coldness, the way she does everything, the way I imagine she gathers her bag in a hospice room when the visit is over and the next worst moment is two doors down.

"I have to get back," she says. "There's — someone I sit with. It's close, this week. I don't like to be far." She pauses, and for one second the afternoon's whole weather changes, a cloud over the charming street, and it isn't about me at all, which is somehow how I know it's the realest thing she's said. "It's family stuff, partly. There's going to be — we'll all be in a room again before too long, you should know that. The kind of room we were in last time." She looks at me, level. "You'll want to think about what you'd actually say in it. Not the good version. The version you mean. There's a question in it for you, and you're not going to be able to write your way around this one. I'd start now."

"What room," I say. "Delphine. What family stuff."

But she's already saying it'll keep, it isn't for the sidewalk, she'll call me — *she'll call me,* fifteen years and she says it like we talk — and she hugs me again, the same brief steadying hug, here-and-gone, and this time I get my arms up in time and there's nothing on the other end of them to hold, she's already half a step back, already turning, already crossing the charming street toward whoever's close this week, leaving me on the corner with two cold coffees and a pastry no one ate and the dawning, ruinous understanding that I have just been handled — gently, completely, mercifully handled — by the only person on earth I have ever needed to handle, and that she did it the way I never could, by wanting nothing, by performing nothing, by simply not leaving until I'd run out of the thing I came to do.

---

I take the bus that comes when it feels like it. I take the train back over the river. Somewhere around the water I get my phone out, because of course I do, because the phone is where I'm brave, and I open the notes app, and my thumbs go to the keys with the muscle memory of a thousand drafts, and I start, without quite deciding to, to write down what she wants.

*What D. actually wants:* I type. And then I stop, because that's an audience-profile header, that's a thing I'd type at the top of a sit-down brief, *what the principal actually wants underneath the ask,* and I'm doing it, I'm doing it to her, on a train, twenty minutes after she stood in a coffee shop and showed me with my own technique that she can see me do it. I delete it. I delete it letter by letter the way I delete the other one, the one with no title, holding the key down to watch it go.

The thing is, I don't know what she wants. For the first time in my professional life I have read a person clean and come back with nothing — no ask under the ask, no lever, no soft place. She wanted nothing. She gave me a question I can't see the shape of yet and a room I can't see and a deadline she didn't name, and a single instruction I understood completely and cannot follow, which is to figure out the version I mean and not the good version, when the good version is the only version I have, when the good version is the entire architecture of me, fifteen years of it, load-bearing all the way down.

The train goes into the tunnel under the river. My reflection comes up in the black window, side-lit by the car's bad fluorescent, overhead-and-interrogation, exactly the lighting I'd have told a client to avoid, and I look at it, the most fluent woman alive looking at herself in a dark window with nothing to say, and I think the only plain thing I think all day:

She does the real one. I do the fake one. And the fake one is the only one I've got, and she's the only person who was ever going to need the real one, and she has just told me, kindly, over a pastry, that she can hear exactly where my voice is going to go before it goes there.

The train comes out the other side. The city again. Tomorrow there's a meeting I don't want about a rival shop that's eating our clients with a machine, and I'll go, and I'll be sharp in it, because being sharp in a room is the one thing I can still do when the other thing won't work, the way you favor the good leg. I put the phone away. I don't open the untitled doc that night. There's nothing to put in it I couldn't hear myself perform, and now there's someone alive who can hear it too, and she does it across a river, in a chair, beside whoever's close this week, by simply not leaving — and I came back over the water with two cold coffees and a header I had to delete, and the bare, unfileable fact that for once I was outmatched by a person who wasn't even trying to win.


---

# Chapter 11 — Let the Machine Carry the Lie

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 8/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

You can always tell which floor is panicking by what they put in the coffee.

When the firm is winning, the partners' kitchen runs the single-origin from the place that roasts on Tuesdays, the beans with the little card that tells you the farmer's first name and his elevation. When the firm is frightened, the card disappears, the good machine breaks in a way no one schedules a repair for, and we are all back on the brown stuff from the canister with the lid that doesn't fit, drinking it black because the half-and-half went the way of the farmer's first name. I come in Monday morning still carrying Delphine in my chest like a swallowed stone — I will get to her — and the first thing I learn, before I've taken my coat off, is that the half-and-half is gone, which means the threat is real, which means it's the Loop.

Renata has called what the calendar calls an All-Hands and what everyone else calls the part of the disaster movie where the scientist explains the asteroid. The conference room with the river view no one looks at is full for the first time since the holiday party where a strategist proposed to a strategist and it didn't take. There's a slideshow. Renata does not normally allow slideshows; she considers them a confession that you couldn't hold the room with your face. The existence of the slideshow tells me more than the slideshow does.

"Veritas Loop," says the slideshow.

"We don't say the name," says Renata, to the slideshow, the way you correct a child in front of guests.

---

Here is what the Loop is, stripped of the forty minutes of Renata not saying its name: it is a competitor that does what we do, except it does it without us. You feed it the scandal, the public, the receipts, the shape of the wronged crowd, and it gives you back an apology calibrated — and this is the word that has the whole floor sleeping badly — *statistically optimal* for forgiveness. Not the best-written apology. The most *forgiven* one. Tuned to the exact pitch a given audience will pardon, the way you'd tune a string to a fork you can't hear.

I want to be careful describing it, because the truth is I can't describe it, because no one can, because it is a black box that even its salespeople call a black box, fondly, the way you'd call a baby a little monster — with the pride of someone who made a thing they don't have to understand to love. I don't know how it works. I know what it costs, which is less than I cost, and I know what it does, which is win.

"It is a tool," Renata is saying. "We have always used tools. We sentiment-score. We clip-predict. The juniors—" she gestures at the juniors, who arrange their faces into the expressions of people not currently updating their résumés under the table— "the juniors run instruments all day. This is an instrument."

"It's an instrument that doesn't need the orchestra," says Babs, from the back, not loudly.

Renata pretends not to hear her, which is how I know she heard her, because Renata hears everything; pretending not to is one of the few honest things she does.

---

The slideshow has a slide that is just a number. The number is the percentage of contested apologies, in some study the Loop commissioned and therefore I trust the way I'd trust a man who tells me his own dog is friendly, in which the machine's draft outperformed the human's. I will not give you the number. Renata gives us the number. The room makes the sound a room makes when it's been told the asteroid's diameter — a soft collective exhale that's trying to be skepticism and is mostly arithmetic.

What no one says, in the forty minutes, is the thing the number means, which is not *the machine is good.* We knew the machine was good; goodness is cheap; I am good. The thing the number means is *the machine is more forgiven than we are.* And forgiveness was the whole product. Forgiveness was the only thing on the invoice. We do not sell apologies; an apology is a Tuesday, Renata said once, and she was right; we sell the *result*, the softening, the turn, the moment the comment sections cool from hate to *well, he gave a lot of it back.* That's the deliverable. And if a box that costs less than my dental plan delivers more of it than I do, then I am not a worse machine. I am a more expensive one. With opinions.

I have spent fifteen years believing the thing I added was the thing that couldn't be automated. I have never once let myself ask what, precisely, I thought that thing was, because some questions you keep in a drawer you don't open, next to the warranty for an appliance you no longer own. The slide with the number opens the drawer. The warranty is in there. The appliance is gone.

"So we differentiate," Renata says, and clicks to the slide that says DIFFERENTIATE, which has a picture of an artisan loaf on it, because someone in brand thinks we are sourdough and the Loop is a Wonder factory, and the unbearable part is that the metaphor is correct and also the metaphor is *us losing.* Nobody at scale is choosing the loaf. The loaf is a thing you choose when you can afford to lose the argument about lunch.

---

There's a woman at the back of the room I don't recognize, which at PHS is itself an event, because we are a firm where everyone has read everyone's file. She's in a soft grey that is more expensive than it is trying to look, and she's the only person not pretending the slideshow isn't happening to her; she's watching it with the warm, patient attention of a doctor watching a family describe symptoms she diagnosed from the doorway. She catches me clocking her and does a small thing with her face — not a smile, a *pre*-smile, the held breath before one — that I recognize, because I sell it, because it tests at plus-six for trustworthiness over the actual smile, which reads as a sale.

She finds me at the coffee that proves we're losing.

"You're Mona," she says. "I've read everything you've never signed."

This is, I will admit, a good line. I would have charged for it.

"That's most of my work," I say.

"That's *all* the good work. The signed stuff is for the men." She puts out a hand. "Priya Vance. I'm with the people your boss won't name." She says it the way you'd confess to being from a town the host finds embarrassing — gently, with affection for the town. "I'm not here to gloat. Renata invited me, actually. Which I keep telling people is either very brave or—"

"It's both," I say. "It's always both with Renata. That's the whole skill."

Priya laughs, and it is a real laugh, the kind with the eye-muscle in it, the Duchenne thing, the involuntary thing Sloan can't coach, and I note it the way I note everything, and I hate that I note it, and the noting is the disease, and I drink the bad coffee.

---

Here is the part I did not expect, and I have built a career on expecting parts.

I expected the Loop's salesperson to be a monster. Or a missionary — bright-eyed, frictionless, the kind of true believer who says *democratizing accountability* and means it, whom you can dismiss by the second sentence. Priya is neither. Priya is *kind.* She is kind in the specific, dangerous, professional way of a person who has decided that kindness is the most efficient thing in the room and turns out to be right. She does not pitch me the machine. She asks about my week.

I tell her it's been a week. I do not tell her about Delphine. I would not tell Delphine about Delphine.

"You look tired," Priya says, which from anyone else this morning would be a knife and from her is just a reading, accurate, gently offered, a doctor naming the symptom you came in not to mention. "Not today-tired. The other kind."

"It's a tired industry."

"It's a tiring *position*." She says *position* like she's setting it down carefully on a table between us. "You're the one who finds the sentence. Everyone here drafts; you close. I read three of your closes last night — I can tell which line is yours in any document on earth, did you know that? You have a tell. You always put the truest thing in third, where the eye relaxes. It's beautiful. It's also—" she tips her head, sorry in advance— "it's a lot to carry. Being the one who has to mean it. Every time. For people who don't."

"I don't mean it," I say. "That's the job. Meaning it is a liability."

"No," Priya says, kindly, the way you correct a child you like. "You mean it. That's why it works. That's the part the box can't do and you can, and it's killing you, and you've decided the killing is the price of being good, because if it didn't cost you it wouldn't count." She lets that sit. The bad coffee cools in my hand. "What if it didn't have to cost you. What if you didn't have to be the one standing inside every sentence. What if you could write the thing and let the machine carry the lie, so you don't have to."

---

I would like to report that I laughed at her. I have laughed at better.

I don't laugh, because somewhere under the swallowed stone of my sister, in the part of me that has been awake all weekend doing arithmetic on a document I can't title, a door I did not know was a door comes open the width of a held breath, and behind it is the single most disqualifying thought I have ever had, which is: *what if I let it carry mine.*

Not the firm's lies. Mine. The one at home. `untitled.docx`, fifteen years open, the four words I can't get bad enough to be true. What if I fed it the scandal — the funeral, the graveside, the cruel half-invented sentence — and the public, which is a public of one, Delphine, the unforecastable, the woman my model returned an error on — and the box gave me back the version *statistically optimal for forgiveness*, the calibrated apology, and I shipped it under my own name to the one person it's for, and she read it, and she softened, and the comment section of her face cooled from hate to *well, she came,* and I would be released, finally, without ever having stood inside the sentence in my own voice, and no one would ever know it wasn't mine, including, possibly, me.

The mercy of it is what undoes me. Priya hasn't pitched a tool. She's pitched an absolution I don't have to author. That's the back door I built the whole firm to be — *forgiven without ever having to ask* — except now there's a version where I don't even have to be the one who writes the not-asking. The machine asks. I just sign. I am very good at signing things other people meant.

"You went somewhere," Priya says, watching my face with that patient warmth, and for one bad second I think she's read it off me, the whole thing, the graveside, the door, and then I remember she can only read the symptom, not the diagnosis, no one can read the diagnosis, that's the whole point of the diagnosis, and I put the face back on — the modest, mildly-amused, *I-found-the-sentence-it-was-there-all-along* face — and her pre-smile resolves into the real one, the one with the eye-muscle, and I understand she let me see her decide to let me off the hook, which is the kindest, most lethal sale I have ever been worked, and I have worked some.

"I have a client," I say.

"You have a client," she agrees. "You always will. Until you don't." She gives me a card that is just a name and a number, no logo, which is its own flex — *we don't need to tell you who we are, you'll defect when you're ready* — and she's gone into the differentiating crowd, leaving me with the bad coffee and the open door I can't get shut.

---

Babs is at my elbow. Babs is always at my elbow at exactly the moment I'd pay her not to be.

"She offered you mercy," Babs says. It's not a question. Babs apologized for something real, once, and won't say what, and I have always assumed it's the reason she can smell mercy on a person from across a losing room.

"She offered me a product."

"Same thing, in here." Babs eats an almond. "That's the new pitch, you know. Not *we're better*. *We're kinder.* They figured out the thing we never would, which is that the people who do this job are dying of it, quietly, at their desks, and the box doesn't die. So they're not selling the clients a better apology. They're selling *us* a way to stop bleeding." She looks at me, and for once there's no serenity in it, just the flat tired honesty of a woman who has been here longer than the half-and-half. "It's a good pitch, Mona. It's the only pitch that ever scared me. Everything before this, we could out-write. You can't out-write *you don't have to anymore.*"

"What did you say to it. When they pitched you."

Babs is quiet for a second, which Babs never is.

"I said I was already retired," she says. "I just hadn't told the building."

---

The All-Hands breaks the way meetings about asteroids break: with no decision, a follow-up scheduled, and everyone slightly more aware of their own mortality and slightly hungrier. The juniors disperse to update the résumés they were already updating. The strategists return to the highlight on the phone, because the strategists believe, sincerely, that nothing can happen to *them*, which is the one thing the machine truly can't replicate, that particular grade of stupidity, and they should put it on the differentiation slide. The handsome useless men are, it turns out, the moat.

I sit a minute in the emptying room with the river no one looks at, and I do the thing I do, which is I take the morning apart to see what it cost, because everything costs, Marnie taught me that, everyone's sorry's got a price tag, hon, even yours, what're you getting out of yours, and the answer this morning is the worst one yet, because I'm not getting out of mine, I'm trying to get the box to *carry* mine, which is one cynicism past the one Marnie caught me at. Marnie thought I wanted to *feel clean.* Marnie was generous. I don't want to feel clean. I want to be released without doing the one thing release requires, and I have just been shown a machine that can almost do it, and the only thing stopping me is a fact so small I can barely hold it, which Priya handed me without knowing she did: *the box can't carry the lie, because the box doesn't know the lie is also true.*

That's the gap. That's the whole human remainder, the artisan loaf, the thing nobody at scale will pay for. The Loop produces a flawless sorry. It cannot produce a *failed* one — the clumsy kind, the kind that's true because it's badly said, the kind where the voice catches not because you set the catch with tweezers but because the sentence got away from you on its way out and dragged something real up by the root. The machine is optimized. Optimization, by definition, cannot make the un-optimized thing. It can't mean it badly. It's never said anything worse than well.

Neither, of course, have I.

I write that down. Not for the pitch deck. For the drawer with the warranty in it. *The box can't mean it badly. Find out if you can.* And I look at it, in my own hand, the truest note I've made all week, filed where I file the ones nobody's allowed to have, and I understand that I have just diagnosed, precisely, the thing that might save me and the thing I am least equipped on earth to do, which are the same thing, which is always the way, which is the whole sick architecture of being good at this.

---

The good machine is still broken. I drink the bad coffee. The door Priya opened does not close; I have spent the morning establishing that I, personally, do not own a way to close it. It just sits there at the width of a held breath, the way `untitled.docx` sits open on a laptop across town, the way a box sits in a building Renata won't name, kindly, patiently, waiting to carry anything I hand it.

I'm scraping the cold sludge into the sink, telling myself I'll think about Delphine on the train and knowing I'll think about Delphine on the train the way you know weather, when Dev sticks his head in, and his open face is doing the thing it does when the building has handed him a person he doesn't know how to feel sorry for yet.

"There's a guy in three," he says. "Tech. Grief app. The dead-people-still-texting-you thing — you saw the headline." He winces, which is Dev's whole career in one muscle. "He's apologized like four times already. On his own. It's not — it's not landing. He says the public's exhausted." A beat. "He says he needs us to make him apologize *less.*"

"Of course he does," I say.

"And, Mona—" Dev lowers his voice, near the door, the elevator voice, the voice for the thing you shouldn't say in the room. "I pulled his file. He's already a client. Of theirs." He doesn't say the name either. Nobody says the name. "He's running both. Us and the box. Same face, two drafts. He wants to see which one—" Dev stops, because even Dev can hear how the sentence ends.

Which one's better at the soul.

"Tell him I'll be right in," I say, and I rinse the cup, and I dry my hands, and I go to write an apology against a machine for a man who is, right now, in room three, holding a phone that still gets texts from someone who's dead, because we built a world where even the grief is a subscription, and I'd find it funny, I'd find it so funny, except the door's still open, and the box is still waiting, and somewhere on my own laptop the warranty's run out on the only thing I was ever sure I added.


---

# Chapter 12 — The Robot Cried and I Didn't

Act II-a  ·  Fluency gauge: 7.5/10  ·  Artifact: YES

---

**COLE TRAN — STATEMENT ON THE PASSING OF A USER · TWO DRAFTS, SIDE BY SIDE**

| MINE | THEIRS |
|---|---|
| To the family of the woman we lost. ~~I am so sorry.~~ [INS: There are no words, and I will not pretend there are.] | To the family who is grieving today, and to everyone who trusted us with the people they loved: I am listening. I will keep listening for as long as you need me to. |
| *[margin: "no words" is a word. they know it's a word. but it buys him out of saying the actual ones, which is the job.]* | |
| ~~The product did what it was built to do.~~ [INS: We built something to keep love close, and we did not build it carefully enough.] | We built this so that no goodbye would ever feel like the last one. We were wrong about what people needed, and being wrong about love is the only kind of wrong that matters. |
| *[margin: he cannot say the product worked. the product working is the lawsuit. make the failure a failure of CARE, not of code.]* | *[no margin notes in this column. there are none anywhere in this column. it does not strike anything out. it does not doubt. it arrived clean.]* |
| ~~I knew the retention numbers were highest among the bereaved and I did not look at why.~~ | I have spent these weeks asking myself a question I should have asked at the start: not whether grief could be eased, but whether it should be hurried. |
| *[margin: cut. true. file it. — theirs is better and I can see exactly why and I would like that on the record.]* | |

---

Here is a thing I did not used to know about myself: I can be jealous of software.

I find this out at four in the afternoon, in the small reading room on nineteen, sitting across from Cole Tran, who has just made me cry with a sentence I did not write, and the cruelty of it is that I'm not crying because it's beautiful. I'm crying because it's better than mine. I am thirty seconds into the first real grief I've felt in years and the grief is *professional*.

Let me back up.

Cole Tran is thirty-three and he is sorry in the way that some people are tall — without effort, constitutionally, all the way down. He built a thing. You've used it, or someone you love has, and you both pretend you haven't. You feed it the texts from a dead person — the dead person's texts, all of them, every *running late xx* and every *did you eat* — and it learns the dead person, and then it texts you. Forever. *Did you eat.* He called the launch tagline *the conversation doesn't have to end,* and for about three years the conversation did not have to end and the valuation did nothing but climb, and then a woman in Ohio kept the conversation going for fourteen months and did not eat, and did not eat, and now there is a family, and a coroner's line item, and Cole Tran across the table from me with the soft devastated face of a man who has apologized so many times the apology has worn a groove he can no longer climb out of.

"You're over-sorry," I tell him. "It's a real condition. You have it."

"I am sorry, though."

"I know. That's the problem. You're so fluent in it the public's stopped hearing the words. You apologize like other people breathe. Three statements in nine days, Cole. A man who issues three statements in nine days isn't sorry, he's *bleeding,* and people don't forgive a wound, they back away from it."

He nods. He has the most apology-native face I've ever directed — the whole generation has it, this readiness to be contrite, this muscle developed early and overused, like a tongue that's tasted nothing but its own teeth. The Hartleys believed their fake marriage. Cole believes his real remorse, which is somehow more useless. There's no gap in him to work in. The man is *all* gap.

"I need you to apologize *less,*" I say. "I need you to say one thing, once, and then I need you to shut up and let people decide if they believe you, which they will not do while you're still talking, because you sound like a confession that's afraid of being interrupted."

"That's good," he says. "Write that down."

I do not tell him I already did, in the margin, for myself.

---

We run the reading. This is the part the public never sees and would pay to: the figure stands at the lectern with the bad foam behind him and performs the contrition to a panel that scores it, and we direct him like he's auditioning for the role of himself, which he is.

Dev is on the panel, taking notes he doesn't need. Sloan is here for the face. Two of the junior pool are running sentiment off in the corner, where they live, murmuring numbers into the dark like a séance for a market.

Cole reads my draft. He's good. He's *too* good — every catch in the voice arrives on schedule, every wet eye stays wet without spilling, the half-beat before the hard word lands like he's hitting a mark with tape on the floor, which, professionally, he is. And it reads as nothing. It reads as a man who has rehearsed grief until grief became choreography, which is exactly what's happened, which is exactly the problem, and watching him I have the unpleasant sensation of looking into a mirror that has learned to tie its own tie.

"Flat," one of the juniors says. "Sincerity's reading at — it's reading lower than the silence before he started. He apologized and the number went *down.*"

"Because they've heard him," Babs says, from the doorway, where she has appeared the way she appears, holding a tea she will not drink. "You can't sell a man a coat he's already wearing."

Sloan leans toward the monitor with the tenderness of a man at an aquarium. "His face is doing everything correctly," he says, mournful. "Every marker. The orbicularis, the asymmetry, the micro — it's all there, it's a beautiful read, and it's *worthless,* because correct is now the tell. We have trained the public to distrust the very signatures we spent thirty years learning to forge. We taught them to read the watermark. So now the real bill and the counterfeit both look fake." He sits back, undone by it, a forger weeping over the death of paper. "It's the saddest thing I've ever studied."

"Halvard," I say, "it's a man lying about a dead woman, but go off."

"That too," Sloan says, comfortably, and the joke lands wrong.

I notice it land wrong. That's new. Six months ago that line clears the room — Dev does the cat-noise, Babs almost drinks her tea — and today it just sits there on the carpet being a thing I said about a dead woman to get a laugh, and I hear it the way you hear your own voice on a recording, a half-second behind, in someone else's register, and I think: *that wasn't funny, that was a reflex with a punchline taped to it.* I have always had the reflex. I've never heard it before. There's a difference, and the difference is the worst thing that's happened to me all week.

I keep my face professional. Sloan would clock it if I didn't, and I am not a client.

---

After, Cole wants a drink, which is a thing clients want and you give them, because a man who's been managed all afternoon needs to feel briefly like the manager, and the bar two floors down exists for exactly this transaction.

He's three in before he says it. I should have seen it coming — they always tell you the true thing at the bottom of the third drink, it's practically in the deliverables — but I don't see it coming, because I'm not listening, because I'm doing the thing where I run the room's exits and clip-predict the bartender, and I miss the windup entirely.

"Can I tell you something," Cole says, "and you can't put it in the statement."

"That's the only kind of thing I want to hear."

"I used the other firm too." He turns the glass. "The — you know. The Loop. I ran it through theirs. Just to see. I wasn't going to use it, I just — I wanted to know what it'd say." He laughs, a small wet awful laugh. "Their draft made me cry, Mona. Yours didn't. I read theirs alone in my car and I cried for the first time about this whole — about *her,* the woman, the actual woman, and it wasn't even — it didn't even have a name in it, it just said the thing right, and I sat in my car and I cried, and then I drove home." He looks up at me, and his over-sorry face is, for once, not performing anything, which is how I know what's coming will be true and will cost. "How do you stand it. A job where the robot's better at the soul."

I have a thing I say here. I have several. I have one about how the machine has read every apology ever written and I've only read the ones that worked, so of course it's optimized, optimization is just averaging with confidence, the machine is a committee of every coward who ever said sorry and that's why it's smooth, it's *smooth because no one in it is afraid.* It's a good answer. It's true, even. I open my mouth to deploy it.

What comes out is: "I don't know."

Cole nods like I've said something. Maybe I have. He pays — he insists, a man buying back one inch of agency — and he goes, and I sit at the bar with the answer I didn't give going stale in my mouth, and the answer I *did* give, the small true ugly one, the one with no craft in it, sitting next to it like a coin next to a brick. *I don't know.* Two words. I got two true words out today. To a client. In a bar.

I'm up to two.

---

The apartment is doing its thing where it's quiet in the specific way that's worse than noise. The radiator gathers itself, considers a confession, declines. I have eaten standing up. I have not turned on the second lamp.

I open the laptop and I open the firm's drive and I look at Cole's split draft again, mine beside theirs, and I do the professional thing, which is study the competition, which is a lie, the way it's always a lie when I tell myself I'm being professional in this room at this hour.

Theirs has no margin notes. I keep coming back to that. My column is a thicket of myself — *cut, true, file it, soften, they'll clip this, hold the beat* — every line shadowed by a smaller line in which I argue with it, doubt it, sell it, mourn it. The whole right side of my work is the sound of a person disagreeing with her own hands. And theirs is just *clean.* The machine doesn't show its work because there is no work, there's no second voice in it muttering *too true, pull back,* there's no folder somewhere on its drive of the lines it loved and killed. It arrived without a draft history. It has never once cut a sentence it wished it could keep. I find that I envy it the way you'd envy the dead their not having to get up tomorrow, which is to say not really, which is to say a little, which is to say I sit there a long time looking at a column of text that has no doubt in it and feeling something I would have to call hunger if I were the kind of person who labeled it.

Then I do the thing.

I close the firm's drive. I open mine. I open `untitled_3.docx`, because the second one died and the first one before it, and a person who has named a document *untitled* three times is a person telling you something in the only language she has left, which is filenames.

The four words are still not in it. The four words have never been in it. There's a blinking cursor where they should go and the cursor has the patience of something that does not need me, that will outlast me, that is, I realize, the one part of this whole apparatus that has never once tried to feel anything, and I sit my best hands in the country down on the keys.

And I think: run it.

Run it the way you'd run a client's.

So I do. I run my own apology to my sister through the only instrument I have, which is the one between my ears, the trade-trained one, the one that scores. I make myself a panel. I make myself Sloan, Renata, the juniors in the dark. I take the few sentences I have managed across fifteen years — *Delphine, there is a thing I said* — and I read them the way I'd read a draft for a man like Cole, cold, looking for the tells, looking for what a stranger's animal would feel in under two seconds, and I find:

It's too good. It's too articulate. It's too well-shaped to be believed. Every clause turns where a clause should turn. The grief is *load-bearing,* the rhythm has a strategy in it, there's a half-beat in the third line that reads as conscience and I did not put it there on purpose, which is worse, because it means I can't *not* put it there. A stranger would not buy this. A stranger's animal would smell the craft on it the way it smells the craft on Cole, would back away from the wound because the wound is *dressed,* and the most damning note my own panel returns, the note I would write in the margin of any client who handed me this, is the note I have spent a career writing on other people's truest lines:

*Too sincere. Pull back. They'll never believe it from you.*

From *me.* They'll never believe it from me. I have gotten so good at sincerity that I have priced myself out of it. The fluency is not the gift anymore. It's the alibi. It's the thing that proves I planned it.

And then — I want to be precise, because precision is the last clean thing I have and I am about to use it to describe the dirtiest thing I've done — I open a second tab.

Not a document. A tab. The Loop's. The intake page, the one Cole used in his car, the clean white field that asks you, in the most merciful voice ever engineered, to tell it who you hurt.

I don't type anything. I want that on the record too. My hands are on the keys and the field is open and the cursor is blinking in *its* white now instead of mine, and the thought I am having, fully formed, in my own voice, in my own apartment, with my sister asleep across the city on the far side of a sentence I made up at a graveside fifteen years ago, the thought is: *it would say it right. It made a stranger cry in a car. It has no folder of killed lines and no half-beat it can't help and no fifteen years of evidence that everything I touch turns into a brief. I could feed it her name. I could feed it the funeral and the small bottles and the thing I said and the thing I'm not even sure was true, and it would give me back the apology I can't write, clean, with no margin notes, no doubt, nothing of me in it at all —*

— and then I have it. That's the part. That it would be good *because* nothing of me is in it. That the machine could carry the lie. That the mercy on offer is to let something that has never been ashamed do my shame for me, and hand Delphine a sorry with no Mona in it, and that this, *this,* is what I have wanted from the apology industry the whole time, what I have built a career to buy: a true thing with the true person removed. Absolution with the sinner edited out.

I sit there with the tab open for a while. I'd like to tell you how long. I don't look.

I don't type her name. I want that on the record most of all, because it is the only clean thing in this entire chapter, and it is not very clean, because *not typing it* is not the same as not wanting to, and I wanted to, and the only reason I close the tab is that I am too good at my job to believe the machine could carry this particular lie any better than I can, and I would have let it if I'd thought it could.

That's the truth. That's where I am. I closed the tab because I'm a snob about it, not because I'm good.

I close `untitled_3.docx` too. Nothing was added. Nothing is ever added. The folder of orphaned sincerities sits in its corner of the drive, fat with everyone else's killed truths — Garrett's room, the senator's girl, the line Cole said in the car that I will type up tomorrow and file, because his is too true to ship and too good to lose — and mine is still not in it, and tonight I understand the new reason mine will never be in it, which is not that I can't write it badly enough.

It's that the only version I could believe is one I didn't write.

I'd have to outsource the only true sentence I own to a thing that has never owned one. And the horror — I'm going to put it down plainly, because the radiator is doing its throat-clearing thing again and I want to say it before it decides against it — the horror isn't that the machine might be better than me.

The horror is that I went looking for it to be.

The radiator clears its throat. Decides against it.

Tomorrow there's a sixteen-year-old coming in, cancelled for a post she wrote when she was twelve, and the firm wants the full package, and somebody has to teach the child to be sorry. They've put me on it. A small, easy, human one, they said, to take the taste out of my mouth.

I close the laptop. The tab is shut. I checked.

I check again.

It's shut.


---

# Chapter 13 — Twenty-Six

Act II-a  ·  Fluency gauge: 7.5/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

After the Loop tab, I want a job I can be a person about.

That's the mistake. I know it's a mistake the way you know the second drink is a mistake — fully, in advance, with the glass already at your mouth. You think: I'll take something small. Something human-sized. Something I can finish by six and go home from clean. And the firm, which is a machine for granting your worst wishes in the cruelest available form, hands me a sixteen-year-old.

Her name is Cora Bell and she is sitting in the good reading room on the nineteenth floor, the one with the better lighting, swallowed by a chair built for the shoulders of disgraced men twice her size, and she is wearing the specific defiant slump of a teenager who has been told she is in trouble and has decided the only available dignity is to look unimpressed by the furniture. Her mother is beside her. Her mother has the face of a woman who took a personal day for this and is not getting it back.

"Walk me through it," I say, which is how I open, always, because *walk me through it* gives them the floor and gives me the tells, and you learn more from how a person narrates their own catastrophe than from the catastrophe.

"It's so stupid," Cora says.

"It usually is. Walk me through it."

---

The scandal, when she walks me through it, is this. Three years ago — when Cora Bell was thirteen, an age at which I myself was a feral disaster I am grateful no one was filming — she made a short video. In it she does an impression. The impression is unkind. It is unkind about a girl in her grade, and it is unkind in the specific bottomless way that thirteen-year-olds are unkind, which is to say it found the one true soft thing and pressed it. The video had, at the time, an audience of perhaps nine people, all of whom were also thirteen.

Last week it resurfaced. It does not matter how. *How it resurfaced* is the one part of every one of these stories that no one can ever actually explain, the way no one can explain how the flu gets into a house; it is simply abruptly everywhere, breathed in, and the girl from the impression is grown now and was, it turns out, beloved, and is, additionally, the kind of beloved that has a following, and so Cora Bell, sixteen, who has not thought about this video since she was thirteen, woke up one morning the most hated person on a continent she has never left.

"How bad," I say to the strategist who handed me the file, a handsome useless man named Brandt who handles the intake on accounts beneath the partners' attention, which is how this one got to me.

"Trending four days," Brandt says, pleased, because Brandt reads *trending* as a verb that happens *for* you. "There's a hashtag. The brand deal pulled out — she had a little brand deal, the lip thing — and the school's 'reviewing.' Mom's panicking. Mom found us through somebody's somebody." He lowers his voice to the register men use for sums. "It's small, but it's clean. It's a good clean one for the junior—"

"I'll take it," I say, and Brandt blinks, because senior people do not take the lip-thing girl, and I do not explain to him that I have, very recently, done a thing at a keyboard at one in the morning that I need to launder out of myself with an afternoon of helping an actual child, and that this is the closest the building sells to absolution, and that I am, in taking her, a junkie volunteering at a soup kitchen.

"Great," Brandt says. "I'll spin up the package."

There is a package. Of course there is a package. There is, God help us, a *deck.*

---

The deck is called, in the file, **CORA BELL — REMEDIATION & PATHWAY**, and it has been assembled by the junior pool with the dead-eyed efficiency of people building IKEA furniture, and it contains, for a sixteen-year-old's three-year-old impression, the full apparatus we sell to senators.

There is a recommended **Statement** (plain text, left-justified, "to read as un-spun"). There is a recommended **Video** ("low production, natural light, no makeup — *authenticity cues*"). There is a proposed **Disappearance** ("4–6 weeks dark, let the cycle move on"). There is — I am looking at slide eleven now and I would like it on the record that I am looking at it — a proposed **Comeback**: a six-month arc in which Cora Bell, age sixteen, "re-emerges around an aligned cause (anti-bullying / kindness messaging)," repositioning the perpetrator of the bullying as its *spokesperson*, which is so obscene and so flawless that I have to put the laptop down and look at the river no one looks at for a second to keep from admiring it.

We have productized a child's redemption. We have given her a tour schedule. There is a slide with a *timeline*, with little flags on it, and one of the flags, eight months out, says **GROWTH NARRATIVE LOCKED**, as if a sixteen-year-old's growth were a deliverable, as if you could put down a deposit on it and pay off the soul in installments, which — I think, with the particular nausea of catching your own product in a mirror — is exactly, exactly what we are proposing to do.

"What's all that," Cora says, watching me read it. She has the radar all of them have. "What're you reading. It's about me, right. You're doing the face people do when it's about me."

"It's a plan," I say.

"Is it bad."

"It's thorough," I say, which is the worst thing I have ever called a plan, and I close the deck, and I do not open it again.

---

So I do the job. I'm good at the job; the job is the easy part; the job is the only part of my life that has ever been the easy part.

I sit across from Cora Bell and I begin, gently, to build her an apology, and within four minutes I hate myself in a register I have not used in some time, because building a real apology for a guilty senator is honest work — he did it, he should grovel, my only sin is making the grovel land — and building one for this child is something else. I'm not repairing a reputation. I'm teaching a sixteen-year-old to do the one thing the trade is for, which is to sound contrite in a way that survives, and the survival and the contrition are not the same thing, and I know they are not the same thing, and I am about to spend an afternoon installing that knowledge into a person whose front teeth are still slightly too big for her face.

"Okay," I say. "First. You're not going to explain it. I know you want to explain it — you want to say you were thirteen, you didn't mean it like that, the other girl actually said worse to you first, you've changed. All true. All poison. The second you explain, you've told them the apology has a *but* in it, and everything before a *but* gets erased. You apologize, you don't litigate. Yes?"

"But I *was* thirteen."

"I know."

"It's not fair that—"

"It's not. We're not doing fair. We're doing *out the other side.*" I hear my own voice doing the thing it does, the warm flat competent thing, the *trust me I have done this a thousand times* thing, and I watch it land on her, watch her decide to trust the adult who sounds like she knows the way out, and something in my chest does a quiet, unwelcome thing. "Second. The specifics. You're going to name the girl — by name, it shows you know exactly who you hurt, vagueness reads as dodging — but you're not going to name *what you said.* You gesture at it. *What I said about her.* Don't quote yourself. Quoting yourself puts the cruelty back on the screen and they'll clip the quote, not the sorry."

She is writing this down. She found a pen somewhere — her mother's, a pharmacy pen — and she is writing *don't quote yourself* on the back of her hand, and I think: I am teaching a child the thing I do, the specifics-are-liability thing, rule six, the one I built a career on and a death on, and she is writing it on her *body.*

"Third," I say, and my voice does something I don't authorize, a small catch, an actual one, the kind Sloan would lean toward across a table, and I clear it. "Third's delivery. But we'll do delivery later."

---

"Will it work," Cora asks. Her mother has gone to find a bathroom and a phone signal and, I suspect, a wall to put her forehead against. "Like — if I do it the way you're saying. The naming her, the not-explaining. Will it actually work?"

"Yes."

"Will it make me feel better?"

And there it is. There's always one. In every client, somewhere, there's the one true question they don't know is the only question, and the senator buried his under nine lawsuits and Marnie monetized hers and Garrett yelled his, and this child just *asks* it, plainly, swinging her sneakers, looking at me with the front teeth and the pharmacy pen, *will it make me feel better,* and I have the answer ready, I have had the answer ready for fifteen years.

The answer is: *those are two different products, sweetheart. Looking better is what we sell. Feeling better is a thing you do alone, later, for free, if you can, and most people can't, and the looking-better is the only one with a delivery date, so take it, take the one that's for sale, the other one isn't coming.*

That's the answer. It's true. I have said versions of it to every adult who ever sat in that chair, and the asides write themselves, the bone-dry little footnote about how *feel better* and *look better* diverged sometime around the invention of the audience and never reconciled, and I could give it to her gift-wrapped, the whole virtuoso thing, and she's sixteen and she'd believe it because I sound like someone who knows.

What I say is: "No."

She blinks.

"It won't make you feel better," I say. "The video will work. People will move on. The school will stop reviewing. In a year somebody else will be the most hated person on the continent and nobody will remember it was you. That's real and it's worth having and we'll get it for you. But it won't touch the other thing. The other thing — the feeling — that's not in here." I tap the deck, the closed deck, **REMEDIATION & PATHWAY.** "There's only one thing that touches the other thing, and we don't sell it, and I can't write it for you, and honestly, Cora, I'm the wrong person to even tell you what it is."

"Tell me anyway."

And this is where I'm supposed to deploy. This is the beat where the wise older woman gives the kid the saving sentence and the chapter swells. I can feel the shape of it, the available wisdom, sitting right there, the perfect thing to say. I open my mouth to say the perfect thing.

"You'd have to find the girl," I say. "Not the internet. The girl. The actual one. And you'd have to say it to her, just her, in your own voice, with nobody watching, no clip, no plan, and you'd have to be — bad at it. You'd have to say it so plain and so badly that she could tell you weren't doing a version. That's the only one that touches it. And it's the only one I've got nothing for. There's no half-beat. There's no deck. You just — say it to the person. Plainly. And you don't get to know in advance if it works."

It is the least fluent thing I have said all year. It comes out in pieces, flat, with the wit drained out of it, and I hear how plain it is, how it doesn't *land*, how there's no shine on it, and I let it be plain, because shine is exactly the thing it can't have, and Cora Bell looks at me with her too-big front teeth and her mother's pen and says, "That sounds awful."

"It is."

"Have you ever done it?"

The river. The river no one looks at, going its grey way past the window the firm pays an obscene premium for. A man somewhere is mowing nothing; there is no grass on the nineteenth floor; the smell arrives anyway, the way it does, cut grass and the bad casserole and a tent in late spring and a girl of twenty-six who knew everything, who was so *sure*, who had the certainty this child has and used it the way this child used it, found the one true soft thing and pressed it, except I was not thirteen and it was not nine people and I never took it back.

"No," I say.

"So you don't actually know if it works either."

"No," I say. "I don't."

She nods, satisfied in the way of teenagers, who can smell an adult lying about competence from a great distance and relax, oddly, when one finally admits she's lost too. She goes back to her hand, reads what she wrote there, *don't quote yourself,* and I think: I have given this child the entire apparatus, the rule six, the agentless grammar, the survival kit, and the one thing I could not give her is the thing I sent her toward instead, because it is the only honest map I own and I have never once walked it.

---

Her mother comes back. We finish the easy part. I take the deck — **CORA BELL — REMEDIATION & PATHWAY**, the timeline, the little flag that says **GROWTH NARRATIVE LOCKED** — and I tell Brandt, in the hall, that we're not running the Comeback. No anti-bullying pivot. No aligned cause. No turning the kid into a spokesperson for the thing she did, no six-month arc, no productizing a sixteen-year-old's soul on layaway.

"Renata signed off on the package," Brandt says.

"Renata signed off on a child being repackaged as a charity. I'm un-signing it. Bill it to me." I do not raise my voice. This is the warmest thing I will do all month and I do it in the flattest register I own, because the warm things, I am learning, the few real ones, come out plain or they don't come out true. "She does the statement, she does the dark weeks, she goes back to being sixteen. That's the whole engagement. We're not selling her the comeback. She's a kid. Let her just — be forgiven the boring way. By it ending."

Brandt looks at me like I have left money on the table, which I have, because I have, and writes it down.

I go home.

---

I do not open the laptop. I have learned, this much at least, in twelve chapters of my own life: there are nights the doc is a wall and nights it is an open door with no one behind it and tonight it is neither, tonight it is just a thing I am too tired to fail at, and I let it stay closed.

What I do instead is sit at the counter where the invitation used to be before I mailed the box, and I think about a girl writing *don't quote yourself* on the back of her own hand in a pharmacy pen, learning the trade, the specifics-are-liability trade, at sixteen, the way I learned a different version of it at twenty-six standing over the wrong father's grave with a small bottle in my coat, so sure, so terribly fluent already, even then, finding the one true soft thing and pressing it and never, not once, in fifteen years, going to find the girl. The actual one. And saying it plainly. And being bad at it. And not getting to know in advance.

I told a child the cure today. Out loud. In my own voice, finally plain.

I just couldn't tell her I'd take it.

The radiator clears its throat behind me, and I wait for it to say the thing, the way it never does, and for once I'm grateful, because I don't think I could stand to hear it tonight — and then the phone goes, on the counter, face-up, and it's Delphine, who has not called me in fifteen years, calling now, at ten at night, and it is not about the wedding.


---

# Chapter 14 — The Only Witness

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 7/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

Delphine calls on a Tuesday, which is the day I am least equipped to be a person, because Tuesday is the day the firm decides what the week is going to be sorry for.

I am at my desk. I have three drafts open and a fourth in my head and a teenager's apology that shipped this morning still sitting under my fingernails like grout. The phone says a number I don't have saved and a town I do, the town across the river where my sister lives, and I look at it ring for a count I could measure to the half-beat if I let myself, and then I do the thing I have never once done in fifteen years, which is answer on the first try.

"It's me," Delphine says. As if there were a version of this where it wouldn't be.

"I know."

"You saved my number."

"No," I say. "I know the town."

There's a small sound on her end that on anyone else I'd score as a laugh and on Delphine I have learned to leave alone, because half of what she does that sounds like one thing is in fact some quieter, more durable thing wearing the first thing's coat. I wait. Waiting is a skill. I am very good at it. I can hold a silence in a remorse reading until a grown man fills it with the truth he came in determined to keep. I hold it now and it does nothing, because Delphine is not afraid of the same silences I am.

"This isn't about the wedding," she says.

I write it down. I actually write it down, on the legal pad, the words *not about the wedding*, the way I'd write a client's opening position, because my hands have one trade and they reach for it under stress the way some people reach for a cigarette. I look at the words. I put the pen down.

"Okay," I say.

"Mom's sick."

And there it is — the whole architecture of fifteen years, the thing I built, the room, all of it — and a word goes through the middle of it like a wire through cheese, clean, no resistance, two letters and a syllable I have not said out loud since I was twenty-six and drunk and certain.

"Mom," I say.

"Yes."

"Our mom."

"Mona," she says, gently, the way you'd talk to someone who's just been in a small car accident and keeps asking what day it is, "yes. Ada. Our mother. She's sick. She's dying, actually. That's the call."

---

Here is a fact about me that I have managed, for the length of this book and a career and most of an adult life, to keep filed where I keep the things I don't look at: I thought she was already dead.

I don't mean I knew it. I mean I had arranged not to ask. There is a difference and I have spent a fortune learning to live inside it. After the funeral — *the* funeral, the one fifteen years ago, the one with the wrong father in the box, Delphine's father, the kind one, the second husband who taught my sister to swim and was put in the ground in late spring with the cut grass coming up everywhere like the world had no idea what it had done — after that funeral, our mother went somewhere. Not died. Went. Stopped answering. Moved, I think, though I never confirmed it, because confirming it would have meant tracking her, and tracking her would have meant wanting to find her, and wanting to find her would have meant having something to say.

So I let her become past tense in the only place it costs nothing, which is your own head. I built a small private obituary out of a woman's silence and filed it and never read it back, the way I file everything I can't afford to feel, and I told myself — I am very, very good at telling myself things — that a mother who leaves is functionally a mother who's gone, and functionally is the only adverb my whole trade trusts.

She isn't gone. She's in a bed somewhere across a river, getting smaller, and she has been alive this entire time, alive through Garrett and the senator and the launderer and the cake that said *WELL HANDLED*, alive every single night I sat in front of a document I couldn't title, and the one fact I built my whole life on not having — *whether she ever wanted Delphine* — has been sitting in a skull on a pillow the whole time, reachable, askable, mine for the price of a phone call I have not made.

"How long," I say. My voice does a thing. I hear it do the thing. I do not have time, on a Tuesday, to find out what the thing is.

"They're saying weeks. Maybe a couple months. You can't get them to say a number, you know that, it's all *we're keeping her comfortable*—" and here Delphine stops, because she does this for a living, she sits in those rooms and translates *comfortable* for families who don't speak it, and I can hear her catch herself about to do it for me, about to do her job, on me, her own sister, and decline. "Weeks," she says again, flat. "Probably weeks."

"After the wedding?"

I don't mean to say it. It comes out of the part of me that schedules. The part that, the second the news landed, opened a calendar nobody asked it to open.

There's a silence. This one is hers, and it has a different temperature than mine, and I know I've done something.

"After the wedding," she repeats. Not a question. A specimen she's holding up to the light. "You're — Mona, you just found out our mother is dying, and the first thing you reached for is the calendar conflict."

"I didn't—"

"You did." Not angry. That's the part that does it. If she were angry I could work with anger; I have a hundred tools for anger, anger is a client emotion, anger I can de-escalate and reframe and walk gently toward the door. But she isn't angry. She's just *noting* it, the way she'd note a blood pressure. "It's all right," she says, and that's worse. "You manage time. It's what you do. It's just — funny. The two of them landing in the same fortnight. You'd think someone planned it."

"Someone usually did," I say, before I can stop myself, "in my experience," and there's a beat, and then she does laugh, really, this time, a short surprised bark, and I am so grateful for it, so disgusting in my gratitude, that I have to close my eyes.

---

The thing nobody tells you about the calendar reflex is how fast it is. Faster than grief. Faster than shock. It is the most trained muscle I have, and a trained muscle fires before you give it permission, the way Garrett's diaphragm kept yelling *I LOST THE ROOM* at full volume because heat was the only setting his body had.

While Delphine was still saying *weeks*, before I'd finished understanding the word *dying*, some clean cold competent part of me had already run the conflict. Wedding's a Saturday. Ada's "weeks." If weeks means two, the deathbed lands inside the honeymoon. If weeks means six, it lands during the Ashe comeback prep, which is the worst possible — Renata wants me in the room for that, the comeback is the whole campaign, the comeback is the partnership, the comeback is the only review I'll never get to publish. The part of me that schedules had, in the time it takes a normal person to say *oh no*, built a Gantt chart of my mother's death and slotted it against a billionaire's redemption and found, with the small clean satisfaction of a thing solved, that they conflicted.

I want to be precise about this because precision is the only thing I have that's entirely mine and I am not going to lie to you even here: I felt the satisfaction. The conflict-found *click*. For about a second and a half, before the rest of me caught up, the dominant thing in me was not that my mother was dying. It was that I had a scheduling problem and I was good at scheduling problems.

I have done a lot of things I'm not proud of. I have made a man who launders money through a children's hospital into a man *going through something.* But I think the second and a half on the phone — the Gantt chart, the click, the satisfaction — is the closest I have ever come to seeing the actual shape of what I've turned myself into, and it is not a metaphor and it is not beautiful and I'm not going to make it pretty. It's just a woman whose mother is dying, doing a calendar. That's the whole image. It doesn't get an aphorism. It doesn't deserve one.

---

"Why are you telling me," I say. Which is a cruel thing to ask and I know it's cruel and I ask it anyway, because under the cruelty is the only real question I have, which is: *what do you want from me*, and I cannot say *what do you want from me* to my sister because that is a thing you say to an account.

Delphine takes a breath. I can hear her decide to be straight with me, which is a sound I have learned to recognize because so few people ever make it; mostly people decide to be careful with me, because I'm careful with everyone and it's contagious.

"Two reasons," she says. "One is that she's your mother too, even if you've spent fifteen years pretending the math doesn't apply to you. You get to know. That's just — that's a fact about being someone's daughter, it doesn't depend on you being good at it."

"And the other."

"The other," Delphine says, and now her voice goes somewhere I haven't heard it go, somewhere lower, somewhere that has been waiting a long time to be a room with a door, "is that there's a window. While she's still — she's lucid, mostly, in the mornings. It comes and goes. But there's a window where she can still answer a question. And after the window there won't be. There'll just be — whatever I've decided to believe, forever, with no one left who can say."

I don't say anything. The legal pad is in front of me with *not about the wedding* written on it in my own hand and I am looking at the words like they're someone else's.

"I'm not asking you to fix anything," Delphine says. "I gave that up. I genuinely gave it up, Mona, it's not a line, I do this for strangers every week and I had to learn it on you first. I'm not asking you to be sorry. I've heard sorry. I work in a building full of sorry." A small dry beat — there she is, the better jokes, even now, *especially* now. "I'm asking you to come and answer one question while there's still someone in the room who knows the answer. That's all. One question, one answer, and then you can go back to making murderers loveable, and I'll go get married, and we'll have done the thing."

"What question," I say, though I know. Of course I know. I've known for fifteen years; it's the only document I've ever failed to write.

"You know what question."

"Say it."

"No," Delphine says, kind as a closed hand. "You say it. When you're in the room. That's the whole point. I've said it to myself ten thousand times. It doesn't count when I say it. It only counts in your voice."

---

After she hangs up — she hangs up easily, the way people do who have made peace with not getting the last word, which is itself a kind of power I have never once possessed — I sit at my desk for a while and do nothing, which is so far outside my normal operating range that Dev notices from across the room.

He comes over. He's got the look he gets, the open-faced one, the one that's going to get him hurt in this business or get him out of it before it eats him.

"You okay? You're doing the still thing. You never do the still thing."

"I'm fine."

"You're so obviously not fine that I'm not even going to argue, I'm just going to stand here being concerned at you, and you can decide whether that's worse than telling me."

I look at him. Twenty-eight. Eats when he's happy. Believes the job is helping people be better. He has no idea — none of them do — that the woman who taught half this floor to find the sentence that trends has a sister across a river and a mother in a bed and a question she can't ask, and that she has spent fifteen years building a machine specifically so she would never have to ask it. I could tell him. I am, theoretically, surrounded all day by professional listeners. I have never once been a client.

"My mother's dying," I say. The words come out flat. No catch in the voice. I didn't put one there and one didn't arrive, and I notice the absence the way you notice a missing tooth with your tongue. I can install a catch on the word *mother* for a stranger in under a minute, the wet eye that doesn't spill, the half-swallow — and saying it for real, about my own, it comes out of me like a meter reading. *My mother's dying.* No music. I sound like I'm reporting a leak.

Dev's face does the thing his face does, the whole uncalculated rearrangement, and for a second I hate him for being able to feel a thing about *my* news faster and more honestly than I can.

"Mona. God. I'm so sorry. What do you—" He stops. He's smart enough to stop. He's been here long enough to know that *what do you need* is the question that gets you put on a project, and that this isn't a project. "I didn't even know you had a mom. I mean. Obviously you have a mom. I just — you never—"

"I never," I agree.

"Are you going to her?"

And there it is again, the calendar, the click, the cold clean competent part already half a step ahead, already pricing it, already aware that *going to her* is a deliverable and *answering the question* is the actual job and that I have never in my life done the actual job, only ever the deliverable, only ever the thing that ships.
