"I don't know yet," I say, which is true, and then, because Dev is standing there being twenty-eight at me with his whole heart out where anyone could take it, I say the truer thing, the one I'd never let a client say: "There's a thing she needs me to say to her. To both of them. A specific thing. And I've had fifteen years to figure out how to say it and I don't have it. I write the impossible ones for a living and I cannot write my own."

Dev is quiet for a second. Then he says — and he doesn't know what he's doing, that's the only reason it's allowed to land, he thinks he's being supportive, he has no idea he's handing me the exact knife — "You'll find it. You always find the sentence. It's the thing you do."

I don't tell him that's the problem. That finding the sentence is precisely the disease. That the one I owe can't be *found*, because anything I find I'll be able to hear myself finding, and a sentence you can hear yourself finding is no sentence at all. I don't tell him because he's twenty-eight and still thinks the gift is a gift, and there should be one person in this building who gets to keep believing that a little longer.

"Sure," I say. "I'll find it."

---

I go home. The radiator clears its throat about something and, for once, like it can read a room, says nothing.

I should not do what I do next. I know I should not. I am going to tell you anyway, because you can't fire me, and because I think the only honest thing I can offer you, ever, in any of this, is to not pretend I didn't.

I open the laptop. I do not open the doc — `untitled.docx`, still untitled, still untitleable. I open something worse. I open a new document, a blank one, and across the top I write, the way I would for any account, the way I have written across the top of a thousand of them:

`OKORO-MERCER, D. — DISCOVERY`

And under it I start to do the thing I do.

*Audience profile,* I write. *What does she actually want vs. what she's saying she wants.* I know this move; I built this move; it's the first move on every job — never trust the stated ask, find the ask under the ask. Marnie said she wanted to seem humble and what she wanted was to keep the supplement line. The senator said he wanted to apologize and what he wanted was for nine plaintiffs to hear an admission and zero to be able to use it. The ask under the ask is where the money is. So: Delphine says she wants the truth said plainly, in my own voice, no craft. But what does she *actually*—

I stop.

I look at what I've typed. `OKORO-MERCER, D. — DISCOVERY.` My sister's name in the slot where the clients go. My sister, who just told me our mother is dying and that there's a window, and that the only thing she's ever wanted from me is a true thing said badly enough to count — and my response, my reflex, the most trained muscle I have firing before I gave it permission, is to *open a file on her.* To run discovery. To find the ask under the ask, as if the ask under the ask were not, in this one case, exactly and only the ask on top: *say the true thing. Out loud. Soon. Before there's no one left who knows the answer.*

There is no ask under it. That's the whole horror. For the first time in my career the stated ask and the real ask are the same ask, the surface is the depth, and I am sitting here trying to drill through a floor that is also the ceiling, because drilling is the only thing my hands know how to do.

I should delete it.

I look at the blinking cursor under *what does she actually want.* The window's open, Delphine said. Mornings, mostly. It comes and goes. And after the window there won't be one — there'll just be whatever I've decided to believe, forever, with no one left who can say. She was talking about herself when she said that. But she was also, without knowing it, describing me. After Ada, there's no witness. After Ada, the question of whether I made it up — whether our mother ever wanted Delphine, or whether twenty-six-year-old Mona just *needed* her not to, at a graveside, drunk on small bottles, jealous of a dead man's kindness — that question stops being answerable and becomes, permanently, a thing I get to decide to believe. Forever. In my own voice. With no one left to check me.

I have until the window closes to find out whether I am a liar.

And the cold clean competent part of me, the part that already built the Gantt chart, the part that opened the file, looks at that — looks at a dying woman as the last verification source on the worst sentence I ever wrote — and does not feel what a person should feel. It feels the thing it always feels when a job has a deadline and a single point of failure.

It feels *ready.*

I do not delete the file.

I tell myself I'll delete it tomorrow. I am very, very good at telling myself things. I close the laptop on it, open, alive, my sister's name in the client slot, and across the city Delphine is asleep, and across a river our mother is awake in the dark getting smaller, holding the one answer in the world I need, and I lie down in my rent-stabilized one-bed and I do not plan the visit and I do not write the sentence.

I plan the discovery.

God help me. I plan the discovery.


---

# Chapter 15 — A/B Testing Love

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 7/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

I open the discovery file at 9:14 on a Wednesday night, the file I told myself I'd delete, the one with my sister's name in the slot where the clients go, and I do not delete it. I do the opposite of deleting it. I give it a structure.

Because here is the thing about panic, which nobody puts in the brochure: it doesn't feel like panic when you're competent. It feels like getting to work. The Ada clock is running — weeks, Delphine said, mornings mostly, the window closing — and a normal person, faced with a dying mother and a sister owed the truth, would feel the bottom drop out of the world. I do not feel the bottom drop out of the world. I feel the specific clean readiness of a woman who has a deliverable and a deadline and a skill, and the skill stands up inside me like a dog that's heard the leash, and it does not occur to the skill that it is the wrong animal for this. It never has. That's what makes it a skill and not a conscience.

So. `OKORO-MERCER, D. — DISCOVERY.` And under it, the headers, the same headers I have typed across the top of a thousand accounts, the senator and the chef and the launderer, my fingers laying them down with the muscle memory of a woman who could do this in a coma and on Thursdays more or less does:

*Audience profile. The ask under the ask. Tells to hit. Tells to avoid. Delivery environment. Desired outcome.*

I look at *desired outcome* for a second. Then I fill it in.

---

The audience profile comes easy, which is the first thing that should scare me and doesn't. I know Delphine. I have, it turns out, been doing passive social listening on my own sister for fifteen years without admitting it was a feed — the town across the river, the work she does, the way she hangs up easily, the better jokes. So I build her the way I'd build a target demographic, and she builds well. *Subject: 39F. Values: directness, presence, the unfixable faced without flinching. Distrusts: performance (HIGH — occupational; she does the real version of the thing I fake, she'll smell craft at forty yards). Sentiment toward source: complicated; not hostile; has, against the odds, no apparent appetite for revenge, which removes my single most reliable lever.*

I sit with that one. In the trade, you build everything off the wronged party's appetite — for blood, for groveling, for the thing they didn't get the first time. You feed the appetite a managed version and they go home full. Delphine has no appetite. She wants a fact, which is not an appetite, it's a question, and you cannot feed a question. This is a problem. I make a note. The note says: *no leverage. she wants information, not satisfaction.* I have written, in fifteen years, that sentence about exactly zero clients, because no client on earth has ever wanted information instead of satisfaction. The whole industry runs on the difference.

The ask under the ask comes next, and here the machine stalls, the way it stalled in CH 14, the way it stalls every time I run it on her, because there is no ask under the ask. I keep drilling for the seam and hitting the same flat rock: she wants the true thing said plainly. That's the ask on top. There's nothing beneath it. For Marnie the ask on top was *help me seem humble* and the ask underneath was *let me keep the supplement line.* For the senator the ask on top was *let me apologize* and underneath was *let nine plaintiffs hear it and zero use it.* For Delphine the ask on top is *tell me if it was true* and underneath is — *tell me if it was true.* It is the only account I have ever opened where the surface and the depth are the same depth, and my whole competence is the ability to swim between them, and there's no between. There's just the one true cold thing all the way down.

I write, in the field marked *the ask under the ask:* `SAME AS ABOVE.`

It is the most honest sentence in the document and I want to be sick.

---

But I don't stop. This is the part I have to tell you straight, with no music on it, because if I dress it up I'm doing the disease while describing the disease, and one of us in this room should stay sober. I don't stop. I move to *delivery environment*, and I start to plan where to say it.

This is a real thing we do. Environment is everything. You don't run a sit-down in a boardroom, you run it in a den with a dog in it; you don't apologize in a suit, you apologize in the soft thing the audience's father wore; you control the light, the chair, the distance, the single prop on the table that says *human* in a language below language. I have placed a thousand people in a thousand rooms calibrated to make a stranger's animal brain decide, in under two seconds, *that one has a soul.* So I do it for Delphine. I actually do it. I open a fresh field and I start to *stage the recantation.*

*Not the hospice* — too loaded, too much of her competence in the air, she's the home-field expert there and I'd be apologizing on her instrument. *Not a restaurant* — public, performed, the catch in the voice carries to the next table. *Her kitchen, maybe. Theo out. Morning, not evening — evening reads as confession, morning reads as decision. Coffee, not wine; wine reads as needing courage and needing courage reads as performance. Sit, don't stand. Hands visible — hands that go still where a liar's would gesture—*

And I get that far, hands that go still, the exact phrase from the Garrett notes, the exact tell I coached into a chef so a bus-stop stranger would forgive him, I get that far before I hear what I'm doing, and even hearing it I keep typing, because the machine is faster than the shame, the machine is always faster, *that's the whole tragedy of being good at something,* and I write the next field, and the next field is *tells to hit.*

---

You want to know what I am. Here is what I am. I made a list of the involuntary signatures of remorse I would need to display to my own sister in order to be believed by my own sister, and I scored them, and I ranked them, and I am not going to soften a single line of it for you, because the only thing I have left to give anyone is the receipt.

And because there is a part of my brain that cannot do a thing without instrumenting the thing, I build the rubric. I actually build the rubric. The same five-column grid I have walked nine associates through in the windowless room on the nineteenth floor, the grid we call, in-house, with the straight face we keep for billing, the *Contrition Scorecard* — *Signal / Believability (1–5) / Detection Risk / Audience Cost / Net.* Across the top, where a normal performance review would say *Exceeds, Meets, Developing,* I have, for actual money, written tiers. *Tier 1: reads as a man undone. Tier 2: reads as a man trying. Tier 3: reads as a man with a publicist.* There is a tab in the firm's shared drive — I built it — that converts a defendant's wet eye into a projected sentiment lift, and the column header on that tab, the literal cell, the one a junior copy-pasted onto a slide for a money launderer last spring, says *Forgiveness KPI.* I have run a launderer's tears through a KPI. I priced a senator's flinch to two decimal places. And now, at 9:31 on a Wednesday, I open the same grid, and I title it `D.`, and I begin to score the involuntary tells of loving my sister against a metric I invented to sell strangers a feeling — *target: believed by morning; owner: me; status: blocked, pending content* — and for one warm efficient second the grid feels good in my hands, feels like competence, feels like a Tuesday, before I read the cell that says *Forgiveness KPI* with my sister's initial at the top of the column, and the good feeling curdles where I sit.

*The wet eye that doesn't spill* — Believability 4, but Detection Risk maxed — risk: she's seen me cry on cue, she's literally told me she could feel where my voice was going before it went there, she'll read the eye as a prop. *Downgrade.*

*The half-swallow before the hard word* — the hard word being *true*, or *Mom*, or — *risk: she knows the half-swallow, it's in everything I ship, she'll hear the half-beat I'm holding.* The half-beat that reads as conscience. I have sold the half-beat that reads as conscience eleven million times. I can't use it on the one person who knows it's for sale.

*The break in the voice on the worst word* — and here I stop, because the worst word, the one the break would land on, I haven't written yet. I've been staging the room for the sentence without writing the sentence. I've furnished the chair, dressed the light, scored the face, and the four words that go in the chair are still the blank they have been for fifteen years.

I have built, I notice with the small clean satisfaction of a craftsman finding the grain — and then with the slower, worse thing that comes a half-second behind the satisfaction now, the thing that's been coming a half-second behind it since the phone rang on a Tuesday — I have built an entire production around a hole. A flawless delivery vehicle with nothing to deliver. The most elaborate envelope in the history of the trade, addressed, stamped, scored, staged, and empty.

This is, I think, what I actually do for a living. I just never had a client whose absence I knew well enough to see it.

---

I should close the laptop here. A better woman closes the laptop here. I am not a better woman; I am a more thorough one; so I open the field I have been not-opening, the one I built the whole machine to feed, and I type the emotional core, because every apology has an emotional core, the one beat the entire document exists to detonate, and I know what the core is, I've known for fifteen years, it's the thing under the recantation, the thing the fact is wearing the coat of, and I type it.

*I love you.*

And then — because I am out of time, because Ada is getting smaller across a river, because the only tool I trust is the one that's killing me and I do not know how to do anything else, because the machine has heard the leash and there is no other dog — I type it again, a second version, the way I'd draft a second version of a tagline, and I put them side by side.

VERSION A: *I love you. I always loved you. That's why what I did is unforgivable.*

VERSION B: *I love you. I think that's why I did it. I couldn't stand that she might have loved you more.*

And I look at them. And the part of me that is entirely competence, the part with no off switch, the part that priced a child's apology and built a Gantt chart of my mother's death, looks at the two versions of *I love you* sitting in their little cells like two candidates for the same job — and it begins, smoothly, helpfully, with the warm efficiency it brings to everything, to *test* them.

*A is cleaner. A centers the love, makes the cruelty an aberration, very forgivable, the audience exhales. B is riskier — B admits the love was tangled up in the cruelty, B says I hurt you out of the same place I loved you, which is true, which is far too true, which is a knife too sharp for — B will read as either devastatingly honest or as a man building a room, depending on the half-beat. Run B with the break on "loved" and it lands as confession. Run B with the break on "couldn't stand" and it lands as self-pity. The difference is nine percent of—*

I stop.

---

I don't stop because I'm good. I want that on the record. I stop the way you stop when you catch your own reflection in a dark window and for a second don't recognize the person, the way you'd flinch from a stranger before you understood it was a stranger wearing your coat. I stop because I have just read, on my own screen, the sentence *run B with the break on "loved" and it lands as confession,* and the it in that sentence is *I love you,* and the word I have used about *I love you* is *lands,* and I am A/B testing which version of loving my sister is statistically likelier to be believed by my sister.

I am running a remorse reading. On love. Mine. With a panel of one, who is also me, who is also the only person in the building qualified to score it, scoring it for the appearance of the thing instead of the thing, because I no longer have access to the thing except as an appearance, because somewhere in fifteen years of furnishing empty chairs I sat down in one and pulled the door shut and now I'm the only one allowed inside, *I built a room where I could be the only one allowed to feel anything,* a line I wrote for a chef, the truest thing I ever wrote, and it was never about a chef, and it was never even about Delphine, it was a report, it was a status update from inside the room, and the room is this, the room is a laptop at 9:14 on a Wednesday, A/B testing love.

I get up. I go to the bathroom. I do this because I have to look at something other than the screen and the bathroom is the nearest room with a different surface in it, and the different surface is the mirror, and I make the mistake, the last clean mistake of the night, of looking at my own face in it.

And I do the thing. God help me, before I can stop it, before the better woman I am not can intervene, I look at my own face in the mirror — wet around the eyes, finally, actually, the real involuntary thing arriving uninvited for once in its life — and the machine, the dog, the part with no off switch, looks at my own wet eyes and reaches, automatically, helplessly, for *Sloan.* For the consult. For the frame-by-frame. *Is that the Duchenne marker. Is the orbicularis oculi engaging. Is that the real one, the one he says he's never been able to coach, or did I produce it, did I just produce it standing here, can I even tell anymore, is there a tell I have on my own face that I can read, and if I can read it can I fake it, and if I can fake it was it ever—*

I turn the light off. I stand in my own dark bathroom with my hand still on the switch. It is the only thing I can think of to do that the machine can't score, and even that — even standing in the dark not-scoring my own grief — even *that*, some part of me notes, would test well. *Restraint reads as depth.* I cannot find the bottom of it. There is no field I can stand in where it isn't already standing there ahead of me, in the dark, with a clipboard, taking notes on how authentically I'm failing to perform.

---

So here is the midpoint of my life, in case you wanted to know where it was. It was not a wedding or a deathbed or a verdict. It was a woman in a dark bathroom understanding, all the way down, with no part of her left to argue, the thing she has spent a career and a fortune not understanding, which is so simple a child has it and I have spent forty-one years not having it:

The craft is not a tool I have. It's a thing I am. The reaching is the me. I went looking, tonight, for the version of myself that could love my sister without scoring it, and I went looking with the only instrument I own, which is the scoring, and the instrument cannot find a self it isn't, the way a thermometer cannot find a temperature it doesn't have. So the Loop never had it backwards and neither, until tonight, did I. It doesn't prove machines can fake souls. It proves there was never anything in me the machine was missing. I am not afraid the box is as good as me. I am afraid the box is *me*, with the overhead trimmed.

I think of Ashe, who edits nothing, agrees to everything, a man so thoroughly authored there's no one left underneath to be sorry. I have been telling myself he's my client. He's my prognosis.

---

I go back to the laptop. The two versions of *I love you* are still up, A and B, in their cells, waiting to be scored. The little cursor is blinking between them, patient as a heart, the way it blinks in `untitled.docx`, the way it has blinked at me for fifteen years, the only thing in my life that has never once been impressed by me.

I select all of it. The audience profile. The ask under the ask that was the same as above. The delivery environment, the morning coffee, the staged hands. The tells to hit, the tells to avoid, the forgiveness with no appetite to feed. Version A and Version B of loving my sister. The whole production, the flawless empty envelope, the most thorough thing I have ever built and the most disgusting, the one document in my career that pays no one and harms only its author.

I delete it. Not letter by letter this time — I haven't got the stomach to watch it go slowly — just select-all and gone, the way I deleted the Tolan, the way I delete everything that's true enough to convict me.

And I am left with exactly what I had in the first week, the blank, the white nothing, the cursor blinking in a field where four words should go. Except. Except now there's a difference, and the difference is the whole back half of my life: in the first week I thought the blankness was the problem. The thing to be solved. The hole the craft would eventually fill, given time, given skill, given one more good night and the right trance.

Tonight I understand the blankness is the only honest thing on the screen.

And I still can't fill it.

That's the midpoint. Not that I learned the lesson. I learned the lesson at 9:40 on a Wednesday and it changed nothing about my hands. The lesson is: the only way to reach her is to stop being the thing I am, and the thing I am is the only thing I know how to be, and I have until a window closes across a river to become a person I have spent fifteen years and every dollar I have ensuring I never had to be. I know the cure now. I have known the cure since a child told me she'd want to know if it works and I said *no, you don't get to know in advance.* Say it plainly. Say it badly. Say it worse than well. I can recite the cure. I sell a worse version of it for four hundred dollars an hour. And I sit in front of the blank and I cannot take a single dose of my own medicine, because every door I find in the wall, every opening, every way in, arrives already wearing the half-beat that reads as conscience, and a sentence you can hear yourself finding is no sentence, and I can hear myself finding all of them.

I close the laptop. The radiator clears its throat. For once I'd give anything if it would say the thing instead of deciding against it, just so one of us in this apartment could.

---

I don't sleep much. Around three I do the math I didn't want to do, the math the discovery file was supposed to spare me. There is no environment. There is no staging. There is no version A or version B. There is a morning, soon, in a room across a river, with a woman in it who is getting smaller and a woman beside her who has done the real version of this her whole adult life and will hear the craft the instant it leaves my mouth, the way she heard it on a bench, the way she heard it before it went there. And I will have nothing in my hands but my hands. The best hands in the country. The hands that built the empty chair.

And the worst part — I'll give you the worst part, since you can't fire me and I've come this far — is that even now, even having seen it, even in the dark at three in the morning with the lesson learned and the cure recited and my own sister's love sitting deleted in a trash folder I'll empty in the morning out of shame: even now, the cold clean competent part of me, the part with no off switch, the part that is the whole of me, is not thinking about Ada, or Delphine, or the four words, or the love I just A/B tested and threw away.

It's thinking that Ashe's victims surface tomorrow. The grateful ones. And that to write their apology — the gratitude, the betrayed trust, the love that got used as cover — I'm going to need an emotional logic I don't have on file for any client.

I'm going to have to borrow it from somewhere.

I know exactly where it's kept.


---

# Chapter 16 — Ventriloquists

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 6.5/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

Imani Brooke comes in carrying a tote bag that says *I'D RATHER BE READING* and an account of someone else's worst night that has sold four hundred thousand copies, and she would like us to help her be the victim of the people who noticed.

I have read the memoir. The firm sent it over with the brief, three days ago, with a sticky note from Renata that said *skim for the load-bearing lie*, which is Renata's idea of a literary opinion. I did not skim it. I read the whole thing, which is a mistake I make about twice a year, because the load-bearing lie in this case is a chapter near the middle — eleven pages, a hospital, a girl who doesn't make it to morning — and it is the best writing in the book, and it is about a death that did not happen to the woman whose name is on the spine.

It happened to her friend. Her dead friend. Who had it first, and lived it, and told Imani about it the way you tell one person a thing so it stops being only yours, and then died of something unrelated four years later and could not, therefore, be reached for comment when Imani published the night as her own.

"The framing," Imani says, sitting, arranging the tote at her feet like a dog she's training, "is what I want to push back on. The framing is that I *stole* it. And I think we both know that grief isn't property."

"We do know that," I say.

"You can't *steal* a feeling."

"You can't," I agree, and I let the agreement sit there long enough that she hears the thing under it, which is *but you can certainly sell one,* and I watch her decide not to hear it, which is a skill, and a relief, because it means she's going to be easy.

---

Let me tell you the problem, because the problem is the whole client.

The wronged party is dead. This is, from a strictly operational standpoint, the cleanest possible scandal, and I want to be honest that some animal part of me lit up at the brief the way it lights up at a clean kill, because a dead victim is a victim who will not give an interview. A dead victim does not have a brother who goes on a podcast. A dead victim does not, on the morning of your sit-down, post a photograph of herself in the hospital bed you described and write *this was my night.* The dead are the most cooperative wronged party in the business. They have made their statement. They will not be amending it.

Which means the entire apology — and there has to be an apology, the noticing has reached a volume where there has to be an apology — gets aimed somewhere else. It cannot be aimed at the dead woman, because you cannot apologize to a person who is not there to receive it; that's not an apology, that's a séance with a press strategy. So you aim it at the living. You aim it at the audience. The dead woman becomes, in the document, a kind of weather the apology happens under — referenced, honored, *held in the light* — while the actual transaction goes on between Imani and the four hundred thousand people who need permission to keep loving her.

Rule five. The apology is never for the wronged. It's for the witnesses. I have said this in this building so many times it's furniture. I have never once felt it move.

It moves now. I feel it move now, low, under the desk, somewhere I don't have a tool for.

---

"The dead can't be wronged twice," Imani is saying. She has clearly been workshopping this. "That's almost a — I think that's almost a *principle.* She's at peace. Nothing I do now touches her. Whereas what *I'm* going through—"

"Tell me about what you're going through."

She does. At length. It is genuinely awful, what she's going through, in the sense that public contempt is genuinely awful and she did not, whatever else she did, deserve to be alone in it at three in the morning reading what strangers think a person like her should do to herself. I write a little of it down. Not because I need it. Because writing makes my hands feel less like the hands that are about to do the next part.

The next part is this. I am going to build Imani an apology that uses the dead woman one more time. We used her once for the memoir — for the four hundred thousand, for the prize longlist, for the chapter that's the best thing in the book. And now, to get Imani out from under the noticing, we are going to use her again, as the centerpiece of the contrition: *her memory deserves better, and so do all of you, and I will spend the rest of my career making sure—* The dead woman, drafted a second time. Once as the material. Once as the alibi. Two appearances, no fee, no consent, because consent is a thing the living do.

I have built this exact machine before. The senator's omni-scandal was eleven living plaintiffs and a poem with no one in it. This is one dead one and a poem that's all about her and not for her at all.

It is, I realize, somewhere around the second cup of the bad coffee, the same machine my whole life is built on.

---

"The move," I tell her, "is that you don't apologize for taking it. You apologize for *loving her so much you couldn't tell where she ended and you began.*"

Imani's eyes do a thing. The wet thing. The good thing, professionally; she's a writer, she's got instrument.

"Oh," she says. "Oh, that's—"

"You carried her," I say. "That's the line. You carried her so long she became part of how you understood your own life, and in the carrying — in the *love* — the boundary blurred, and you told her story as your own not out of theft but out of a grief so total it dissolved the line between two people. You're not a thief. You're a woman who loved someone past the limit of her own skin."

"That's *true,*" Imani breathes, and the terrible thing, the Hartley thing, the thing I have learned this year is the worst thing — is that for her it is. She believes it. She is going to perform it perfectly because she is not performing. I have just handed her a way to be sincere about a lie, and she has taken it the way you take a coat someone holds for you, sliding her arms in without looking.

"It tests," I say, which is the most honest thing I will say all meeting. "Loved-her-past-her-own-skin tests at the top of the range. People forgive love. They don't forgive theft, but theft dressed as love, they'll not only forgive, they'll *cry.* We're not lying. We're reframing the same facts under a kinder grammar."

I hear myself say *we're not lying.* I have a folder at home full of the times I was lying. I do not say this. I write another word on the pad. The word is *skin.*

---

Here is the part I am not going to dress up.

About forty minutes in, Imani is reading a draft of the load-bearing passage aloud — the one where she carries her friend past the boundary of herself — and she gets to a sentence I built, a sentence I built *well,* about putting the words in a dead mouth so the mouth would not have to stay silent, and the thing happens.

I have a stomach. I forget this. It is not in the brief. But somewhere under the part of me that schedules and the part that scores, there is a stomach, an animal, dumb, ungovernable organ that does not subscribe to the firm's view of the world, and it has been listening to this meeting, and it picks this exact sentence — *so the mouth would not have to stay silent* — to register an opinion.

The opinion is nausea. Flat, cold, rising. The kind that has no metaphor in it because the body does not deal in metaphor; the body deals in *out.* I put my hand on the desk. The room goes slightly far away, the way it does, the lights and the foam and Imani's wet good eyes receding down a tube, and I think, with the small administrative clarity that survives everything, *I am going to be sick in a remorse reading, and that has never happened, and I do not have a line item for it.*

I do not throw up. I am a professional. I breathe through it the way Sloan taught us to coach clients to breathe through the urge to over-emote — *low, into the belly, four counts* — and I use the technique I invented for faking composure to manufacture the real thing, which is its own small horror I file for later. The room comes back. Imani has not noticed. Imani is crying, beautifully, on a line I wrote about a woman she put words into after the woman could no longer take them out.

"That's the one," I say. My voice is fine. My voice is always fine. "That's the line we lead with. Hold a half-beat before *silent.*"

It is the line we will lead with. It will work. It is also the exact thing I did at a graveside fifteen years ago, and my stomach knew it before I did, and that is the only honest organ I have left.

---

Because here is what my stomach knew.

I made our mother say something.

I have always told the story to myself as a thing I said *about* Ada — *Mom never wanted you* — a cruelty I committed against Delphine, the living one, the one in front of me, the one I could hurt. That's how I've filed it for fifteen years: a thing I did to my sister. And it is that. But it is also, I understand now with my hand flat on a conference table and a stranger weeping over my sentence, a thing I did to my *mother.* I put words in her mouth. I ventriloquized the dead — except she wasn't dead, she was upstairs, she was alive, she has been alive the whole time, which is somehow worse, because Imani's friend at least had the decency to be unreachable. Mine wasn't. Mine was *available.* And I made her say the cruelest possible thing, in absentia, at a funeral, to her own daughter, and I never once asked the actual woman whether she'd have said it, because asking would have meant she might say *no,* and I needed it to be *yes.*

Imani used a dead woman who couldn't object.

I used a living one who didn't know she'd been quoted.

We are the same client. I am sitting on the wrong side of the table coaching myself.

---

The reclassification finishes here. I can feel it finish, the way you feel a bone set — a wrongness moving into a worse but more honest place.

For fifteen years the thing I owed was an apology. *I'm sorry I said it. I'm sorry I hurt you.* An apology is about me — my regret, my growth, my managed remorse, my four hundred dollars an hour of contrition, and I have spent a career learning that an apology, however gorgeous, asks nothing of the facts. You can apologize for a thing that's true. You can apologize for a thing that's false. The grammar doesn't care. *Sorry* is sovereign over feeling and silent on fact, which is exactly why I'm good at it and exactly why it's useless here.

Because Delphine doesn't want an apology. She told me that on a Tuesday with our mother dying in the background. She wants to know *if it was true.* And that is not an apology. That is a *recantation,* or a *confirmation,* and there is no product for either, I've checked, I wrote the menu, the menu does not include *taking it back* because you cannot sell taking it back; the whole economy runs the other direction.

And now — sitting here, sick, having watched a woman cry over a borrowed corpse — I understand the recantation isn't owed to one person. It's owed to two. It's owed to Delphine, the living one, who has lived fifteen years inside a sentence. And it's owed to Ada, the quoted one, the one I made speak, who never got to say whether the words I put in her mouth were words she'd ever have chosen. I libeled a living woman to wound her daughter. The debt has two names on it now, and one of them is in a bed across a river, getting smaller, holding the only verification in the world — and she can only be reached, the dead-soon one, the witness, through the living one. Through Delphine. The two of them are a single closed circuit and I am the short.

You owe the dead through the living. Imani can't reach her friend; she can only reach the audience. I can't reach my mother except by going through the sister, into the room, before the window shuts. The recantation is one act with two recipients and a clock on it.

I write none of this down. My hands want to. I don't let them.

---

We break. Imani goes to call her agent, who is also her cousin, which is the kind of detail that used to delight me. Dev finds me by the window with the foam and the better lighting, and he has the deck open on his tablet, the slides, the *PATHWAY,* and he's frowning at it in the way that's going to cost him.

"Can I say a thing," Dev says, "that's maybe above my pay grade."

"Your pay grade is below the building's plumbing. Say the thing."

"The friend." He says it quietly. "The dead one. We've got her in here eleven times. I counted. She's in the acknowledgment, she's in the apology, she's in the *comeback* note as the reason for the new foundation. We're — Mona, we're putting her to work. She's dead and we've got her doing eleven jobs."

I look at the slide he's holding. There she is, the dead woman, a name in a sans-serif font, scheduled across a redemption arc like a contractor.

"That's the job," I say.

"I know it's the job." He's not letting it go. He doesn't let things go; it's the most expensive thing about him. "I just — at what point is the apology worse than the thing it's apologizing for. Like. She stole a dead woman's worst night. And our fix is to make the dead woman *star* in the apology for stealing her worst night. We're stealing it again. With a candle."

And there it is — the exact knife, handed across by a twenty-eight-year-old who thinks he's asking a craft question. *At what point is the apology worse than the thing it's apologizing for.*

I want to tell him it's a brilliant question. I want to tell him it's the only question. I want to tell him that I have a sister and a mother and a sentence, and that I have spent fifteen years building apology after apology so I would never have to face the fact that the apology I owe is worse than useless — that what I owe isn't an apology at all, that an apology would be one more theft, one more time I use a quiet woman as raw material for my own absolution. I want to tell him that I am the client. That I'm Imani. That I'm worse than Imani, because Imani's victim is at peace and mine is awake in the dark, askable, for a few more weeks, and I am still — even now, even sick, even *knowing* — running the discovery instead of making the call.

"Bill the slide deck out under Strategy," I say. "And cut her to four. Four mentions. Not eleven."

Dev blinks. "That's — we lose the comeback note if I cut—"

"Then we lose the comeback note." I take the tablet, and I delete the dead woman out of seven slides myself, one tap at a time, and it does nothing for her, it can't, she's dead, it's a gesture, it's me dusting a grave with my sleeve. "She's done enough jobs. Let her clock out."

Dev watches me do it with an expression I don't have a score for, which is becoming a thing that happens around Dev, and I do not enjoy it.

---

I go home. The radiator says something halfway and reconsiders, which is the most tactful thing in my life.

I do not open `untitled.docx`. I do not open the discovery file with my sister's name in the client slot, the one I told myself I'd delete and didn't. Tonight my hands don't reach for the keys, and I notice the not-reaching the way you notice the dog hasn't barked.

Instead I sit on the edge of the bed in my coat and I do a thing I have not done in the entire length of this — I try to picture the room. Not draft it. Not light it, not pick the angle, not stage Ada the way I staged Delphine on a doc three months ago with a side angle and a soft key. Just picture it. A bed. A woman in it I have not seen in fifteen years, smaller now, the witness, the quoted one. Delphine somewhere near, doing the thing she does, the genuine version, the just-not-leaving. And me, in the doorway, owing two people one impossible sentence, with my stomach for once telling the truth and my mouth — the trained one, the four-hundred-dollar one, the best in the country — standing there full of every word except the four that count.

I can build anyone a way to be sorry.

I cannot build a way to take it back, because the only honest way to take it back is to not know how it'll sound, and I have never once in my life said a sentence I didn't already know the sound of.

The window's open, Delphine said. Mornings. It comes and goes.

I should call her tonight. I should say *I'm coming.* I have the number now; I know the town; the call is four taps and a breath. I pick up the phone. I look at it. The cold clean competent part of me is already drafting what I'll say when she answers, already building the opening, already reaching for the dial —

and I put the phone down, because a thing I can already hear myself saying is not a thing worth saying to a dying woman, and that is the whole disease, named, in my own hand, with no one paying me to write it.

I'll call tomorrow.

I am very, very good at telling myself things.

Across the river my mother is awake getting smaller, holding the answer, on the clock. And my stomach, the one honest organ I have left, sits in the dark with me and waits, the way you wait outside a room someone is dying in, and does not let me sleep.


---

# Chapter 17 — The Grateful Victims

Act II-a  ·  Fluency gauge: 6.5/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

The thing nobody tells you about a redemption is that it has a warranty, and the warranty is shorter than the redemption.

Draft 1 shipped three weeks ago. Draft 1 worked. The public exhaled, the sentiment line did its hopeful little climb, eleven million people decided that Roland Ashe was a man who had lost his way and found a microphone, and for three weeks the firm was happy in the way the firm is happy, which is to say nobody said the word *bonus* but several people started standing closer to Renata.

Then a reporter in another country found a second filing cabinet. Metaphorically. I don't know if filing cabinets are still a thing people find. The point is that the laundering — the dirty money pressed flat and ironed into the shape of a children's hospital — turned out to be the *clean* part of the story. The laundering was the alibi. What the laundering was an alibi *for* is the thing we are now in a conference room about.

"Walk me through the new exposure," Renata says.

The strategist whose turn it is to be useless today — a tall one, Garrick or Garrett, not the chef, a different one, the firm grows them like cress — clicks to a slide. The slide says, in the firm's expensive sans-serif, **MATERIAL DEVELOPMENTS.** Material developments. As if rot were a flooring choice.

"So the access," Garrick says. "Through the foundation. It wasn't only financial. He used the foundation's relationships. The children's hospital, yes, but also a refugee resettlement program and a hospice network — the foundation underwrote them, so the foundation got the run of them. And there are now, ah. People. Coming forward. Who were in those programs."

"Victims," I say.

"We're not using that word yet."

"I know we're not. I'm using it in the room so we don't forget it exists. What did he do to them."

Garrick does the thing where a man's Adam's apple becomes briefly the most expressive part of him.

"It's less *did,*" he says. "It's more — he was *present* to them. As a benefactor. He sat with the dying. He held the children. There's footage. He's good in the footage, he's — warm, he's, he wipes a tear, he means it, you can tell he means it —"

"He doesn't mean it," I say. "He can't mean it. We established three weeks ago there's nobody home."

"Right, but the footage. And these people — the ones coming forward — they're not angry that he *hurt* them. That's the problem. They're —" Garrick checks his notes as if the word might have improved while he wasn't looking. "They're grateful. They loved him. He was the nicest thing that happened to some of them. And now they've found out he was using them — the photographs, the access, the *I held a dying child and a photographer was there* — and they feel —"

"Used," Renata says.

"Worse than used," I say, before I've decided to. "Used would be clean. Used you can be angry about. These people gave him something real and found out it went into a machine. They're not just hurt. They're *embarrassed* for having loved him. That's a different injury. That's the one that doesn't scar over."

The room looks at me. I let it. There's a particular silence a room makes when I've said the true thing one beat earlier than a true thing is wanted, and I have learned to enjoy it the way you enjoy a draft from a window you can't find — coldly, and while looking for the gap.

"Good," says Renata, who hears everything as a deliverable. "That. Put that in the apology. The embarrassment. Make him *name* it."

"Make a man with no inside narrate the inside of the people he gutted."

"You're the one who said it doesn't scar over. Use it. Bigger, this time. Braver." She caps her pen, which is how she signs a contract with the air. "Draft One was a *sorry.* Draft Two has to be a *reckoning.* He has to look like a man who finally understood what he took. The first apology was for what he did to the *numbers.* This one's for what he did to the *people.*"

"He didn't understand anything. There's nothing in there to do the understanding with."

"Then you understand it for him," Renata says, "and lend it to him at our hourly rate. That's the job, Mona. That's been the job the whole time. You're just usually further from the window when you do it."

She's not wrong. She is never wrong; it's her least bearable quality. I write down *braver* on my legal pad, and then, because my hand has opinions my brain hasn't cleared, I write under it *the one that doesn't scar over,* and then I look at that, and then I turn the page so I can't.

---

Here is the gallows joke the room has been telling itself all week, and I'll give it to you because you can't repeat it.

We call Ashe *the gift that keeps on giving.* This started as bleak shorthand for the billing — every layer of rot is a new deliverable, a Statement begets a Reckoning begets, God help us all, a *Listening Tour* — and somewhere in week two it stopped being about money. Now we say it the way you say the name of a weather system that's decided to live over your house. Babs said it Tuesday with almonds in her cheek: *the gift that keeps on giving, and it's giving again, and we are the registry.* Dev didn't laugh. Dev has stopped laughing at the Ashe jokes, which I have noticed, the way you notice a tooth before it's a problem.

The comedy of the thing — and it is comedy, it is the blackest comedy I have ever been paid for — is that the worse Roland Ashe turns out to be, the *better* the apology has to be, which means the worse he is the harder I work, which means I am pouring the best of myself into the deepest hole on earth, with a smile, on a schedule. He betrayed a hospice. Cost: forty billable hours and the best sentence I have. He betrayed *refugees,* people who crossed water to be safe and were photographed being safe by a man monetizing the safety. Cost: a long weekend and a metaphor I'd been saving.

You'd think there'd be a bottom. There is no bottom. There's just the next invoice and a fresh atrocity to put a catch in the voice on. *There is no apology,* Babs says, somewhere down the hall, to no one. *There's only the next thing they're allowed to want from you.* I used to think that was about clients.

---

I take the draft home, which is the first mistake.

I don't usually. There's a hygiene to it — the room is where I do it, the room has bad foam on the walls and worse coffee and Petra running sentiment in the corner and a whiteboard that's seen things; the room is the kitchen and you don't bring the kitchen to bed. But Draft 2 is a reckoning and a reckoning is a long document, and the building closes, and I am, I tell myself, a professional who can keep her drives sorted.

So it's eleven at night and I'm at the kitchen table that is also my desk that is also the only table, and the radiator is making the sound it makes — a man clearing his throat to say something difficult, then deciding the silence is fine, actually — and I have the Ashe file open, and I am trying to build the sentence Renata wants. The one where Roland Ashe, who is not in there, looks at the people who loved him and tells them he knows exactly what he took.

The problem is craft and the problem is craft only. I want to be clear about that, because in a minute it's going to stop being only craft and I want you to have seen the seam.

The craft problem is this: betrayal of *grateful* love is the hardest grief to fake, because it's the hardest grief to *feel,* because it requires the apologizer to inhabit, fully, the specific humiliation of the person who chose to trust him. Anger is easy to write to. Anger wants spectacle, wants a villain, wants the apologizer on his knees, and a man on his knees is a man I can stage in my sleep. But these people aren't angry. They're standing in the wreckage of their own good faith, holding the evidence that the warmest thing in their life was an expense line, and the apology has to reach *that* — has to find the exact tone of a man saying *you were right to love what you saw, the love wasn't the lie, I was the lie, and I let you keep loving it because your love was useful to me and I'd rather you went on being used than that I lost the use of you.*

I write toward it. I write three bad versions, corporate, weather, *trust was broken, harm was caused,* crimes as climate. I strike them. I write a fourth that's better and lands nowhere, that's *competent,* and competent is the new failure, competent is the watermark, Sloan's right, correct is the tell now. I strike that too.

And then, in the trance — the one where the sentences start arriving from the room under the room, the place I don't sign invoices from — I find it. I find the line that does the thing. It comes the way the true ones come, with the heavy click, the grain found, and I get it onto the screen before the part of me that bills can object:

*You let me keep believing the best of you because it cost you nothing and it kept me. You knew I'd defend you. You built your whole defense out of people who'd defend you for free.*

I read it back. It's perfect. It's the sentence. It is, I understand with the particular vertigo of a craftsman who's overshot, far too good — not too sincere for the public, the public will eat it, the public will *clip* it — too good in the other direction, the dangerous one. It's true. It's the truest thing in the document and it has the heavy click and I am so pleased for one half of one second, purely, stupidly pleased, the way I was pleased about a chef once —

and then I see who it's for.

It is not for Roland Ashe.

It has never once, in the history of the world, been about Roland Ashe.

*You let me keep believing the best of you because it cost you nothing and it kept me.* That's Delphine. That's Delphine at nine, at twelve, at twenty-four, being the wanted one, the easy one, the second daughter who got the second, happier marriage and the father who taught her to swim and a mother who — and there I am, the older one, the harder one, the one who raised herself two rooms over, watching my sister believe the best of all of us for free, knowing what I'd eventually do with that free defense, that I'd built nothing but kept the option, kept her, kept her trusting me right up until the graveside where I spent the whole account in one sentence. *You knew I'd defend you.* She did defend me. To people. For years. Before. She told people her big sister was the smart one, the funny one. She defended me for free and I knew she would and I let her, the way you let a thing keep running because you might need it, and then I needed it, and I used it to destroy her, at her own father's funeral, with our mother's voice in my mouth.

I sit at the table that is also my desk and I do not move for a while.

The radiator clears its throat. Thinks better of it.

---

I want to tell you I deleted it.

I want to tell you I had the decency to recognize a stolen thing and put it back. But here is the part I'd deny under oath, here is the new sickness, worse than the chef:

I *kept* it. In the Ashe draft. Because it works *in the Ashe draft.* Because Renata wants *braver* and there is nothing braver in the English language than a man telling the people who loved him that he built his defense out of their love, and the only reason I can write that sentence at all, the only reason it has the click, is that it's true about me, and the cruelest economy I have ever run is now fully operational and I'm running it on myself: my most private wound is my most billable asset, and I will sell it, tonight, into the mouth of a man with nobody home, and it will *land,* it will land harder than anything I've shipped this year, *because* it's real, and no one will ever know it's real, or whose, or what it cost, and that — the not-knowing, the way the true thing disappears clean into the lie — that's not a flaw in the work. That's the work performing perfectly.

I should be more disturbed than I am. There isn't a line item for *appropriate amount of horror at yourself,* so I move on, which is the most damning sentence in this whole chapter and I'm aware of it and I'm typing it anyway. That's the job. That's been the job the whole time.

I do the margin notes. This is reflex; I annotate everything; the annotations are where the cynicism and whatever's left of me argue in the only forum they have. I write, beside the stolen line: *STET — best in the doc.* I write: *they'll clip this; protect the build to it.* I write: *hold half a beat before "free" — costs him to say it.*

And then I write, in the margin, in my own hand, to no one, in the voice I use for the thing under the thing:

*just say it. stop performing it and say it.*

I look at that note for a long time.

Because that note isn't for Roland Ashe. You don't tell a client to stop performing; performing is what he's paying for; *stop performing* is malpractice in my trade, it's the one note I'm professionally forbidden to write. *Stop performing* is a note I have written exactly once before, two nights ago, at this same table, on a different document — the one with no name, the one in `untitled_3.docx`, the one to my sister — where it was the only honest thing on an otherwise blank page and I deleted the page and kept, apparently, the note, the way you keep a key to a house you've sold.

It migrated. The note migrated. I meant it for Delphine and it walked across some membrane I didn't know was permeable and now it's sitting in the margin of a billionaire's reckoning, *just say it, stop performing it and say it,* a private instruction to my own soul filed as professional guidance to a man who has no soul to instruct, and I cannot tell — I sit here at eleven-forty at night and I genuinely cannot tell — whether I wrote it to Ashe and it's leaking toward Delphine or wrote it to Delphine and it leaked into Ashe.

I don't delete it.

I tell myself it's a good note for the draft. It's not a bad note for the draft.

I am very good at telling myself things, but the gift has a half-life and tonight I can hear it expire.

---

There's one more thing in Garrick's deck, and I'd almost gotten past it, and now it gets past me.

The list. The foundation's relationships, the access points, the warm rooms where Roland Ashe was photographed being warm. Children's hospital. Refugee program. And the third one, the one I read in the conference room without reading it, the way you walk past your own door when you're going somewhere worse:

*Hospice network.*

He went into hospices. He sat at the ends of beds with people in their last weeks and held their hands while a foundation underwrote the room and a story underwrote the foundation, and somewhere there is footage of Roland Ashe being present, with no script, at the unfixable — being, for the camera, the exact thing my sister actually is, every day, for almost no money, with the cameras off, because there are no cameras, because nobody productizes the real version. Delphine does the true thing in the rooms Ashe rented for the false one. The same rooms. I am writing an apology for a man who counterfeited my sister's vocation, using a wound I gave my sister, to be performed in a hospice-shaped sentence, while two weeks from a wedding our mother lies in an actual one of those rooms, dying, with the only answer to the only question I have left getting fainter on her tongue by the hour.

The worlds aren't supposed to touch. I keep them in different files. I have always kept them in different files.

They're touching.

I close the laptop. I don't add the Ashe line to the orphaned-sincerities folder, where the true things people made me delete go to live; I can't; it isn't deleted, it's *shipping,* it's the headline, it's going out under his name on Thursday and it will work and I built it out of the worst thing I ever did and a sentence I wrote for a chef taught me I could only do this through someone else's mouth and now I've graduated, I can do it through the worst mouth there is, and I still can't do it through my own.

The draft is due Thursday. It'll ship. It always ships. The public will soften; the grateful will be invited, gently, to forgive; the sentiment line will do its hopeful little climb; and somewhere in there, eleven million strangers will feel, in the soft animal place that decides these things, that they have witnessed a man understand what he took — feel it because of a sentence that's true, that's mine, that's hers, that's for sale.

I get up. I put the laptop in the other room, the way you'd carry a thing that might go off. The radiator clears its throat behind me, gets all the way to the edge of the word, holds it half a beat — that reads as conscience — and then, like always, like me, decides against it.

Tomorrow there's a corporation coming in. Faceless, soulless, systemic, clean — a client with no inside to betray and no love to spend, the purest air in the building. After the week I'm having I could kiss it. They hurt a lot of people and nobody can say which executive, and they want to know which word tests better, *devastated* or *sorry,* and I already know the answer, everybody in the trade knows the answer, and I'll run the focus group anyway because the numbers are a sacrament, and I'll write the word that wins.

It's the cleanest job there is. Nothing of mine can leak into it.

I keep telling myself that, all the way to a bed I won't sleep in.



---

# Chapter 18 — Devastated, Not Sorry

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 6/10  ·  Artifact: YES

---

**THE BELLWETHER GROUP — PUBLIC STATEMENT, DRAFT 6 (FOR APPROVAL)**

> On behalf of everyone at Bellwether, ~~we are sorry.~~ [INS: we are devastated.]
>
>       *[margin: "devastated" +9 vs "sorry" (n=412). "sorry" admits a them; "devastated" makes US the room that got hurt. STET devastated. it is the whole ballgame.]*
>
> We have learned that some of our ~~products caused~~ [INS: families were affected by issues related to] our ~~baby formula~~ [INS: nutrition portfolio], and ~~we did this~~ [INS: mistakes were made].
>
>       *[margin: never "we caused." "families were affected" — the harm becomes weather, the families become the weather system, nobody's standing in the rain on purpose. "mistakes were made": the most beautiful empty chair in the language. who made them? the mistakes made themselves. STET.]*
>       *[margin: cut "baby formula." "baby" is a face. a face is a verdict. "nutrition portfolio" tested at +14 vs "baby formula" — people can't be angry at a portfolio, there's no one home in it. that's the point.]*
>
> Our hearts go out to ~~the children who~~ [INS: all those impacted], and we ~~take full accountability~~ [INS: are committed to doing better].
>
>       *[margin: "our hearts go out" — agentless, free, costs nothing, ships heat with no liability. "the children who" — NO. never finish that sentence, the end of that sentence is a deposition. "all those impacted" — passive, faceless, un-suable.]*
>       *[margin: kill "take full accountability." focus group: "accountability" now reads as PR, -3, they've heard it from everyone. "committed to doing better" forward-looks, no past tense, no admission. the past is where the lawyers live. keep him in the future, the future is empty.]*

---

The Bellwether Group makes, among nine hundred other things, an infant formula that, for a window of about fourteen months, was not quite what it said on the tin, and an unknown number of families found out the hard way, and now I am in a focus-group facility off the interstate with two-way glass, watching eleven strangers from a screened sample decide which word makes them less inclined to sue.

I want to be clear about the facility, because the facility is the whole joke. It is a beige conference room with another beige conference room behind a mirror, and we — me, a strategist named Corwin who is paid to be handsome at clients, and Dr. Halvard Sloan with his laptop full of faces — sit in the dark side, eating the same shrink-wrapped cookies the eleven strangers are eating in the light side, watching them feel things on cue for forty dollars and parking validation. There is a dial. Each of the eleven holds a dial, an actual physical dial like a thermostat, and they turn it left when a sentence makes them feel worse about Bellwether and right when it makes them feel better, and a line on my monitor goes up and down in real time, and that line is the closest thing to a human conscience this entire operation has produced in fourteen months.

"Run the A," I say into the headset.

A moderator in the light room — sweet, bovine, reads the statements off cards with the warmth of a woman announcing bingo — reads version A. *On behalf of everyone at Bellwether, we are sorry.*

The line drops. Eleven dials, left.

"They hate sorry," Corwin says, delighted, the way he is delighted by everything, because to Corwin the dial going down is information and information is billable and Corwin has never once in his life stood in the rain.

"They don't hate sorry," I say. "Watch the readout. They're not angrier. They're more *sure.* Sorry made them sure. Sorry told them there's a them and a Bellwether, and once there's a them, they remember they're the them. Sorry is an organizational chart. Run the B."

The moderator reads version B. *On behalf of everyone at Bellwether, we are devastated.*

The line goes up. Not a lot. Nine points, give or take, which is the number I knew it would be before we drove out here, because I have run this exact test on this exact word for a baby-food company and a pipeline and a megachurch, and the answer is always nine, give or take, the way water is always wet. *Devastated* outperforms *sorry* by nine points and I could have saved Bellwether the drive and the cookies and the forty dollars times eleven, but the executives don't believe a word until they watch a stranger hold a dial, and there's a deep one there about the men who run the world only trusting numbers they personally watched a poor person generate, but I'm on the clock, so I file it.

"Why," says Corwin. "Why *devastated.* It's a bigger word. You'd think a bigger word—"

"Because *sorry* is about them and *devastated* is about us." I don't look away from the line. "*Sorry* says we did a thing to you. *Devastated* says a thing happened, near us, and look how it's affected our hearts. *Devastated* puts Bellwether in the ICU next to the babies. You can't stay mad at a company that's in the next bed over, also suffering, also a victim, of the formula it made. *Devastated* invites the public to grieve *with* Bellwether instead of *for* the children, and grieving with someone is the fastest forgiveness there is. *Sorry* is a confession. *Devastated* is a candlelight vigil where the company gets to hold a candle."

Behind me Sloan makes a small noise. Sloan makes small noises. He is watching the eleven faces on his split screen, frame by frame, coding the involuntary muscles, and he says, not to me, to the faces, the way he always talks to the faces, "Respondent six is crying on B. Real one. Look at the orbicularis — that's a Duchenne wet eye, that's not performed, she's actually moved."

"Moved by what," I say.

"By *devastated,*" Sloan says, and there's a thing in his voice, an old grief he carries around like a man with a bad knee. "She thinks the company is sad. She's crying because she feels for the company. She's one of the families, she's a screened user, that's why she's in the sample, her — her nephew was in the recall window. And she's crying for *Bellwether.*"

We all look at respondent six. Forties, cardigan, the dial loose in her hand, turned all the way to the right, all the way to *I feel better about Bellwether*, and a real tear, the kind I bill four hundred an hour to fake, coming down a real face because a sentence I wrote made a multinational that poisoned her nephew's bottle look, for nine points' worth of a moment, like the saddest one in the room.

There is a version of me, a younger one, who would have felt something here. I want to report honestly that I do not. I feel the click. I feel the small clean competent satisfaction of a thing landing exactly where I aimed it. *Devastated* works. Respondent six is the proof. I have made a victim cry for her own perpetrator and the line on the monitor is pointing up, and the only sound in the dark room is Sloan, quietly, like a man at a grave that isn't his, saying, "God, she's good, isn't she. The cardigan one. She'd believe anything you put in front of her." And then, lower: "We did that. We taught the rain to apologize for the people standing in it."

"Bill it as insight," Corwin says, writing it down.

---

The Bellwether brass come in for the read-out at three. There are four of them and they are interchangeable in the specific way that very expensive men are interchangeable, like a set of golf clubs, and they file into the beige room and sit and want to be told that the dial says they're good people.

I run them the numbers. I am good at running numbers at men like this. You give them the *sorry* line first and you let the dial drop and you watch their faces fall, watch them feel, for one delicious unbillable second, the cold of being hated, and *then* you give them *devastated* and the line goes up and you watch the relief flood through them like a drug, and they think the relief is forgiveness when it's just contrast, but the contrast is the product, the contrast is what they're paying for, the feeling of having been forgiven that I manufacture out of nine points and a dial.

"So we go with devastated," says the one in the middle, the CEO, who has the face of a man who has never once carried his own bag.

"We go with devastated."

"And we don't say — the lawyers said we can't say—"

"You don't say anything. That's the genius of it. *Devastated* doesn't say anything. *Devastated* is a feeling, and you can't be sued for a feeling. We center the statement on Bellwether's grief, we make the harm into something that *happened* rather than something you *did*, we put your hearts out to all those impacted — note 'all those impacted,' not 'the children,' the second you say 'children' a jury can see a child and a jury that can see a child writes a number with a lot of zeroes — and we keep you entirely in the future tense, committed to doing better, never once admitting you did worse, because the past is where the discovery is."

The CEO nods slowly. He likes it. They always like it. And then he says the thing they always say, the thing that is the actual reason they hired a firm instead of just shutting up, the thing that is the whole rotten sacrament at the center of my trade:

"I do feel terrible about it. The families. I want them to know that. I genuinely lose sleep."

And I say what I always say, the thing that lets them sleep again, which is the actual deliverable, the thing they're really buying:

"Of course you do. And we're going to make sure they can feel that you do."

We are not going to make him feel it. He already feels it; feeling it is free; feeling it costs him nothing and changes nothing and the babies are still the babies. We are going to make eleven million strangers *believe* he feels it, which is the only feeling that's ever been worth money. I am not in the truth business. I have said this before. I will say it until the day they put me in the ground next to whatever's left of respondent six's faith in cardigans. I am in the witness business. And business is excellent.

"This is good work," the CEO tells me, on the way out, with the warm condescension of a man tipping a sommelier. "You should be proud of this."

"I'm devastated I can't take public credit," I say, deadpan, and Corwin laughs because Corwin thinks it's a joke about the word, and it is, it absolutely is, it's a tight little joke about the word, and I built it fast and clean and it lands, and I file the fact that I built it before I felt anything, the way I file everything I can't afford, in the place where the things go.

---

The statement ships at six. It will work. It always works. *Bellwether says it is devastated by issues affecting families in its nutrition portfolio* and the public will read *devastated* and feel, in the soft animal two-second part of them that decides these things, that a big sad company is hurting too, and the line on a thousand monitors will tick up, and somewhere a cardigan will believe it, and that is the proudest invisible thing a person can be, and most nights it's enough.

I should go home and sleep. I don't sleep. I go home and I open the laptop, and the laptop has the day still hot in it, the whole vocabulary still loaded, *devastated, hearts go out, all those impacted, committed to doing better,* and I do the thing I do every night and lose at, which is I open `untitled.docx`, the one I can't title, the one for my sister, the one with the window in it now, the window into a room across a river where our mother is awake in the dark getting smaller and holding the one answer in the world I need.

I have a deadline now. I didn't, for fifteen years, and now I do, and a deadline is a thing my hands know what to do with. Delphine said weeks. The window is mornings, mostly. It comes and goes. *Say the true thing, out loud, soon, before there's no one left who knows.* I have a clock and a single point of failure, and the part of me that schedules is, God forgive it, *ready.*

I put my hands on the keys.

And what comes — the only opening I can find, the door in the wall, the first two words that arrive after a day of arriving at words, fast and clean and loaded and warm:

*I'm devastated.*

I look at it.

It sits there on the white, *I'm devastated,* the catch already pre-installed where it would go on the second syllable, the word that outperforms by nine points, the word I built a thousand empty chairs out of, the word that puts you in the ICU next to the person you hurt so they can't stay angry at a body in the next bed, the word that makes the harm into weather, the centers-on-me word, the candlelight-vigil-where-you-get-to-hold-a-candle word, the word I have spent my entire career choosing on purpose over the other one because it is *better,* it tests better, it works—

I typed the company's word.

To my sister. To Delphine, who does not want a candle and does not want me in the next bed grieving prettily and does not want my hearts going out to all those impacted. Who wants one fact said plainly in my own voice. And the most trained muscle I have, firing before I gave it permission, faster than grief, reached past the four words a child has and handed her *devastated,* handed her the thing I'd say on behalf of a baby-food company, because somewhere under everything, lower than I can usually see, I would rather be the one who's hurting than the one who did it. I would rather be devastated than sorry. Even here. Even now. Even to her.

There is a word she's owed. I know which one it is. I've known for fifteen years; it's the one I make a fortune talking other people into the *appearance* of and have never once said in my own mouth. It's the confession word. The one that admits a them. The one that builds the organizational chart, *me here, you there, and a thing I did between us,* the one that drops the dial. *Sorry* drops the dial. Of course it does. That's what it's for. It's supposed to cost you the nine points. The nine points are the price, and I have spent a career and a fortune learning, with tweezers, exactly how never to pay them.

I don't write *sorry.* I can't get my hands to do it. I sit here, the best in the country, and my fingers will produce *devastated* in their sleep and lock up solid on the other word like a man whose right hand won't sign a confession.

I delete it.

Letter by letter, holding the key down the way I do, watching *I'm devastated* go, the d, the e, the v, all of it, back into the white. The cursor blinks at nothing. Patient. A heart with nothing in it yet.

The distance between those two words is nine points. I know that number better than I know my own. Nine points is the gap between the word that works and the word that's true, between the witnesses and the wronged, between the candle and the chart. I have spent my whole adult life standing on the *devastated* side of that gap and selling the trip across to people who never meant to make it. Nine points. It doesn't sound like much. It's the only distance left in the world I don't know how to cover, and it runs from a one-bedroom on this side of a river to a bed on the other, and it is due, in my own voice, before a window closes, and I cannot move my hand nine points to the right.

---

I leave the doc open this time. I don't know why. For fifteen years I have closed it; tonight I leave it open, blinking, the cursor sitting in the white where a word should be, and I get up and I stand at the window and the radiator clears its throat and, reading the room again, says nothing, the coward.

There's a thing I notice, standing there, and I notice it the way Sloan notices the orbicularis, frame by frame, against my will. I notice that even now — *especially* now — some part of me is already drafting. Already at the legal pad. Already wondering whether *sorry* would land better with a pause before it or after, whether to do it on the phone or in the room, whether the room is the venue or the parking lot, what the delivery environment does to the word, where Delphine's tells are, what the ask under the ask—

There is no ask under the ask. I learned that on the phone on a Tuesday. The surface is the depth. And still, *still,* my hands are reaching for the swatch-book, the tweezers, the dial, because there is, it turns out, no document in my life the trade won't try to write — not the formula, not the senator, not my own mother dying, not this — and tomorrow, I already know, I will open the Delphine letter again and find that I have started, somewhere in the margin, in my own hand, to leave myself notes.


---

# Chapter 19 — Stet the Catch in the Voice

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 5.5/10  ·  Artifact: No

---

The Ashe Draft 2 shipped on a Tuesday and worked, which is the part I keep not being able to feel.

I want to be accurate about the timeline, because the timeline is the only thing I still trust. Layer 2 broke ten days ago — the laundering turned out to be the floor over a worse room, the worse room being a children's charity and a refugee program and a hospice that Roland Ashe used the way other men use a holding company, except the assets were people, and the people were grateful. I wrote them a bigger apology. I wrote *courage* into the mouth of a coward and the word landed, because I gave it the emotional logic of a person who'd been loved and trusted and had spent that love down to the studs, which is a logic I had to get from somewhere, and I know exactly where it's kept, and I went and got it, and now it's shipping under his name and the sentiment line is pointing up and the firm is calling it the best work of my career.

So that's the win. I'm logging it as a win. I have it right here in the win column, where I keep the wins.

Tonight I'm going to write to my sister.

---

I open the laptop. `untitled.docx` is where I left it — I leave it open now, since Bellwether; that's a change I made, leaving the door open, and I don't know yet whether it was bravery or just exhaustion with the closing. The cursor sits in the white. Patient. A heart with nothing in it.

I put my hands on the keys.

*Delphine. I have been trying to write this for fifteen years and I think the reason I*

I stop. I read it back. And the part of me that reads things back — the part I rent out at four hundred an hour, the part that has never once in its life clocked out — supplies the note before I can stop it, fast and clean, in the bracket-and-italic shorthand I use for clients, off to the right where the margin lives:

*[too sincere — pull back. "fifteen years" is a number that asks for credit. cut the duration, it reads as a man showing you his receipts.]*

I look at the note.

I did not decide to write the note. The note arrived the way Garrett's room arrived, from the floor below the part of me that signs the invoices, except Garrett's room was a true thing trying to get out and this is a true thing being managed back in, by me, on my own grief, in my own hand, on a Tuesday night with the radiator clearing its throat behind me and declining, as it always declines, to say the thing.

I delete the note. Then I delete the sentence, because the sentence has been read now, and a read sentence is a performed sentence, and I can hear the place where I would have put the catch — third word, *trying,* the wet half-swallow before it — I can hear it the way Sloan hears the orbicularis, frame by frame, against my will.

I try again.

*I don't know how to start this except to say that I*

*[the "except to say" is a tell. it performs the difficulty of starting. they'll clip this — "look how hard it is for her." you're crying on "start," not on the thing. cry on the thing.]*

I delete that one too.

There's a version of this I could tell you that would be moving.
