If Ashe says it, it's his. Not because of copyright. Because of what saying does. Once eleven million people have heard a billionaire pay for those words and weep them on cue, those words are *the apology Roland Ashe gave,* forever, in the only ledger that counts, the soft animal one — and if I then drive across the river and say the same words to Delphine, in my own voice, at our mother's bed, she will hear, under them, the thing she always hears, the thing she heard the last time I crafted at her: she'll hear that I've said this before. Performed. Tested. That somewhere there's a focus group. That the catch on the word is one I've used. Because there will be. Because I will have. The truest sentence I own becomes, the instant Ashe performs it, *a sentence that has been performed* — and a sentence that has been performed is the one thing Delphine has told me, plainly, kindly, immovably, she does not want.

The sentence or the career. Not both. The sentence works exactly once, and the second use is a forgery of the first, no matter which order I do them in.

I sit with that for a while. The room is very quiet. Down the hall a junior laughs at something. The radiator here doesn't clear its throat; that's the home one; here there's just the HVAC, indifferent, corporate, devastated by nothing.

I do not write the joke that occurs to me. It occurs to me — *I finally found the one apology I can't ghost, and it's mine* — and it's good, it's tight, it's the kind of thing I'd file, and I look at it and I leave it where it is, unwritten, because I have noticed that the joke is just the spigot facing a third direction, the *away* direction, and I am trying, this once, this one morning, to face the thing.

---

Priya comes at lunch. She is the only one who could have gotten in; she has a way of being already in the room, a mercy that arrives before you've finished wanting it.

"You look," she says, "like a person who's seen the number."

"What number."

"There's always a number. The one where the math stops being abstract." She sits. She doesn't take the client chair; she takes the chair beside me, which is a thing she does, a thing I've clocked and admired and resented, the way she never positions herself across from you. "You've got the Ashe thing. The big one. I can tell because you're writing with your mouth."

I stop writing with my mouth.

"I'm not here to recruit you today," she says, which is what she says on the days she's recruiting me. "I'm here because I heard about the leak, and because I like you, in the genuinely inconvenient way, and I want to tell you the merciful thing before someone tells you the clever one."

"Tell me the merciful thing."

She does. She tells it the way she tells everything, plainly, kindly, without the tweezers, which is its own kind of weapon. The merciful thing is: let it carry. Let the machine — the Loop, the box, the thing I refuse to be afraid of and am — let it generate the Ashe statement. Feed it the brief, let it tune the contrition to optimal, let it produce flawless. It'll be good. It won't be *mine* good, she says, generous even now, but it'll be good enough, and the point is — the point, Mona — *you don't have to spend yourself on him.* You don't have to put your one true sentence in his mouth. Let the box lie. The box doesn't have a sister. The box has one liver and it's made of math and it can render in every direction at once because it has no direction, no soul facing the spigot, nothing it's saving. *Let the machine carry the lie so you don't have to.*

"And then," she says, softer, because she's good, because she sees the door her own sentence opens, "whatever you've got left — you take it across the river. You keep it. You spend it where you mean to."

It is the kindest thing anyone has said to me in a year and it is the temptation and I can feel exactly how it feels to say yes. It feels like sleep. It feels like the not-sleeping ending. It feels like setting down a thing I've carried so long my hands have grown around it. Let the box be Ashe. Keep the sentence for Delphine. Both. Have both. The machine eats the impossible choice and I walk out clean with my one true thing intact in my pocket, and I drive across the river, and I say it, finally, in my own voice—

And there it is. The flaw. The flaw is at the end, where the flaws always are, in the part of the sentence the lawyers call the future.

I say it in my own voice. *My own voice.* Which I would have spared, by letting the box carry Ashe, which means I'd arrive at Delphine's having outsourced the worst version of myself to a machine specifically so that I could show up to my sister as the woman who *didn't* — clean hands, unspent, the good draft saved for the person who deserves it. Curated. Staged. The delivery environment optimized. I'd have run the whole production after all; I'd just have run it on myself, hired a ghost for the lie so the truth could keep its hands nice for the recipient who matters.

Delphine does not want the version of me that kept its hands nice. She has been very clear. She wants the woman who did it. The one with the dirty hands. The one who can't be both the apologist and the believed, who has to choose, in front of her, with everything on it, which one she is.

If I let the box carry Ashe, I keep the sentence — but I keep it the way I've kept it for fifteen years. *Safe.* Curated for an audience. The audience just happens, this time, to be the right one. And a thing kept safe for the right audience is still a thing kept safe, still a performance, still — Delphine's word — *very good.*

"You're not going to take it," Priya says. Not disappointed. Reading me. "I can see you're not."

"No."

"Why. Tell me why, because I'd genuinely like to know, and not for the pitch." And she means it, which is the unbearable part, she actually wants to know.

I open my mouth to give her the reason, and I have it, I have it the way I have everything, ready, phrased, the small clean competent click of a thing that will land — and I hear it, in the half-second before, the way you hear a stair you know is loose, I hear that it's *good,* that it's a sentence I'd be a little proud of, and I understand that if I say it well it won't be why I'm not taking the mercy, it'll be one more place I went instead of the thing.

So I don't say the good version. I say the bad one. I say, "Because then I still didn't."

Priya waits.

"Still didn't what," she says, gently, the way Delphine would, the way the dying are talked to.

"Do it." It comes out flat and short and a little stupid, missing the word it needs, and I let it be missing. "The — the thing. Myself. With the hands that did the rest of it. If the box carries the bad one so I can carry the good one across nice and clean, then I've still got nice hands, and the whole — the whole problem was always the hands." I stop. That's not a sentence. I leave it not a sentence. "I can't get there with clean hands. I have to take the dirty ones."

It is the least fluent thing I have said to another adult in years, and Priya looks at me for a long moment, and there's something in her face I don't have a word for, which never happens, and she says, "Okay," and she stands, and on her way out she touches my shoulder once, the way you touch a person you've decided to stop selling to, and she says, "For what it's worth, the box couldn't have written what you just said. Not the sentence. The not-being-able-to-finish it." And she's gone, and I sit there having been, for once, correctly read by exactly the wrong instrument.

---

So now I know the arithmetic and I've refused the cheat, and I have, on the screen in front of me, the masterpiece. Finished. Perfect. The catch in the right place, the agent back in the chair, the acknowledgement that's always defective made flawless for the one man on earth who deserves it least. Renata is expecting it by end of day. The firm is saved if I send it. The leak is neutralized if I send it — who cares who wrote the saint when the saint is this good. Partner if I send it. Vindication. The wing of the hospital with my name on it, finally, in invisible ink only I can read.

I select it. The whole thing. Highlighted blue, the way I select everything before I delete it, the senator, the love variants, *I'm devastated.* My finger's on the key.

I don't delete it.

I don't send it either.

I do a third thing, a thing I have never done with a document in my life, a thing the trade has no product for and no name for and no margin note that fits, and I notice as I do it that there is no margin note, that I've stopped annotating, that somewhere in the last hour the part of me that leaves notes in the margin went quiet the way a noise you've heard so long you only notice when it stops. I take the masterpiece — the one true sentence, the only one I own, the thing that could be Ashe's salvation or Delphine's fact and can only ever be one — and I move it. Out of the Ashe file. Out of the firm's drive. Off the network entirely, onto the thing in my pocket, into the folder with no name, the one I keep for the true things people paid me to delete.

The folder of orphaned sincerities.

It has hundreds in it. Everyone's. The senator's girl. The actress who knew. Garrett's room. Every true line a client ever flinched from, lifted to safety, filed where I look at them the way you look at photographs of a country you can't get a visa back to.

And now there's mine. The one line I could never write. It's in there. Whole. Perfect. True. Unspent. The masterpiece I will not ship and have not said, sitting in the dark with all the other things nobody could afford to mean, and I understand, closing the laptop, that I have not solved anything, I have only done the one thing left that isn't a forgery — I have refused to spend my soul on the man who'd pay the most for it, and I have not yet found the man, the woman, the bed, the room, the dirty-handed un-staged unbearable place where I could spend it on the person it's actually for.

I have the sentence. I am holding the sentence. I have torched nothing yet and saved nothing yet; I have only, for one morning, declined to lie with my best material.

Renata's going to want the document at five.

The wedding is in eleven days. Our mother has, Delphine says, less than that, more than that, nobody knows, the window is mornings.

I put my coat on. There's a thing I have to go not-do, in person, across a river, with the sentence I can't say burning a hole in a folder no one can know I keep, and the only thing I'm sure of, standing up, is that I will not be allowed to keep it safe much longer, because a sentence kept safe is just the fifteenth year of the same crime, and I am, finally, almost out of years to commit it in.


---

# Chapter 24 — Two Weeks

Act II  ·  Fluency gauge: 3/10  ·  Artifact: NO

---

Here is the schedule.

The wedding is on a Saturday. Delphine and Theo, a church hall with a kitchen, a carpenter and a woman who sits with the dying, sixty chairs, a sheet cake. I have the date in three places because I put it in three places, which is a thing I do with dates that matter, the way I triple-confirm a ship day, except this one I am not running. I am a guest. There is a word for what I am that I keep reaching for and it is *guest* and it keeps not feeling like the word.

Ada is the other date, and Ada's date is not a date. Ada's date is a window, and the window is closing the way windows close in a house no one heats anymore, slowly and then all at once and you only notice when you go to open it.

The doctor said weeks. The hospice nurse, who is more honest than the doctor because she is paid to be present and not to be right, said it could be the wedding weekend. She said it without weight, the way Delphine says things, a fact handed across a counter. *It could be that weekend. These things don't read a calendar.*

So that is the schedule. The wedding on Saturday and our mother dying inside the same two weeks, the two events leaning toward each other across the days like two people about to collide in a corridor, both apologizing, neither stopping.

I have spent fifteen years managing the calendars of guilty men. I have moved a confession off a Friday because Fridays die in the weekend news cycle and I needed it to bleed all week. I have held a senator's apology eleven days to clear a holiday. I am, among the small grim things I am genuinely the best at, a person who can find the optimal day for a sorry. And I sit at my own table with my own two dates and there is no optimal day. There is no version where I am in both rooms. The river runs between them and I cannot stand on both banks.

I catch myself, the first night, opening a blank calendar block. Actually doing it. Dragging the cursor down the Saturday to make it a meeting, *Wedding*, color-coding it, and then the cursor hovering over the following week with nothing to call it, because you cannot color-code a deathbed, there is no category, the drop-down does not have it. I sit there with the drop-down open. *Busy. Free. Tentative. Out of office.* I close the laptop. That is the whole joke. I am too tired to even finish it.

Renata calls Wednesday about the Ashe ship date. She wants it locked. The firm wants it the same week as the wedding, because the news will be busy with other things and a busy week is a soft week and a soft week is where you bury the thing you can't afford a second look at. She does not know about the wedding. She does not know about the bed across the river. She has a Gantt chart and I am a resource on it, and she is right, professionally she is completely right, that week is the week, and I hear myself say *let me look at the calendar* in the voice I use for clients, the calm voice, the voice that has never once been late, and I look at the calendar, and on the calendar is a Saturday I can't run and a window I can't close and a sentence I can spend exactly once, and Renata waits on the line, and I tell her I'll get back to her, which I have never said to Renata in my life. There is a silence. She files it. I can hear her file it.

---

I go to the hospice on a Tuesday.

I have arranged, for fifteen years, not to think about where she was. I assumed a tense and lived in it. So I have to be told the address, and I write it on the back of a receipt like a delivery, and I drive across the river in the middle of the afternoon when the light is doing nothing, and the building is low and brick and could be a dentist's, could be a place you renew a license, with a sign out front so soft and beige it apologizes for the word *hospice* before you reach it.

The smell is the first thing. Not the smell I keep waiting for — not cut grass, not the casserole, not the trapdoor. A different smell, the real one. Antiseptic over something underneath it that the antiseptic is losing to. Warm food no one wants. Lilies, somewhere, doing their job. I stand in the lobby and I understand that this is the smell my whole career is a deodorant for. This is the room. This is the actual room the firm sells the *feeling* of having visited. We make people the kind of person it happened to. Here is the thing it happens to. It is beige and it has a fish tank.

A volunteer points me down a hall. I do not go down the hall. I stand at the mouth of it and I look at the number on a door, 14, the same number as everything lately, and behind that door is the only witness in the world to the thing I said, the one person who can tell me whether the sentence I built fifteen years ago out of jealousy and small bottles and a graveside was a true sentence or a thing I made up and then made come true by saying it, and I cannot make my feet move.

I am very good at rooms. I walk into rooms full of frightened powerful people and I take the room. It is the thing I do. And I stand outside this one and I am not anything. I am a woman in a coat who drove across a river.

Delphine finds me there. Of course she does. It is her building in the way the church hall is her building — not hers, but the place she is most herself, the place her competence lives. She comes out of another room entirely, drying her hands on a cloth, and she sees me standing at the mouth of the hall like a coat someone left, and she does not act surprised, and she does not act glad, and she does not perform anything at all, which is the thing about her I cannot get over, the thing that beats me every time.

"You came," she says. Not a question. Not warm, not cold. A fact handed across the counter.

"I came." I can hear that I have nothing to put after it. I, who always have the thing to put after it. There is a silence where the second clause should be and I leave it there, naked, and it is the most honest thing I have done in the building so far.

"She's not great today," Delphine says. "She's in and out. Mostly out, since lunch." She folds the cloth. She has good hands, my sister, capable hands, hands that have closed a lot of eyes. "You don't have to go in. People think they have to go in. You don't."

"Do you want me to go in?"

She looks at me for a second longer than the question deserves. "I'm not going to manage that for you," she says, and there it is, gentle as anything, the small blade. She knows exactly what I came to do. I came to have her run the scene. I came to be told where to stand, on which word to break, whether to do it in the room or the hall — I came to outsource the decision the way I outsource everything, and she will not take the brief. She has never once taken the brief. It is the only thing she wants from me and it is the thing I keep trying to hand back.

---

I go in.

The room is small and the bed is large in it and our mother is small in the bed, which is the arithmetic of these rooms, everything getting smaller except the bed. There is a chair. There is a window with the blind half-down and the afternoon light lying across the floor like it got tired on the way to the bed and stopped. There is a machine doing a quiet thing, and a cup with a sponge on a stick, and a card from someone, and the smell, the real one, closer now.

She does not look like the photographs. She does not look like the funeral, which I keep forgetting was not her funeral. She looks like what is left when a face has been a face for eighty years and is now mostly bone deciding what to do. Her eyes are open. They are open at the ceiling. They are not at me.

I sit in the chair because the chair is there and sitting is a thing I know how to do.

"Mom," I say.

Nothing. The machine. The light.

I have a thing I came to ask. I have, God help me, rehearsed it, the way I rehearse a client into a microphone, *was it true, did you ever, did you want her,* and I sat in the car and worked the phrasing because that is what my hands do, they work the phrasing, and the whole drive over I was building the question to be answerable, building it the way I build everything, with a soft entry and a clear ask and a half-beat where the conscience reads, and I get into the chair next to the actual dying actual woman and the whole apparatus is just — gone. It is not that I won't use it. It is that there is nothing here for it to grip. You cannot strategize a person who is not in the room. There is no audience. There is no dial. There is a ceiling she is looking at and a question that no longer has anywhere to land.

So I just say it. Plainly. Worse than I have ever said anything, which is, I am beginning to understand, the only register that counts.

"Did you want her," I say. "Delphine. Did you. I need to know if you wanted her."

Her eyes come down off the ceiling. Slowly. They find me. For a second — for one second — there is somebody in them, somebody who knows the room and knows the chair and maybe knows the face in the chair, and her mouth works, and I lean in, I lean all the way in, the way I would never let a client lean, too eager, no control, and I am closer to a true answer than I have been in fifteen years —

"Is it Tuesday," she says.

"Yes," I say. "It's Tuesday."

"I have the thing," she says. "On Tuesday. I have the thing." And her eyes go back up to the ceiling, to whatever Tuesday she is in, which is not this one, and the somebody is gone out of them like a light under a door going off, and I am alone in the chair with the machine and the light and the only witness in the world, and she has the thing, on Tuesday, and there is no thing, and I will not get my answer here. I can feel that I will not. Not today and not, I think, ever. The window is open and there is no one at it.

I sit with her anyway. I don't know why. I have nothing to manage and no one to perform for and the most useless I have ever been, and I sit in the chair and I hold the hand that is on the blanket, the small dry hand, and I don't say anything else, because there is nothing in my entire trained life that fits in this room, and for once I have the sense to not make it fit. The light moves a little. The machine does its quiet thing. My mother is somewhere on a Tuesday I can't reach, and she may have wanted Delphine, and she may not have, and she is taking the answer with her, slowly and then all at once.

---

Delphine is in the hall when I come out. She is with a family. Not ours. Someone else's. A woman my age and an older man and a teenager who is doing the thing teenagers do in these halls, which is to be too big for the chair and looking at a phone they are not seeing. Delphine is crouched in front of the woman my age, down at her level, holding nothing, fixing nothing, just there, her face open the way Dev's face is open, the way I cut out of every draft, and she is saying something I can't hear, and whatever it is, it is the real version of the thing I sell.

I stand at the end of the hall and I watch my sister do, for no money, in a beige corridor that smells of lilies and the losing thing underneath, the exact work I have spent a fortune learning to fake. Presence at the unfixable. No script. Nothing to take, no comeback to pre-sell, no second helping. She is not making the woman the kind of person it happened to. She is just sitting with the person it happened to, which it turns out is a different job, an entirely different job, and the one I never learned.

I watch her hands, because I always watch hands, it is the first thing Sloan taught me, the hands tell you before the face does. The woman my age has hands that won't stay still — picking at the cuff, the wedding ring, the cuff — and Delphine's hands do not reach in to stop them, do not pat, do not perform the comfort. Delphine's hands stay where they are, open, on her own knees, and they just wait, and somehow the waiting is the thing, the not-reaching is the thing, and after a while the woman's hands slow down on their own. I could not have coached that. I would have reached. I would have put a hand on the hand. I would have given her the involuntary signal of comfort, the tweezered version, and it would have landed and it would have been a lie, and Delphine gives her nothing and it is the only true thing in the hall. She lets the woman be the one feeling something. She doesn't take it for herself. I have built a career on taking it for the company, the candle in the company's hand, and my sister sits in a beige hall and lets the grief belong to the person it belongs to, and that, I understand, standing there, is the whole difference, that is the nine points, that is the river. She does not borrow the feeling. She has never once needed to be the saddest one in the room.

She comes back to me after. The woman my age has gone in to a different room, a different 14.

"That doesn't get easier," I say, which is a thing people say, a placeholder, and I hear myself reach for the next clause, the one that would make it land, and I don't say it. I leave it bad.

"It's not supposed to get easier," Delphine says. "That's the whole job. If it got easier I'd be doing it wrong."

We stand there. There is a fish tank between us doing nothing.

"The wedding's Saturday," I say.

"The wedding's Saturday."

"And she might —"

"She might." Delphine looks at the door of 14, our 14. "She might during the toasts. She might next month. She doesn't get a vote and neither do we and that's been true the whole time, Mona, it's just that now you're standing close enough to see it." She says it without heat. She has had fifteen years to get the heat out of it. "You came over here thinking you had to choose. The wedding or this. Like there's a right room."

"Isn't there."

"No," she says. "There's just where you are." And she puts a hand on my arm, brief, steadying, the same hand she gave me the first day across the river, the hand that closes eyes, and she goes back to her work, because the work does not stop because I drove over, the work does not stop for anything, that's the work.

---

I drive home in the dark with the radio off.

Here is what I have, laid out, the way I lay out an account. I have a sentence I owe and one chance to spend it. I have a man — Ashe — who would pay me a career to put it in his mouth, and a firm holding its breath, and a leak somewhere with my name in it, and a partnership on the far side of shipping a thing I cannot, it turns out, ship. I have a wedding on Saturday and a deathbed in the same fortnight and one body, mine, that can only be in one room. I have a sister who will not run the scene. And I have a mother who is the only person alive who knows whether the thing I said was true, and she is leaving without telling me, on a Tuesday that isn't this one, and I am going to have to do the impossible arithmetic of taking back a sentence without ever knowing whether it was a lie.

That is the part I keep arriving at and driving past. Not *I have to apologize.* Not even *I have to recant.* It's worse and it's smaller. I have to tell my sister whether it was true, and the honest answer — the only true answer I have, the one I have been circling for fifteen years and have never once let myself land on — is that I don't know. I made it up. I made it up out of a few real cold afternoons and a great deal of being twenty-six, and then I said it out loud over a grave, and the saying made it real, the saying always makes it real, that is my entire profession, I have known it my whole career and never once turned it on myself. I said *Mom never wanted you* and the moment it was in the air it became a thing the world contained, and now the one person who could weigh it is on a Tuesday I can't reach, and I am going to have to hand Delphine, in my own voice, the worst sentence I have ever built, which is no sentence at all. *I don't know. I don't know if it was true.* No structure. No half-beat. No place to put the catch. A thing with a hole where the answer goes.

I am the best in the country at saying things well. I make my living standing in the gap between the word that works and the word that's true and selling people the trip they never take. And the one thing I have left to do, the only thing, is to stand in front of my sister with no dial and no swatch-book and no firm and no script, and say the unmanageable thing badly enough that, for the first time in my life, she might believe me.

I get home. I do not open the laptop. There is nothing on it that helps. The document I cannot title is still open from last week, the cursor blinking in the white where a word should be, patient, a heart with nothing in it yet, and I look at it across the room and I understand, finally and completely, that the answer was never going to be on it. You cannot write this one. A written one is a performed one; she will hear the page in it the way she hears everything. It has to be said, out loud, in a room, badly, with my whole stupid face, the one Sloan could never code, doing the thing it does when it finally tells the truth, whatever that is — I have never seen it.

The radiator clears its throat.

For once, so do I.

Saturday is four days out. The window is open across the river and there is no one at it. I have one sentence and two rooms and no idea, none, which one I'll be standing in when I finally say it worse than I have ever said anything.

I turn off the light. The cursor goes on blinking in the dark, in the white, where a word should be, where a word is finally, after fifteen years, going to have to go.


---

# Chapter 25 — The Decoy

Act III  ·  Fluency gauge: 2.5/10  ·  Artifact: NO

---

The church hall has a low ceiling and a smell of floor wax and warming chafing-dish chicken, and there is a banner on the wall, a real banner, made by a person with a glue stick, that says THEO & DELPHINE in letters cut from the kind of metallic card stock that comes in a craft-store multipack, and the letters are not evenly spaced. The P-H of THEO has gone slightly downhill. Someone loved him enough to make that banner and not enough to measure it, and I stand inside the door in the coat I bought for this and I look at the downhill letters and I have to put a finger to the corner of my eye, briefly, the way you press a bruise to make sure it's still there.

I attend weddings the way I attend everything, which is professionally. I clock the room. I cannot help it; it is the first thing Sloan taught me and the last thing I'll forget. Sixty folding chairs in rows that don't quite line up. A table of foil trays a church group brought. A man at a keyboard who is, I would bet money, a colleague of Theo's, doing something gentle and unrehearsed to a hymn. No step-and-repeat. No photographer with a lanyard. No one is managing this. I keep waiting to find the person managing it and there is no person, there is just a roomful of people who came, and the absence of management is so total that it reads, to me, as a kind of nakedness, the way a stage reads naked with the work lights up.

This is the room my entire career is a forgery of. I make people the kind of person it happened *to*. Here are the people it is happening *with*. There is no nine-point difference between the two; there is a river.

Theo is up front in a suit that fits him the way a suit fits a man who owns one suit. He is broad and plain and his hands, when he turns them over, have the cracked-knuckle look of a man who works with them, the stairs-that-hold look, and he keeps touching the breast pocket of the suit like there's something in it he's checking on, and the something is, I will learn later, the ring, which he has been told three times to give to the best man and has each time decided to keep on himself, because he does not trust the system, because he is a carpenter and he trusts the thing he can put his own hand on. I like him before he says a word. I had not planned to. There was no line item for it.

I take a chair on the end of the third row, on the aisle, which is the seat you take when you want a clear path to a door, and I tell myself it is for that, and it is not for that. It is so I can see her come in.

---

She comes in.

I have been to a great many produced entrances. I have *staged* entrances; I once held a disgraced governor in a stairwell for ninety seconds so his walk to a podium would coincide with a key change a junior had married to the live feed. I know what an entrance is built to do and I know the muscles that do it. And Delphine comes up the not-quite-straight aisle of a church hall in a dress that is just a good dress, on the arm of no one, because the father who would have walked her is the one whose funeral I poisoned, and she comes the way she does everything, without weight, a fact handed up the aisle, and the room turns to her and she lets it, she does not perform being looked at, she just lets it happen to her the way she lets grief happen to the people in her corridor, and it is the most powerful entrance I have ever seen, and there is not one engineered second in it.

I think: *I could not have built that.* I think it the way you'd think *I could not have built that mountain.* It is not modesty. It is arithmetic.

The vows are short. Theo's are written on an index card he reads from and loses his place on twice, and the second time he loses it the whole hall laughs with him, not at him, and he laughs too, and finds the place, and finishes, and the thing he finishes on is not clever. It is *I'll be where you are.* That's the whole vow. *I'll be where you are.* He says it badly, with a catch in it he did not put there and could not have put there, a catch that arrives in the wrong place, on *be*, which is a terrible place for it, no one would coach a catch onto *be*, and the hall makes the sound a room makes when something true has been said clumsily by a person who can't say it any other way.

I sit in the third row with my career in my lap and I think, with the part of me that will apparently be the last part to die: *that. I have spent fifteen years and a fortune learning to manufacture exactly that, and he did it for free, by accident, because he couldn't do it on purpose, and the fact that he couldn't do it on purpose is the entire reason it worked.*

I have known the thesis of my whole life for a long time. I have said it to clients. I have written it on the inside of my own skull. I have never once, until a carpenter caught his voice on the wrong word in a church hall, felt it land in my body like a true sentence, with the heavy click, the way Garrett's room landed, the way the real ones land.

Delphine sees me from the front. Of course she does. She finds me in the third row the way she found me at the mouth of the hospice hall, with no surprise and no warmth and no performance, just located, the way you locate a coat you left somewhere. And she does not nod, and she does not not-nod. She just knows where I am now. She files it. I can see her file it. It is a thing we both do.

---

There is a part of the afternoon for cake.

The hall is loud with it — the church-group women circulating with the foil trays, the keyboard man playing something with a beat in it now, the teenager from the hospice hall, the one who is somebody's, doing the dance teenagers do which is mostly not-dancing while watching to see if dancing is safe. Theo is being hugged by enormous men. There are no toasts scheduled, I have checked, I checked twice, there is no program, which means there is no slot, which means if I am going to do the thing I came to do I will have to make the slot myself, off the cuff, in a room with no microphone, the way I have spent my life teaching men to *avoid* doing.

And I am going to do the thing I came to do, because I am out of road. The sentence is still in me, unspent, fifteen years unspent, and Ashe is unshipped on my desk and a partnership is bleeding out behind me and there is a leak somewhere with my name in it and a window across the river that will not stay open, and I have come to the last room in which it can possibly be said before the only witness leaves, and I have, God help me, *prepared.*

I want to be honest about this, because honesty is the only thing I have left that's worth anything and I'm about to spend the rest of it badly. I prepared. In the car I prepared. I have been preparing, if I'm being exact, and I am always exact, for two weeks. I have a thing to say to her and it is *good*. It is the best thing I have ever written and I wrote it for the one person I have never been able to write for, and I know — I knew in the car, I knew at the hospice, I knew at twenty-six — that you cannot prepare this, that the preparing is the disease, that Brennan told me to say it worse and Delphine told me she wants the fact not the feeling and the kid told me you don't get to know in advance. I know all of it. I have known all of it for the entire book of my life. And I have a thing to say to her and it is good and I am going to say it anyway, because it is the only thing I have, because when the room is naked the one of us with clothes on reaches for them, because a person drowning does not invent a new stroke, she does the stroke she knows even when the stroke she knows is the thing pulling her down.

I find her by the kitchen pass-through, between trays, in the half-minute she has to herself, which is the only kind of minute she ever has, and I have timed it, of course I've timed it, I time everything, and she sees me coming and she does not move away and she does not arrange her face and she sets down the tray and she waits, and I stand in front of my sister on the afternoon of her wedding and I open my mouth and I give her the best apology I have ever made.

---

I will not write it all down. I'll write enough.

It is plain on the surface and engineered all the way down. I have stripped it of every word that signals craft — there is no *devastated*, there is no *accountability*, there is not one of the trade's defibrillator nouns in it — and the stripping is itself the craft, the most expensive kind, the kind that costs the most because it has to hide that it cost anything. I begin not with sorry. I begin with her father, because the wound began at his grave and a thing that begins at a grave has to be picked up there, and I name him, I use his actual name, which I have never once said aloud to her, and I watch the name land and I keep going. I tell her I was twenty-six and drunk and the most certain I have ever been, and that the certainty was the lie under the lie. I tell her I took a thing I half-knew and made it whole because whole is easier to throw. I do the hard middle, the one I rehearsed forty times, where I say I have spent fifteen years getting very good at saying true-sounding things to strangers specifically so I would never have to say one true thing to her, and I let my voice do the thing on *strangers* that I would have coached onto *strangers*, the small drop, the half-swallow, because I cannot not, because it is not a thing I'm doing anymore, it is a thing I am.

And I land it. I land it perfectly. I bring it home to a close so quiet and so plain and so apparently artless that I feel, even as I say it, the awful familiar pleasure of a sentence built to look unbuilt, a sentence that took two weeks to make sound like it took no time at all, and I say the last word and I stop, and I let the silence do its work, because the silence is part of the work, the silence is the most worked part of the whole thing.

She is quiet for a moment.

I have, in fifteen years, watched a great many people receive a great many apologies. I know the faces. I know the face that's softening and the face that's deciding and the face that's already forgiven and just waiting for the polite interval before it says so. I lean in, the way I would never let a client lean, and I read my sister's face for the involuntary signatures, the wet eye that doesn't spill, the give around the mouth, because I have just done the best work of my life and I am, God help me, already feeling the high come up under me, the high I felt over Garrett, the clean-job high, the *it always works* —

And her face is not doing any of those things.

Her face is doing the thing it did across the counter at the hospice. It is just present. It is just looking at me. There is something in it, but it is not softening and it is not deciding and it is not the polite interval. It is something closer to — and I hate this, I hate that the word my mind reaches for is the trade word — *recognition*. She has recognized something. She has, I understand, with a cold drop that starts at the back of my neck, *heard* something. Not the apology. Under the apology. She has heard the thing I spent two weeks burying so deep I'd half-convinced myself it wasn't there.

"Mona," she says. Gently. So gently. "That was beautiful."

And the floor does not drop, the way the style guide of my soul has always promised the floor would drop, because some part of me knew it was coming, has known since I started talking, has known since the car. The floor doesn't drop. It just is not there. It turns out it had not been there for some time.

"You said it perfectly," she says. "You said his name. I felt you say his name." Her eyes are bright and they are dry, my sister has the driest brightest eyes in the world, eyes that have watched a hundred people leave and not once borrowed their leaving. "I felt the whole thing coming. I felt it the way I always feel it with you — three sentences out, four, I can feel the shape of where it's going, I can feel the place where your voice is going to do the thing before it does it. You did the thing on *strangers*. I heard you decide to do it on *strangers* and then I heard you do it. It was —" she almost laughs, and the almost is the worst part, the almost is the kindest cruelest thing anyone has ever done to me "— Mona, it was the best one yet. You've gotten so good. You've gotten so good I could die."

"Delphine —"

"I'm not being cruel." She isn't. That's the thing. There is no cruelty anywhere in the room; I would know; cruelty is a thing I can detect at forty feet, I've sold it wholesale. There is only her, plain and bright and married, in a good dress, holding a tray she set down, telling me the truth I have spent my life insuring people against. "I'm telling you it was beautiful and I'm telling you I didn't ask you for a beautiful one. I've never once asked you for a beautiful one." She tilts her head, and the tilt is the carpenter's tilt, the looking-at-a-thing-that-isn't-level tilt. "I asked you a question. I've been asking you the same question for fifteen years. And you just answered it with the most gorgeous speech I've ever heard, and Mona — listen to me — a speech is not an answer. You can hear that, can't you? Of all the people alive, you're the one who can hear that."

---

Here is the part I did not prepare for, because you cannot prepare for it, because preparing is the disease.

I have a follow-up. I am the best follow-up in the country. When the first apology doesn't land you don't repeat it louder, you find the seam it missed and you go in through the seam, and I am already, even now, even gutted, even with the floor confirmed absent under me, *finding the seam* — I can feel my mind do it, the horrible competence of it, the way a severed limb is said to keep twitching, my mind reaching for the version that would acknowledge her point, that would fold *I know a speech isn't an answer* into the speech, that would weaponize the very fact of my failure into a more moving failure, *you're right, and the fact that I can't stop being good at this even now is the most honest thing about me*, oh, that's good, that's so good, I can see it land, I can see her give —

and I open my mouth to say it,

and I look at her face, the dry bright present face of the one person in the world who will hear the seam-work as seam-work, who will feel the next sentence coming three words out, who will watch me decide to be moving about being unable to be moving and will love me too much and trust me too little to be moved, and I understand, fully, finally, in a church hall under a downhill banner, that there is no version of this I can win, because every version is a version, because the moment I make it good I make it false, because she is the one audience on earth that cannot be sold and I have brought my entire inventory to the one customer who isn't buying, and I will keep producing, I will produce until I die, the spigot does not have an off valve, I learned that at the midpoint, the reaching is the me —

and I close my mouth.

I don't say the follow-up.

It is the hardest thing I have done in the entire hall, harder than the name, harder than the speech, to stand in front of her with a perfect sentence loaded and *not fire it*, to let the silence be a real silence and not a worked one, to let my failure just sit there being a failure and not improve it. My hands want to move. My voice wants to do the small drop. I hold still. I hold still the way Delphine held still in the corridor, hands open on her own knees, not reaching, and I do not reach, and it is nothing like when she does it, it is clumsy and effortful and visible, you could see the not-reaching from the back row, but I do it, I manage, barely, to do the one thing my whole life is built to never do, which is nothing.

"There she is," Delphine says, very quietly. Almost to herself.

And she picks up the tray.

---

That's it. That's the scene I came for. That's the cinematic reckoning, the wedding confrontation, the thing the whole story has been leaning toward — and it is the wrong scene, I understand that now, standing in the noise with the cake going around, it was always going to be the wrong scene, you cannot say the true thing on a stage, the stage is the problem, the stage is what I am, and she knew it, she has known it for fifteen years, she did not invite me here for this. She is at her own wedding. She came back to her own wedding.

I stand by the pass-through a while. No one needs me. There is no one to manage. The teenager has decided dancing is safe and is doing it now, badly, gloriously, and Theo is dancing with an old woman who must be an aunt, holding her like she's made of stairs that won't hold, and the keyboard man has found a song everyone knows, and the room is doing the thing rooms do when no one is running them, which is the thing I have never once been able to make a room do, which is be happy without being told to.

I find my coat.

Outside the light is going. There is a phone in my coat and the phone has done a thing while I was inside, the small accumulating thing phones do, and I take it out on the church steps to call the car, and there is a text on it from a number I have under HOSPICE because I could not make myself put a name to it, and the text is from the nurse, the honest one, the one paid to be present and not right, and the text is four words, and they are not complicated words, a child has all four, and I stand on the steps of the hall where my sister just married a carpenter and I read the four words that the nurse has sent me, which are:

*Come now if coming.*

---

I do not go back in.

I stand on the steps and behind me the hall is warm and loud and full of the only true thing I have seen in years, my sister married, the banner downhill, the catch on *be*, and across the river there is a window closing in a house no one heats, and the only witness in the world to the only question that matters is, the nurse is telling me in four plain words she did not have to make sound like anything, leaving.

I have a sentence I owe and one chance to spend it. I have a man on my desk who would buy it for a career. I have a sister thirty feet behind me who heard me spend two weeks of craft and felt the shape of it three sentences out and set down a tray and picked it back up. And I have a mother on the far side of a river who is, right now, while a keyboard plays a song everyone knows, doing the thing, finally, the actual thing, not on a Tuesday, on a Saturday, on this Saturday, and she is the only one who knows whether what I said at the graveside was true, and she is taking it with her, and I am out here on the steps in my coat with the best speech of my life dying unused in my mouth, and I cannot, it turns out, be in both rooms.

I look at the phone. *Come now if coming.*

I look back at the hall door, where the warm noise is.

There is no optimal day. There never was. There is just where you are.

I go down the steps toward the river, toward the room where there is nothing left to say well, where I will have to say the unmanageable thing or not say it at all, and the speech goes on dying in my mouth the whole way down, a beautiful thing, the best I ever built, useless as a key to a house that's already on fire, and for once — for the first time in my trained and fluent and forfeited life — I let it die without trying to save it.


---

# Chapter 26 — Kill the Account

Act III  ·  Fluency gauge: 2/10  ·  Artifact: NO

---

The car comes. I get in it. I tell the driver the firm, not the hospice, and I hear myself do it, and I do not correct it.

I want to be clear about why, because I am about to do a stupid thing and I would like the record to show I did it on purpose. The masterpiece is on my desk. It is finished. It has been finished since Tuesday, sitting in the orphaned-sincerities folder where I put it, where it should not be, because it is not orphaned, it has a home, the home is a billionaire's mouth and a Friday deadline and the rest of my life. Renata wants it by five. It is four-forty. The wedding is behind me and the hospice is across the river and the thing that stands between those two places, the thing I have to walk through to get from the one to the other, is one click.

So I am going to go click it. That's all. I am going to the office to send a document.

That's the lie I take across the bridge.

---

The floor is empty the way a floor is empty on a Saturday evening when a deadline lives on it. Two juniors at the far end, lamps on, headphones, the blue of their screens. The carpet smells of nothing now; I have stopped being able to smell it; that is a thing that happens, Sloan told me once, the nose gives up on a constant. The reading room is dark. The whiteboard in the room has Friday's session still on it, half-erased, a ghost of arrows, and somebody's word — *contrition* — with a box drawn around it and a question mark, and I look at the box and the question mark for a while, longer than I mean to, the way you look at a word in a language you used to be fluent in.

I sit down at my desk and I open the folder and I open the file.

I am not going to write the file out here. I wrote it in a trance, the way I wrote Garrett's room, and like Garrett's room it is the truest thing I have made and it is for the wrong person. It is the best thing I have ever done. I knew that when I finished it. I know it now. It would work. I can tell you it would work the way I can tell you the sun comes up; there is no part of me that doubts it; the doubt was burned out of me years ago, it was the first thing the job took.

Here is what shipping it does. It crowns me. It buries Layer 3 so deep no one ever finds the bottom, because there is no bottom, the bottom is paint all the way down and my paragraph is the last coat. It neutralizes the leak — once the apology lands, once the public exhales, the question of *who wrote it* stops being a scandal and becomes a footnote nobody clicks. It delivers Ashe redeemed, which delivers the partnership, which delivers the one thing I have wanted since I was twenty-six and starving and learned I could make a stranger believe a stranger had a soul. The whole want. All of it. Mine, finally. One click.

I put the cursor on the button.

I want to tell you a feeling arrived here, a large one, a cinematic one, the floor dropping, the music. It didn't. What arrived was small and procedural and it is the only kind of true thing I have left. What arrived was: *if he says it, it's gone.*

That's the whole of it. That's the entire mechanism. If Roland Ashe stands up and says my sentence — and he will say it well, I built it to survive even him — then it becomes the apology everyone heard a billionaire pay for. It trends. It gets clipped. It gets stitched and dueted and read aloud on morning shows by people deciding, sincerely, *you can tell he means it.* And then it is used up. A sentence can only be the first true thing once. After that it's a quote. I could never say it to Delphine after that. She'd hear it coming from a mile off, because she'd have already heard it, the world would have heard it, it would arrive at her secondhand and laundered and worth nothing, the most expensive worthless thing I ever made.

I have one true sentence. I have been carrying it for fifteen years without being able to set it down. And I can spend it once.

I am not going to spend it on him.

I take the cursor off the button.

That's it. That's the whole turn. It is not dramatic. It is a woman moving a cursor four centimeters to the left, in an empty office, on a Saturday, and then sitting very still.

---

Renata is in the building. Of course she is. She lives on ship days the way I do; it is the one thing we have ever fully shared. She comes down the dark floor with her coat already on, which means she came in to check the send and leave, and she stops at my desk and looks at the screen, which is open to the file, unsent, the little word UNSENT in the corner where the timestamp should be, and she looks at it and then she looks at me.

"It's four-fifty," she says.

"I know."

"It's done. I can see it's done." She has read it; she read it Tuesday; she came down here tonight to watch me press a button and she was looking forward to it, I can see that too, Renata enjoys a clean send the way other people enjoy a sunset. "It's the best thing you've ever written. Mona. It might be the best thing anyone in this building has ever written. Send it."

"No."

She doesn't react. That's the thing about Renata; she has seen everyone in this trade break in every direction; nothing a person does in a crisis surprises her, she has a price for all of it. She sets her bag down on the next desk. She is recalibrating. I can watch her do it, the way I can watch myself do it, the horrible competence, the *what is this actually*, the *what does she want, what's the ask under the ask*.

"You want more," she says. "Fine. You held the whale, you carried it, the firm flinched and you didn't. You want partner. I was always going to give you—"

"I don't want partner."

"Everyone wants partner."

"I know," I say. "I did too. I wanted it more than you know how to want anything. That's not it."

She waits. She is good at waiting; she taught me to wait; the wait is part of the work. And I have nothing to fill it with, which is new, which is the whole of what is new, I am sitting in front of the most dangerous reader of intentions I know and I have no intention to manage, no seam to work, no version. I just have the thing, and the thing is not for her.

"I can't send it," I say. "It's not his."

"He's paying for it. That makes it his. That's what paying is."

"It's not his," I say again, and I hear how that sounds, I hear it land in the room with no weight, no spin, a sentence a child could say, *it's not his*, and I don't dress it. I used to be able to make a sentence like that carry a cathedral. I make it carry nothing. I let it sit there being four words.

Renata looks at me for a long moment. Then she does a thing I will think about for the rest of my life. She picks her bag back up.

"You understand what this is," she says. Not unkindly. "If you don't send this, the apology doesn't land, the leak breaks, you're the woman who wrote Roland Ashe, and Roland Ashe doesn't get redeemed, which means Roland Ashe gets *exposed*, which means the firm that touched him—" she gestures, a small economical gesture that takes in the dark floor, the juniors, the box around *contrition* "—we don't survive that clean. You're not just quitting, Mona. You're a liability we now have to apologize for. We're going to have to do to you what you do to them."

"I know."

"You'll be the client."

"I know what I'll be."

And here is the only joke in the whole chapter, and it isn't mine, and it isn't really a joke. Renata stands there with her bag and her coat and her perfect murderous patience and she says, "I'll write you a good one."

I think she means it. I think it's the kindest thing she has ever said to me. *I'll write you a good one.* The woman who would sell her own funeral as a brand moment, offering to make me the kind of person it happened *to.*

"Don't," I say. "Make it bad."

She doesn't understand that. Why would she. Nobody in this building has ever asked for it bad. She frowns, the small frown of a craftsman handed an instruction that violates the craft, and then she decides it's grief, or shock, or some chemical thing that'll pass by Monday, and she files it, and she leaves. Her heels on the carpet, then the harder sound at the elevator, then nothing.

I sit at the desk that isn't mine anymore. The juniors haven't looked up. The word in the corner still says UNSENT.

---

Babs is in the kitchen.

I don't know how long she's been there. She's eating almonds out of her palm, one at a time, in her coat, and there's a mug going cold in front of her, and she's looking at the dark window where the river is, and she doesn't turn around when I come in, she just says, to the glass, "I wondered when you'd do something stupid."

"You heard."

"The whole floor's two juniors and they had their headphones on and I still heard. *It's not his.*" She says my line back to me without cruelty, the way you'd hand someone a thing they dropped. "Fifteen years I waited for that. I'd about given up. I thought you were going to make it to the end clean."

"You're not going to talk me out of it."

"God, no." She turns then. Babs has a face that stopped expecting anything from the species during the Clinton administration, and right now it has a thing in it I have never seen there, and it takes me a second to place it because I have only ever seen it from the panel side, scored on other faces, named for clients. It's the real one. The orbicularis thing. The muscle that can't lie, doing its small involuntary work at the corner of her eyes, for me. "I'm the last person who'd talk you out of it. I'm the cautionary tale, Mona. I'm the one who stayed."

"Babs—"

"I apologized for something once. A real one. You know that. You've all wondered for years what it was." She eats an almond. "I'm not going to tell you what it was. But I'll tell you the only thing I learned from it, free, as a leaving present, because you are leaving, whether you've worked that out yet or not." She sets the mug down. "There is no apology," she says — her line, the bleakest one in the trade, the one she drops like a coin and never watches the ripple. And then she watches the ripple. She has never once watched the ripple. She watches it now. "There's only the next thing they're allowed to want from you. I've said that to you a hundred times. I said it to you about Garrett." She stands, gathers her coat around her. "Here's the part I never said. You can get off it. You can decide there's no next thing. They can want and want and you can just—" she shrugs, a whole serene cosmic shrug "—not be on the menu anymore. It costs everything. It costs everything you've got." She passes me, close, and for one second her hand finds my arm and squeezes it, dry and brief, a thing Babs does not do, a thing I would have bet my career Babs could not do. "You've got everything. Spend it."

And she's gone too. The almonds left in a paper cup on the counter. I eat one. It's stale. Everything in this building is slightly stale; that's the smell I'd stopped smelling; it was almonds and carpet and other people's worst days.

---

Dev is at his desk when I come back out, which he should not be, it's nearly six on a Saturday, and I know why he's there, he's there because he saw Renata's coat and my face and he stayed, the way he stays, the boy who hasn't been hollowed out, who covered for me with the misfiled draft and said *no one saw it* when that wasn't true, who has spent a year watching me flinch and writing notes he doesn't need so he'd feel less complicit.

He stands up when he sees me. He has my coat, somehow; he's holding it; he must have gotten it from the rack. He doesn't hand it to me yet.

"You didn't send it," he says.

"No."

"Renata told the juniors to go home." His voice does a thing. "She doesn't send people home early on a ship day unless there's nothing to ship."

"There's nothing to ship."

He looks at me, the whole open face arranging itself around the actual question, the way it did over Garrett's room a year ago by the coffee machine that's heard of coffee, and I brace, because I know this kid, I trained this kid, I know the shape of what's coming three sentences out the way Delphine knows the shape of me, and I cannot stop it and I do not want to stop it.

"Mona," he says. "Can I say a thing. I've wanted to say it for about six months and I've been too scared, and you're leaving, I can tell you're leaving, so I'm going to say it." He swallows. The half-swallow, the real one, the involuntary one, the one I have spent a fortune teaching men to fake. "You're the best apologist alive." A beat. He's not pausing for effect; he doesn't know how to pause for effect; that's why I hired him; that's why I'll never replace him. "And I've never once heard you be sorry."

There it is.

I have been waiting for this sentence for fifteen years. I built a career so I would never have to hear it. I have engineered a thousand rooms specifically to keep this sentence out of them. And it arrives in a fluorescent office on a Saturday from a twenty-eight-year-old holding my coat, and it does not gut me, which is the surprise, it does not gut me at all, because it is true, and a true thing said plainly does not gut you, it *frees* you, that is the thing nobody in my trade knows because we have never once let one into the room. It's not a wound. It's a door.

"No," I say. "You haven't."

He waits for the rest. There's always a rest. With me there is always a rest, the follow-up, the seam-work, the beautiful recovery. I can feel it loading even now, the twitch in the severed limb, *you've never heard me be sorry because I save it, Dev, I save it for the one person, the way a sommelier doesn't drink the good bottle at lunch* — oh, that's good, that would land, I can see him give —

I don't say it.

"You're right," I say instead. Just that. The plainest sentence I have produced in this building in fifteen years, and the juniors are gone so no one scores it, and Dev doesn't score it, Dev just nods, slow, and there's water in his eyes that he isn't performing because he can't, and he hands me my coat.

"Go," he says. "Wherever it is. Go."

---

I take the elevator down. The lobby guard nods at me the way he's nodded at me for nine years, and I nod back, and it occurs to me on the street that I will probably never see him again, and that I never learned his name, and that this is the kind of thing the job did, it made everyone a face to score and no one a name to keep.

The river is right there. The hospice is on the other side of it. I have a phone in my pocket that says *come now if coming* and a sister in a church hall thirty minutes behind me who set down a tray and picked it back up, and a mother across the water who is, right now, leaving, taking the only answer with her.

I have no career. I have no script. I have no draft, no folder I'd want, no half-beat, no tweezers, no client, no firm, no version, no follow-up, nothing in either hand. I have spent everything. Babs said spend it and I spent it; I am, for the first time since I was twenty-six, a person with nothing to perform with.

I have one sentence left, unspent, and I do not know how to say it, and that is, it turns out, the only condition under which it could ever be true.

I start walking toward the bridge.

The walk is long and plain and I am not going to make it mean anything. It's cold. The water's loud. My feet hurt in the shoes I bought for the wedding. There's nothing in me reaching for a way to put it, and the quiet where the reaching used to be is the strangest thing I have ever felt, like a missing tooth, like a turned-off engine you can still feel in your hands.

Across the river there's a low building with a few windows lit, and behind one of them a woman who knows the answer is forgetting it faster than I can get there, and at the front desk of that building there will be a corridor, and a fish tank, and a sister doing the real version of the only thing I ever learned to fake.

I am going to walk in with nothing and try to say the thing I have never been able to say.

I am going to be very bad at it.

For once in my life, I am counting on it.


---

# Chapter 27 — The Same Words

Act III  ·  Fluency gauge: 1/10  ·  Artifact: YES

---

**[ — DRAFT — FOR READING ]**

> ~~There is no version of me that did not need it to be true.~~ [INS: I needed it to be true and I told myself that was the same as it being true.]
>
>       *[margin: this is the masterpiece line. it works for him. STET. — whose him.]*
>
> I let you keep believing the best of someone because it cost ~~me~~ [INS: nothing] and it kept you ~~close~~ [INS: where I could—]
>
>       *[margin: hold the catch on "close." — stop. don't you dare STET your own—]*
>
> ~~I made you say a thing you never said.~~ [INS: I put words in a mouth that wasn't mine to fill.]
>
>       *[margin: too true — pull back. nobody recants the self. — no. leave it. it's the only—]*
>
> ~~I think she did. I think she did want you. I just couldn't stand that she—~~
>
>       *[margin: cut. this is not his. — then whose is it.]*
>       *[margin: —]*
>       *[margin:* ~~*—*~~ *]*

---

I read it once, at my own kitchen table, at eleven at night, in the coat I wore to the wedding, with the river still in my ears from the walk.

I printed it. I don't know why I printed it. I have not printed a thing in this apartment in nine years; I had to find the cable. But there was a thing in me that wanted it on paper, off the screen, where I could hold it and not just scroll it, and I understand now that the thing in me wanted to hold it the way you hold a body — to confirm the weight, to make sure it's real before you do the thing you've come to do.

So I am holding it. One page. Single-spaced. The margin notes running down the right side like a second voice losing its nerve.

It is the best thing I have ever written. I have said that before, in this building and out of it, and every other time I said it I was a little bit lying — flexing, scoring myself, putting the catch in my own voice on the word *best.* This time I'm not. This is just the fact. This page is the finest object I will ever make, and it is finished, and it works, and there is nothing left to do to it.

That's the problem. There is nothing left to do to it.

A document you can't improve is a document that's done with you.

---

Here is what the page is, if you take it apart, and I am the only person alive who can take it apart.

On one reading it's the apology that redeems Roland Ashe. *I let you keep believing the best of someone because it kept you close* — for Ashe that's the donor, the grateful patient, the city that put his name on the wing. It would ship Thursday. It would trend. A stranger at a bus stop would watch a billionaire say it and decide a soul was present.

On the other reading — the same words, not different words, the *same words* — it's the thing I owe Delphine. *I put words in a mouth that wasn't mine to fill.* That one's about a graveside and the wrong father in a box and a sister I rearranged with a sentence I made our mother say.

The page does not pick. I built it not to pick. The same sentence carries the billionaire's pardon and my confession on a single set of words, and the margin notes — you can see them up there — tried to sort it, *this is his, this is not his, whose line is this,* and then they couldn't, and then they thinned, and near the bottom one of them is just a dash, and the last one is a dash struck through.

I have been annotating myself my whole life. The notes ran out on the page before they ran out in me, but they're running out in me too. I can feel it going. It's not peace.

---

I could read this page to Delphine.

I want to be honest about how close I came to that plan, because it is the plan a person like me arrives at, the elegant one, the one that lets you keep your hands clean while looking like you got them dirty. I could go to the hospice. I could sit in the corridor with the fish tank and the smell, and I could take out this page, and I could read it to her in my own voice, with the catch I built landing on *close,* and it would be the most beautiful thing she had ever heard.

And she would hear the craft.

She told me that already. Three weeks ago on a bench, then again at her own wedding, she heard it coming three words out — *I felt the place where your voice was going to go before it went there.* I gave her my best, twice, and both times she clocked the tweezers. This page is better than either of those. And being better is the thing that disqualifies it, because the better it is, the more it's a thing I *made,* and she didn't ask for a thing I made. She asked me a question. She asked if it was true.

You cannot read a true answer off a page. The reading is the performance. The words can all be true and the *delivery* is still a lie, because delivery is the thing I do to truth to make strangers buy it, and Delphine is not a stranger and is not buying.

There's no version of this page I can read aloud that isn't me reaching for the dial one last time.

That's what the page was for, it turns out — not for her, not for him, but to teach me the thing the trade had left to teach me: even the truest writing is still writing. A sentence I labored over is a sentence I controlled, and the only sincerity left to a person who can fake anything is the thing she can't control.

I have one true sentence. What I didn't know until tonight, holding the paper, is that the sentence can't *be* on the paper. The instant it's good enough to write down well, it's already not true enough to say.

---

So.

I take the page to the sink.

This is, I'm aware, a melodramatic thing to do, and I have spent a career stripping melodrama out of other people's grief — *don't burn the letter, nobody believes a person who burns a letter, it tests as theatrical* — and here I am, forty-one, in my wedding coat, holding a printed page over a stainless basin with a box of the long matches I keep for the pilot light, about to do the most theatrical thing available to a human being in a kitchen.

I don't burn it.

I was going to tell you I burned it. I had the match out. There's a version of this where I burn it and it's a beautiful paragraph, the orange crawling up the margin notes, *too true — pull back* going to ash, very cinematic, tests well. I wrote that version in my head standing at the sink and I heard myself writing it and I put the matches down, because burning it would be one more performance, one more thing I did *beautifully* to a true thing, and I am trying — I am trying, badly, for the first time — to stop.

So I just tear it.

It's not graceful. Paper doesn't tear the way it does in films; it goes crooked, it fights you on the fold, the margin notes end up on three different pieces and *close* is on one piece and *—* is on another and *I put words in a mouth that wasn't mine to fill* gets torn through the word *mouth,* which is so on-the-nose that if a junior put it in a draft I'd cut it for being too much, and I leave it, because it's not a draft. It's a Tuesday. It's a thing that's just happening, with no one scoring it.

I put the pieces in the kitchen bin, on top of a takeout container and a wine cork and an envelope from the management company about the building going condo that I have been not-opening for a month, and I close the lid, and that's it. The best thing I ever made is in the trash under a cork, and I delete the file off the drive too, the original, hold the key down and watch it go, and then I empty the folder it lived in — no. I don't empty the folder. The folder has the others in it, the orphaned ones, the chef's room and the senator's girl and the actress's *I knew and I let it happen.* Those aren't mine to throw out. Those are theirs. I just take mine back out of the company they were keeping, the masterpiece, the one that was never an orphan because it always had a home, and I let it go, and the folder closes around the others, who are still waiting, who will always be waiting, for someone to say their true thing out loud, which is the one service Pierce Holloway Strategic has never once provided and never will.

I keep my own line out of the folder. I always have. There was a reason. I could never get it down badly enough to be true.

I am about to find out if I can get it *said* that badly.

---

The car won't come out here this late; I don't call one anyway. I take the train. It's the last useful one, half-empty, a man asleep against the window, a woman eating something out of foil, two kids in scrubs who got off a shift at the place I'm going to or one like it. The window is black and throws my own face back at me, and I look at it, and I can't read it. That's the new thing. I read every face in every room for a living and I cannot read this one. I don't know if what I'm about to do is real. I'm just a tired woman on a late train in the wrong coat, and I don't know.

I think that might be the answer to Delphine's whole question. Not just *was it true* about the mother. Whether anything is, about me. And the answer is: I don't get to know in advance. That's what the kid said it cost — Cora, sixteen, *you don't get to know if it works before you do it* — and I said it back to her like wisdom, sitting on a craft I'd never once paid. Now it's my fare. You go in not knowing and you stay not knowing and the not-knowing is the price.

The train smells of disinfectant and somebody's coffee and, faintly, under it, cut grass, which is impossible, there is no grass on a train, and I know what that is, I have known for fifteen years what that smell is and what room it opens, and I let it open. I don't fight it down this time. There's no draft to protect. There's nothing in either hand. The room opens — the graveside, the bad casserole, the white tent, the twenty-six-year-old with a plastic cup of warm white wine and a sentence she was about to fire into the only person who still loved her — and I just sit in it, on the train, and let it be a room I'm in, and the woman in scrubs across the aisle looks at me for a second the way you look at someone who might be about to be a problem, and then looks away, because I'm not crying, I'm just sitting very still with my face doing nothing the instrument can name.

---

The hospice at one in the morning is a low building with the wrong number of lights on.

I know this building now. I was here Tuesday — was it Tuesday, it was the day Ada said *is it Tuesday, I have the thing, on Tuesday,* and then went somewhere I couldn't follow her to and took the answer with her. The doors are the kind that sigh open for you whether you're ready or not. The lobby has the fish tank, lit blue, the same six fish doing the same circuit, the most expensive calm in the building, and a desk, and a young man at the desk in a cardigan who looks up and knows me, which means Delphine told them I might come, which means Delphine left a door open for me even now, even after the bench and the wedding and every crafted thing I handed her that she set back down.

He says, quietly, "She's been waiting for you. They both have."

And here it comes — I feel it load, the old reflex, the thing I have done a thousand times to get through a hard threshold, the joke that takes the temperature down a degree so everyone can breathe. I feel the whole shape of it arrive, fully formed, *both of them, well, that's a tougher room than I usually open,* dry, deflecting, a little bit cruel, perfectly weighted to make the man in the cardigan exhale and like me and let me past without either of us having to feel the size of the thing — I feel it arrive in my mouth the way every line has arrived in my mouth for fifteen years, on time, finished, gift-wrapped.

And it dies there.

It just — doesn't come out. I open my mouth on it and there's nothing behind the teeth, no breath under it, the way there was no word behind the breath the first night I tried to write the four words. The joke is right there and I cannot pick it up. It's not that I decide not to. Deciding not to would be a kind of control and control is the thing that's gone. It's that I reach for it, the muscle reaches, and the muscle closes on air, like a hand that's been holding a tool so long it's forgotten it can also just be a hand.

The man waits. He's good at waiting; everyone in this building is good at waiting; it's the job, my sister's job, the real version of the thing I learned to fake — being present at the unfixable with no script and nothing in your hands.

"Thank you," I say. Just that. The least I have ever said to a stranger at a threshold. No joke, no warmth dialed in, no catch. I sound, to my own ear, like nobody in particular. Like a person.

He nods and tells me the room, end of the hall, past the second nurses' station, and says, "Take your time," which is a kind thing to say and also a kind of joke, because the one thing none of us has down here is time, and he doesn't mean it as a joke, and I don't take it as one, and that is the whole new country I'm in.

---

I stand up. My feet hurt in the shoes I bought for the wedding. I tore the page; I have nothing in my hands. I have the coat, which is wrong for the weather, and one sentence I have carried for fifteen years and have never once been able to set down well enough to file or badly enough to believe.

Down the hall there's a light on in the last room. There's a fish tank behind me throwing blue on the floor. There's a sister somewhere ahead of me doing the real thing, and a mother in a bed who is leaving, hours from taking the only answer with her, and I have to walk toward her and stand there and try to say a true thing with nothing to say it with.

I'm going to be very bad at it.

I built a room where I could be the only one allowed to feel anything, and I've lived in it alone for fifteen years, and the only way the door opens is badly, in my own voice, in real time, where it can't be edited or taken back.

I walk down the hall toward the light.

The door is open.


---

# Chapter 28 — It Wasn't True

Act III  ·  Fluency gauge: 0/10  ·  Artifact: NO

---

The door is open.

I stand in it for a while. I don't know how long. I have spent my whole life knowing exactly how long a pause runs — to the half-beat, to the frame — and I stand in this doorway and I could not tell you. A while.

I walked the hall to get here. Past the second nurses' station, like the man said. The hall has a handrail on both sides and a print of a beach on the wall and a cart with little cups on it, and at the end of it the last door, and I walked it slowly, in the shoes I bought for the wedding, and my feet hurt, and I had nothing in either hand. No page. No folder. No version. I have never once walked toward a hard room with nothing in my hands and I did it slowly because slowly was all my legs had.

The room is small. There is a bed and a window and a chair pulled up to the bed and a second chair against the wall and a machine on a pole doing the thing machines do, and a smell, which is the smell, the one I have carried for fifteen years and called by other names, and which here is just the smell of a room where a person is dying, and under it, impossibly, cut grass, because someone has had the window cracked and it is June and someone, somewhere, in the dark, has mowed.

Ada is in the bed. My mother is in the bed.

She is very small. I knew she would be small — I was here Tuesday, I sat in this chair, I asked her the question and she went where she goes and took it with her — but I had three days of not seeing her and the not-seeing reset something and now she is smaller than the three-days-ago version I was carrying and I have to stand in the door and let the size of her arrive.

Delphine is in the chair by the bed.
