She has her hand on the blanket where Ada's arm is. She is not holding the hand. She is just resting her own hand there, on the blanket, near, which I understand now is a thing she knows how to do that I do not, a thing she does for strangers every day, the being-near without the grabbing. She looks up. She has been crying, or she has been doing the thing that is past crying, and her face is bare in a way I have no word for that I would use, so I will just say it was bare.

"You came," she says.

"Yes."

"She's not — " Delphine stops. She does that. She lets a sentence stop where it stops. "It's close."

I come in. I sit in the second chair, the one against the wall, because the one by the bed is hers and I am not going to take it. I sit with my coat on. It is too warm in the room for the coat and I leave it on.

Theo is not here. I notice it without deciding to. There is a paper cup on the windowsill with cold coffee in it and a folded sweater on the back of Delphine's chair that is too big to be hers, and I understand that he was here and that he stepped out, on purpose, to give the room to the two of us and the bed. My sister married a man who knows when a room is not his. I never learned that. It is a simple thing and I never learned it.

---

For a while nobody says anything.

I want to write that the silence was full of something, that it pressed, that it had a quality. It didn't. It was just quiet. The machine ticked. Ada breathed in the slow uneven way she was breathing, a breath and then a long nothing and then another breath, and each long nothing was long enough that I would think it was the last one and then it wasn't.

I have sat in a hundred rooms and managed the silence in all of them. I am the person you bring in to manage the silence. I sit in this one and I have nothing. There is nothing to manage. The silence is not a thing that wants managing. It is just the room being the room.

A nurse comes in once and looks at the machine and writes something on a clipboard and looks at Ada and does a small thing with the blanket and goes out again. She does not look at me. She looks at Delphine and Delphine nods, a small nod, and the nurse nods back, and the two of them have a whole conversation in two nods that I cannot read, and I have read every face in every room for fifteen years and I cannot read this one, the plainest one, two women agreeing about a death, and I sit there outside it.

Delphine moves her thumb on the blanket. Once. Back and forth.

"She had a good afternoon," Delphine says. "She knew the nurse. She asked about the radio. We had the radio on."

"That's good."

"It was good." She looks at me. "You can come closer. She's not — she won't bite."

It is a small joke. It is the kind of small joke that people who do this work make, the gentle devastating kind, my sister's register, and three weeks ago I would have caught it and lobbed something back, something dry, something that took the temperature down a degree, and it would have been good, and it would have been a wall.

I don't have it. I reach for it and it isn't there. I move my chair closer instead. The legs drag on the floor and the sound is too loud and I wince at the sound and that is the most honest thing my face has done in a decade — a wince at a chair, nothing dialed, nothing held.

---

I look at Ada.

I am not going to describe her face well. I could. I know faces. I could give you the orbicularis, the slack and the not-slack, I could read this face frame by frame the way Sloan taught me and tell you what is firing and what has stopped, and it would be accurate and it would be obscene, so I won't.

She is my mother. She is dying. Her mouth is open a little. Her hand on the blanket is the hand I am thinking of when I think of being small, the actual hand, and it is so thin now that it is almost not a hand, and I think — I am ashamed of the thought even as I have it — I think, *I could draft what she'd say. If I needed her to say a thing. I could build it.* The thought comes the way they all come, on time, finished, and I feel sick, because that is exactly what I did. That is the thing I am here about. I built what she said. Fifteen years ago I needed her to have said a thing and she hadn't and I built it and put it in her mouth and fired it into Delphine, and now the mouth is open and saying nothing and will never say anything again and I cannot un-build it because the only person who could tell me the true version is leaving the room one long breath at a time.

"Mona," Delphine says.

"Yeah."

"You should say it now."

---

I knew she would say that. I came knowing.

What I did not know — what I am finding out in the chair, with my coat on, too warm — is that there is nothing in me to say it *with*. I have always had the thing. The thing is the whole of me. You ask me to say a hard sentence and I have, instantly, ready, the version that lands, the version with the catch set and the half-beat placed, the version that a stranger at a bus stop would watch and decide a soul was present. I have it for Garrett. I have it for senators. I had it three weeks ago on a bench and again at the wedding, the best apology in the country, twice, and both times my sister set it down like a dish she didn't order.

I reach for it now. Out of habit. Out of fifteen years of habit, the way your tongue goes to the tooth. I reach for the version and —

It's gone.

Not held back. Gone. I want to be clear, because it matters, it is the only thing left that matters: I am not choosing to be plain. Choosing would be a kind of control and the control is the thing that's gone. I open my mouth to say the true thing and the machine that builds the sentence isn't running. The fan that has roared over everything my whole life is off, I felt it go off on the train, and in the quiet there is no draft and no version and no door in the wall, there is just me and a question and a mouth that has forgotten how to do anything except the thing I will not do.

So what comes out is bad.

---

"Del," I say.

That's all I have for a second. Her name. Half her name.

"I'm here," she says.

"It was — " I stop. The sentence I reached for had a shape and the shape is wrong and I drop it. "I have to tell you a thing and I'm going to do it badly. I don't — okay. Okay."

"Okay," she says.

"At the funeral. Your dad's funeral. When I said the thing about Mom."

"Yes."

"It — " And here it comes, the catch, the actual catch, the one I have sold to forty men, the wet thing at the corner of the eye and the half-swallow before the hard word, except it is not on the corner of an eye I am watching in a monitor, it is in my own throat, it has arrived in my own throat without my permission, uncoached, and I cannot do the thing I do, I cannot pull it back, I cannot STET it, it just happens to me, the way it happens to people, and my voice does the thing and I hate it and I let it. "It wasn't — I made it — "

I stop.

"Take your time," Delphine says. The thing the man at the desk said. The kind thing that is also a joke because there is no time, and she does not mean it as a joke and I do not take it as one.

"I made it up," I say.

It comes out flat. It comes out like a person reading a number off a card. There is no music in it. I have spent my life putting music in things and there is no music in it and I leave the no-music there because the no-music is the only true part.

"I don't — Del, I don't know if it was true. That's the — that's the thing I came to. I needed it to be true. At the funeral. I needed it so bad. You had — you got the good dad and the good years and Mom upstairs every night doing the thing with you, the radio, the — and I raised myself, I did, I raised myself, and I was drunk and I was twenty-six and I needed there to be a thing wrong underneath your good thing, and I made one. Out of — there were these — she'd say things, when you were little, she'd get this look, and I took the look and I built — I built a whole — "

I can't get to the end of it. The sentence wants an end and I don't have the end. I have built a thousand sentences to their landing, I always know where a sentence is going to come down, and this one I don't, I am inside it and I cannot see the ground.

"I think she did want you," I say.

Delphine goes still.

"I think she did. I — that's the — I think she did want you, Del, I think she did, and I couldn't — I couldn't stand that she — "

The breath in the bed stops. The long nothing. I wait inside it. It goes on. It goes on too long. And then it doesn't, then there's another breath, and I am crying now, which I have not done in front of a person since I was younger than Cora, and it is ugly, it is not a single tear placed on the right word, it is the whole face going, the nose, the sound, the thing I would cut from any draft for being too much, and I leave it.

"I don't know if it was true," I say. "That's what I came to say and it's the worst thing because it's the thing you can't — there's no — I can't give you the clean one. I wanted to come in here and tell you it was a lie, I wanted to give you that, give you the clean thing, *she wanted you, I lied,* tied off, done, and I can't, because I don't *know,* I half — I made it half out of real things and I can't get back to which half, and Mom's the only one who — and she's — "

I look at the bed.

"And she's not going to tell me," I say. "She's not going to tell either of us. I asked her Tuesday. I sat right there and I asked her and she went away. She took it. Whatever it was. She took it with her and I don't — "

I stop because I am at the place where the sentence breaks and I let it break.

"It wasn't true," I say. "Del. I don't think it was true. I think I needed it and I made it and it wasn't — I'm — "

And I can't finish it.

I sit in the chair with my coat on, too warm, with the whole face gone, in front of my sister and my dying mother, and the most articulate woman in the country cannot finish the sentence, because the true thing does not have a clean end on it, because if I finished it cleanly it would be a lie again, it would be the version, it would be the thing I do, and the only way it stays true is broken, and it stays broken, and I let it, and I am so ashamed of it, of how bad it is, how naked, how nothing, no shape, no landing, a sentence a child would be embarrassed by, and the shame is the only thing in fifteen years I have been certain was real.

---

Delphine doesn't say anything for a while.

She doesn't reach for me. She doesn't reach for the clean version either, doesn't hand me back something to manage. She is a person whose entire life is being present at the unfixable with no script and she does not fix this, because it isn't fixable, and she knows that the way she knows the breathing, and she lets it be what it is.

Then she says, "Okay."

That's all. *Okay.*

And I look at her and she is looking at me and her face is doing nothing I could name, nothing for a monitor, just looking, and I understand that she believes me. I have stood in front of more people than I can count and made them believe a thing and I know exactly what believing looks like from the outside, the soft animal click of it, and I have never once produced it with the truth, only with the version, and here it is, on my sister's face, produced by the worst sentence I have ever said, the one with no end on it.

She believes me. Not because it was good. Because it was bad. Because it had the thing in it the box can't put there and I can't put there, the broken place, the not-knowing left in raw, and she has sat with enough dying people and their families to know the sound of a person who has stopped performing, it is her whole profession, the real version of my counterfeit, and she heard it.

"I didn't want sorry," she says. "I never wanted sorry. Sorry's for you."

"I know."

"I wanted to know if it was true."

"I know. And I — " The thing rises again, the wanting-to-clean-it, the wanting to give her the tied-off version even now. I push it down. "And I don't know. And that's the — that's the answer. That I don't know. I'm so sorry it's not a better answer."

"It's the true answer," Delphine says. "It's the first true thing you've said to me in fifteen years." She moves her thumb on the blanket. "That'll do."

I almost say *thank you.* I have it ready. It would be the right thing and it would be a small clean thing and it would be, even now, a thing I reached for because it would go down right, and I catch it and I don't say it, because I don't want to put another good thing in this room. I want to leave the room with only the bad thing in it. So I don't say thank you. I just nod. I have spent my life being unable to leave a silence alone and I leave this one alone, and it is the second-hardest thing I have done tonight, and the hardest one is already done and sitting in the air between us with no end on it.

---

We sit.

There is nothing to manage and I do not manage it. The radio is off but I can hear, very faint through the cracked window, a car somewhere, and the grass, the impossible cut grass, and the machine, and the breathing, the breath and the long nothing and the breath.

I move my chair closer again. I put my hand on the blanket near Ada's arm, not on the arm, near it, the way Delphine does, the thing I did not know how to do an hour ago, and I copy my sister, and that is allowed, I think, copying a true thing, it is the only honest kind of copying I have ever done.

My mother breathes. I will not know. I came here to find out and I am not going to find out. She is going to leave with the answer the way the dead leave with everything, and I am going to have to live in the not-knowing, in the half I made out of real things and the half that was real, forever, with no version to file it under and no clean line to tell myself, and I sit there and I let that be true too, the not-knowing, the worst one, the one with no end on it, the same shape as the sentence.

Delphine's hand is near Ada's hand. My hand is near Delphine's hand. None of us is holding on. We are just near, in the small room, with the window cracked on the grass, and nobody is scoring it, and the breath goes in, and the long nothing comes, and we wait inside it together for it to be the last one, not knowing, which is the only way any of us was ever going to do this.

The breath comes again.

I don't reach for a way to put it. There isn't one. For once that is not a failure.


---

# Chapter 29 — The Plain Place

Act III  ·  Fluency gauge: 1/10  ·  Artifact: NO

---

Ada dies on a Thursday, which is a ship day, and I notice that I noticed, and I let it go.

She dies at four in the morning, the hour I used to do my best work, the trance hour, the hour the true sentences arrive from below the part of you that signs the invoices. I am in the second chair against the wall when it happens. Delphine is in the chair by the bed. There is a long nothing and then there is no breath at the end of it, and we wait inside the nothing the way we had been waiting, and at some point Delphine puts her hand flat on the blanket and leaves it there, and the machine on the pole keeps doing the thing it does for a minute and then a nurse comes and turns it off, and that is all. That is the whole event. I had thought there would be more event.

She died without telling me. I want to put that down plainly because it is the fact and the fact is the thing. She did not, at the end, open her eyes and say the one thing that would have let me file the rest of my life under *true* or under *lie*. She went the way the dead go, which is with everything. I asked her on Tuesday and she went where she goes. She is gone now and the answer is gone with her and it is not coming back, and I am going to live the rest of my life not knowing whether the worst thing I ever did was also true, and there is no version of that I can make land softer, because the not-knowing does not have a soft version, I have looked.

I am, for the record, no longer employed.

---

I should say what is gone, so the size of it is clear. I am good at the size of things. It is most of what I have left.

The account is gone. I did not ship the Ashe apology, the masterpiece, the one that would have made me a legend in a trade with no legends because no one is allowed to know your name; I killed it, I sat in Renata's office with the good water and told her I was not going to write it, and she looked at me with the specific warmth she keeps for the moment before she stops finding you useful, and said she understood, in the tone of a woman who does not, and that was the firm. Fifteen years. The byline I never had. Gone.

And the leak went the way leaks go. Someone could name me as the invisible hand behind a dozen famous contritions and someone did, and so the one thing I wanted my whole life — to be seen doing the invisible thing — arrived at last, in the worst possible form, as an accusation, in a tense I cannot control. I am, this week, a small item. *The woman who wrote their sorrys.* There are quotes from people who feel betrayed to learn that the apology that moved them was built. I understand the betrayal. I helped invent it. I would feel it too if I were them, and were able to feel things in the ordinary, un-itemized way other people manage, which is a skill I am told you can learn and which I am starting, very late, at forty-one, to attempt.

So: no whale. No firm. No byline that isn't a wound. The rent-stabilized one-bed, which I would have lain down in traffic to keep, I am going to lose, probably, because terror was always the only thing the job paid in and the terror has stopped paying. I have a folder of true things other people couldn't say and no job that requires it. I am, by every metric I know how to run, and I know how to run all of them, losing.

I notice I am not, this morning, especially afraid. I notice it the way you notice a tooth has stopped hurting — by reaching for the pain out of habit and finding the place gone quiet. I keep reaching for the fear, the good clean professional fear that used to organize my whole life, and the dial is there and I turn it and nothing happens, the way nothing happened in the chair when I reached for the version. The fan is off. It went off on a train and it has not come back on, and in the quiet there is no terror and no draft and no version, there is just a Thursday, and my mother is dead, and I do not have a job, and the worst sentence I ever said still does not have an end on it.

That is the inventory. I have always been thorough.

---

I help Delphine with the things you do.

This is a sentence I did not expect to write. There are things you do, it turns out, in the days after, and they are small and physical and have nothing to do with language, and my sister knows all of them, because her whole life is the days after other people's mothers, and I do not know a single one. I am the most articulate woman in the country and I do not know which form goes where or who you call or what you do with the radio that was on the afternoon Ada had a good day. Delphine knows. Delphine has done this hundreds of times, for strangers, for almost no money, the genuine version of the thing I sold, and now she does it for our mother, and she lets me carry boxes and stand in lines and hold the door, and I do, and it is the most useful I have been to another human being in fifteen years, and not one bit of it requires me to find the right word.

"You're good at the boxes," Delphine says, in the parking lot of the place where you go for the forms.

"I have always been good at carrying a thing that isn't mine," I say, and then I hear it, the reflex, the little curl of self-incrimination polished to a shine, the move, and I stop, because I do not want to do the move at her, not anymore, not even when it would be true. "Sorry," I say. "I do that. I'll stop doing that."

"You don't have to stop," she says. "Just maybe not all the time."

"Not all the time," I say. "Okay."

It comes out flat. I leave it flat. The flatness is a thing I am practicing now, the way other people practice a language late, with an accent, getting it wrong, the verbs in the wrong place. I have spent my life with one fluency and I am trying, badly, to learn a second one, which is the fluency of meaning a small thing and just saying it the small size it is.

---

Theo makes us eggs.

He does it without ceremony, which I have come to understand is the entire content of Theo — a man who does the next needed thing and does not narrate it, who steps out of a room when the room is not his and steps into a kitchen when eggs are required, and who has, the whole time, in the corner of every scene, been quietly building load-bearing things that hold weight and do not announce that they are holding it. He puts the plate down in front of me. The eggs are not good. They are a little rubbery and he has used too much of something. They are the best meal I have had in a year, and I do not tell him that, because telling him would be a sentence, a good one, I can feel it forming, *the best meal I've had in a year*, with the small catch on *year* — and I let it go. I just eat the eggs. Eating the eggs is the thank-you. I am learning that some thank-yous are not said.

"She liked the radio," Theo says, to no one, scraping the pan. "At the end. The classical one with the man who talks too much between the songs."

"She always hated that man," Delphine says.

"She hated him on purpose," Theo says. "It gave her something to do."

And Delphine laughs — really laughs, the gentle devastating laugh, the one with the real *orbicularis oculi* in it, the Duchenne marker, the muscle Sloan spent his career teaching liars to fake, firing for free in my sister's kitchen over our dead mother and a radio host — and I do not reach to score it. That is new. The reaching used to be involuntary, the way the catch is involuntary, the way the truth finally was, in the chair. I used to read every face in every room because I could not not. And here is my sister's whole real face doing the real thing and I am not measuring it. I am just at the table. I am just glad she laughed.

I think that might be what other people have. I think that might be the thing.

---

The wedding happened, by the way. I should say. People keep asking me, the few people left who ask me anything, whether the wedding still happened, as if the death must have eaten it, and it didn't. It was last Saturday, twenty people, a kitchen's worth, Theo in a suit that did not fit at the shoulders because he built it as carefully as he builds everything except his own clothes. Ada was still alive then, barely, four days from the chair, and Delphine got married knowing her mother was four days from the chair, and she did it anyway, on purpose, the way Ada hated the radio host on purpose, because you take the good thing while the good thing is on the table and you do not wait for permission from grief, which never gives it. I delivered no speech. I had one. It was very good. I left it in the car. I am learning what to leave in the car.

I stood at the back. Nobody scored it. The vows were plain and a little clumsy and Theo lost his place once and just said the next part out of order and nobody fixed it, and it landed, it landed harder for being out of order, and I stood at the back of my sister's wedding and understood, with the slow click of a sentence that is true and that I did not write, that I have spent my entire career manufacturing the exact effect that a man losing his place in his own vows produces for free. The catch. The break in the line. The thing that reads as conscience. He did it by accident, badly, and it was real, and you could feel the whole room get it, the soft animal click, eleven million bus-stop strangers' worth of mercy, produced by a carpenter forgetting a sentence about a woman he loves.

I have been undercharging the universe for years. Or overcharging it. I can never remember which way that joke goes anymore. I used to be able to remember which way all of them went.

---

Dev calls. He is the one person from the firm who calls, which tells you everything about Dev and everything about the firm.

He is twenty-eight and idealistic and the job has not finished hollowing him out, and I had been, for two years, the thing it was using to do the hollowing, the proof that you could be the best in the country at this and stay intact, except I was never intact, I was just very good at the lighting. He knows now. He saw the misfiled draft once and moved it and never said. He saw the laptop lid go down a hundred times. He is the one at work who could always see me flinch.

"You actually did it," he says. "You killed the account. People are saying you killed the account."

"People are correct. There's a first time."

"I keep telling them you must've had an angle. A play. That nobody walks away from the Ashe thing for nothing." He pauses. "You didn't have an angle."

"No."

"I've worked next to you for two years," Dev says, and his voice does a thing, the young thing, the un-hollowed thing, "and that's the first time I've heard you say a word with nothing under it. No floor under the floor. Just the word." He laughs, a little wrecked. "You're the best apologist alive and I never once heard you be sorry. I used to think that was the scariest thing about you."

"It was," I say. "It still should be. Don't let it stop scaring you. That's the whole job, the part that should scare you. The day it stops is the day you're Babs."

"Babs is fine."

"Babs is a warning shaped like a person," I say, and there it is, the curl, the polished little knife, and I let this one go all the way because it is true and because Dev needs a true funny thing more than he needs me to be careful, and he laughs the real laugh, and I think: that one was allowed. Some of them are still allowed. The trick I am learning, very late, is which ones.

"What are you going to do," he says.

"I don't know," I say, and I mean it, flatly, the way you mean a number, and it does not frighten me the way it should, and that is either growth or shock and I have decided to stop running the diagnostic on which.

---

Delphine and I have one conversation that matters, and I want to put it down right, which means I want to put it down plain, because if I make it beautiful I will have lied, and I am done lying to her, it is the one thing I have, it is most of what I am now.

It is the night after the forms. We are on the porch, the one with the chair Theo is halfway through building, the unpainted one, the one that has been halfway through being built since before any of this, because Theo builds the way the dying breathe, a thing and then a long nothing and then a thing. Delphine has tea. I have tea. I do not want tea but it is a thing to hold and I have learned, this month, the use of a thing to hold.

"I keep waiting for you to ask me if I forgive you," Delphine says.

"I'm not going to ask you that."

"I know. That's why I brought it up." She blows on the tea. "Most people would have asked by now."

"Most people are my whole client list."

She lets that sit. She is good at letting a thing sit. It is the thing I could never do.

"I don't, by the way," she says. "Forgive you. I want to be straight, because you'll respect straight more than you'd respect the other thing." She looks at the dark yard. "That's not what this was. You did a thing to me when I was twenty-four that took me about a decade to climb out of, and I climbed out of it, and the climbing is mine, I did that, you don't get that. And I'm not going to stand here and hand you a clean feeling so you can go home lighter. Sorry is for the person saying it. I told you that."

"I know."

"But," she says, and stops, and I do the hardest thing, which is wait, which is not fill it, which is let her get there on her own legs the way I let myself get there in the chair, with no end on it. "But you came in that room with nothing in your hands. I watched you walk in with nothing. And you said the true thing, finally, and you said it so badly I believed it." She turns her face to me. There is no craft in it. I would know. "I've spent fifteen years being able to hear when someone's performing. It's my whole job too, you know. Different room. Same skill. I sit with the ones who are about to die and the ones who are about to be left, and you learn fast which sentences are real, because the unreal ones don't do anything, they just sit on top of the dying like a coat that doesn't fit. And I have heard you be unreal my entire life. And in that room, for about a minute, you were not. That's not forgiveness. I don't have a word for it. But it's the thing I drove a wedding invitation across a river to get, and I got it, so."

"So," I say.

"So that'll do," she says. "I told you that'll do. I meant it."

We drink the tea. The yard does nothing. A car goes by somewhere and the sound of it arrives and leaves and neither of us turns a phrase onto it. I used to be unable to leave a silence alone. I leave this one alone. It is getting easier. Not easy. Easier.

"You'll be all right," Delphine says, after a while, and it is not a comfort, it is an assessment, she is a person who assesses the dying for a living and she has turned the instrument on me and that is the reading. "Not because everything's fixed. Everything's worse for you, actually. You lost the whole thing."

"I lost the whole thing," I agree.

"You'll be all right anyway," she says. "Because you finally found out there was something in there the job couldn't reach. That's the part people think they're hoping for and then it terrifies them when they get it. You always knew exactly what you were. Turns out you were wrong by a little. That's enough to live on. It's not a lot. It's enough."

She is wrong about one thing — it does terrify me, the something-in-there, it terrifies me daily, a self I can suddenly feel and have no idea how to operate, like waking up with a hand I didn't know I had — but she is right about the size of it. It is not a lot. It is enough. I do not correct her. Some corrections you leave in the car.

---

So here is where I am.

I have no job and a folder of other people's truths and a mother freshly in the ground next to the wrong father, the second father, the kind one, the one who taught my sister to swim, the two of them side by side now under cut grass, and I will smell that grass for the rest of my life and it will mean both of them and the chair and the not-knowing, all at once, because the past does not file things in separate folders, it was always one smell. I have a sister who does not forgive me and who drove across a river to not forgive me to my face, which is, I am coming to understand, a far rarer and more valuable thing than the forgiveness would have been, because the forgiveness would have been about me and this was about the truth, and she has only ever wanted the truth, and for one bad minute in a small room I finally gave her one.

And I have this voice. The one telling you all this. It still works — you can hear it working, the curl is still in it, the reflex to make a clean line out of a bad day, I caught myself three times this chapter and let two of them go and kept one because it was true and small and I decided the small true ones are allowed. The gift didn't die. I half-wanted it to. It would have been cleaner. But it didn't, it just got demoted; it used to be the thing standing between me and the truth like a pane of glass I built myself, and now it is just a voice I have, the way a person has a voice, that I can put in front of a true thing or, on a better day, decline to. I am not fluent in nothing. I am fluent in one fewer thing than I was. I can no longer hide behind it. That is the whole change. It is internal. It does not show up in the inventory. Nothing on the outside got better.

I would not have believed, a month ago, that nothing-on-the-outside-got-better could feel like this. Like a window cracked on a June yard. Like the moment after you set down a weight you'd been carrying so long you'd stopped calling it a weight and started calling it your arms.

There is one thing left to do. I have been putting it off the way I put off the only thing that ever mattered. The folder is still on my drive, the one with the name no colleague would understand, the orphaned sincerities, everyone's true line that someone like me reached in and lifted to safety. Garrett's room. The senator's girl. Hundreds. And there is one line that has never been in it, that could never be in it, for fifteen years, for the simplest reason in the world: the folder is for true things people couldn't say, and I could never write mine.

But I said it. Out loud. Badly. With no end on it. In a small room with the window cracked.

So now, maybe, finally, there is one I can file.

I open the laptop. The radiator clears its throat — to say something, the way it always does — and then, for once, in the new quiet where the fan used to roar, it does not decide against it.


---

# Chapter 30 — One Entry That Isn't a Client's

Act III  ·  Fluency gauge: 1/10 (at rest)  ·  Artifact: YES (the anti-document)

---

It is a Tuesday, which used to mean a thing and now just means a Tuesday.

I am at the table in the one-bed I am going to lose. I have a date on it now — the loss, I mean; it has a date, the way everything in my old life had a date, only this one I'm not going to manage, I'm just going to let it arrive, the way the breath arrived in the bed, the long nothing and then the thing. The radiator does its noise. It clears its throat to say something and then, lately, says it, or doesn't, and either way I have stopped waiting to find out which.

The laptop is open. I opened it three weeks ago, the night after the forms, and then I closed it, because what I came to do that night was bigger than I had hands for, and I have learned, very late, at forty-one, that some things you have to open the laptop for several nights running before you do them. I am not in a trance. I want to put that down because the trance was always how the true sentences came — four in the morning, from below the part of me that signed the invoices — and I am not in it. It is the afternoon. I have had two coffees and a sandwich. There is nothing coming up from below. There is just me, sitting up here, at a table, in the light, with a thing to do that I have known how to want for fifteen years and never once known how to do.

I open the folder.

---

You've had the folder since the first night — the no-name joke, the hundreds of true lines people paid me to delete, Garrett's room and the senator's girl and the rest. I used to tell you I read them the way you'd look at photographs of a country you used to live in. That was a beautiful way to put it. The plain version is that the folder was where I went to feel something secondhand, because it was the only place I kept anything real, and a person who has to go to a folder for the real thing has built her whole life around not having it loose in the rooms where she lives. That version doesn't have a country in it. I am going to stop putting it the other way.

And there is one line in here now that doesn't belong, and I want to be honest about how it got here, because honesty is the only fluency I'm still trying to learn and I'm bad at it, I'm at the stage where you get the verbs in the wrong place.

A few weeks ago I would have told you the folder was for the true lines people *couldn't* say. That was the rule. That was the whole architecture of it. The folder held the things that got too true and had to come out — orphaned sincerities, lines with no home, because the mouths they belonged to weren't allowed to keep them. And mine could never go in, for the simplest reason in the world: the folder was for true things people couldn't say, and I could never write mine. I could never get it down badly enough to be true. Every time I wrote it, it came out good, and good was the proof it was a lie.

But I said it. Out loud. In a small room with the window cracked on the grass. I said it so badly that, for the first time in my life, I was believed. So now there is a thing I could not write but did say, and it has nowhere to live, and that is the definition of an orphan, and this is the folder for those.

So I am going to put it in here. Hers. Mine.

I have been trying to figure out how.

---

Here is the problem, and it is a funny problem, in the small way things are still funny to me now, which is the way a thing is funny when the laugh already has the ache in it and you can't get the two apart:

I can't write it down.

Not the way I couldn't before — that was a failure of nerve dressed up as a failure of craft. This is the opposite. The thing I said in the bed cannot be written, because the moment I write it, I will fix it. My hands will fix it. They cannot help it. I sat down to transcribe what I said — just take it down, the way it came out, the stammer, the half-words, the place where it broke — and I got three lines in and I caught my own right hand smoothing the break. Closing the gap. Setting a comma where the air had been. The break was where I'd stopped, in the room, because the true thing had no end on it, and my hand wanted to give it one, the way a hand wants to set a dislocated thing back into its socket, and a clean sentence is a socket, and I have spent my whole life setting sentences. The cleaner I made it, the more it lied. By line four it read like something. By line five it read like *me.*

I deleted it. Then I tried again, slower, telling the foreman to stand down, and got to line three before he smoothed it again, quieter that time, almost tender, which is worse, because tenderness is just cruelty with better manners and I taught a hundred clients to weaponize exactly that. I deleted that one too.

I am, you understand, deleting the truth in order to keep it true, which is the most Pierce Holloway sentence I have ever generated and I am going to leave it standing because it is also, for once, just what happened. There is a version of my whole life in that sentence and I am too tired to draft it.

The trade has a rule for this, somewhere near the bottom of the list, the rule nobody says out loud: *the better the writing, the less the reader believes it of you.* We sold it as a warning for clients who over-apologized — Cole, drowning in his own fluency, the public exhausted by his remorse. I never once applied it to the page in front of me until now. My handwriting is good. My handwriting has been good since I was nine, since before I knew what it would be for, and good handwriting is just one more thing a lawyer would approve, one more surface that holds, and the woman in the bed needed the opposite of a surface that holds. She needed the thing that broke. And you cannot break a thing on purpose; the moment you do, it isn't broken, it's *distressed,* it's a finish, it's the firm aging a logo to look like it's been trusted for a century. I have distressed a hundred apologies. I cannot distress this one. It has to actually break or it's a lie, and you can't make a true thing break by trying, you can only have already broken and then not fix it.

So that's the move, the last one I'll teach you, the only one I learned too late to sell: don't fix it.

---

So I do the thing I do not want to do, which is the thing my sister would do, which is to stop trying to make it good.

I take out a pen. I take out a piece of paper — the back of a Pierce Holloway envelope, because that is the paper I have, the firm's cream stock with the embossed name of the place that I no longer work, which is a joke I notice and decline to develop. And I write it by hand, the way I tried to in the bad month and couldn't, because even my handwriting performed, the careful lift on the loop, the plainness-on-purpose that was just one more move. I tried, then, to write ugly, and ugly came out as a style.

It is different now. I don't know why it is different and I have decided to stop running the diagnostic on why. Maybe it is different because I am not trying to *be* believed anymore. I already was. It happened. It is behind me, the one time, and you cannot perform a thing toward an outcome you already have. The pressure is off the line. Nobody is going to read this. That is the whole point of it. It is going in a folder with a lost punchline for a name, on a drive, in a one-bed I'm about to lose, and the only person who will ever see it is me, and I have already heard it, in a room, in June, with the grass coming through.

So my hand stops trying to win.

I write what I said. I get the half of the name. I get the *I made it up* with no music on it. I get the *I think she did want you* twice, because I said it twice, not for effect, I want that on the record, not for effect — I said it twice because I could not get past it, and a thing you say twice because you can't get past it is the realest grammar there is, and I leave both of them, I do not cut the repeat, I who have cut a thousand repeats. I get the place where it broke. I get the dash that doesn't resolve. I get the *I'm—* with nothing after it.

And I look at it.

It is bad. I want to be precise, because precision is the only thing I have that's entirely mine, and I am using it now to tell you the truth instead of to hide it: it is *bad.* It is a sentence a child would be embarrassed by. The repeats. The breaks. The hand wobbling on *want.* No catch placed, no half-beat held, no landing — it just stops, mid-air, where the floor should be, the way I stopped in the chair. There is nothing in it a stranger at a bus stop would watch and decide a soul was present. There is no place in it I could put the tweezers.

That's how I know it's hers and not a client's.

---

I don't add margin notes.

I notice I don't, the way you notice a tooth that's stopped hurting — by reaching for the habit and finding the place gone quiet. The reach is still in me. I will not pretend it isn't. Some sentence in the back of my head, the foreman who ran the floor for fifteen years, looks at the wobble on *want* and says, automatically, on time, finished, *too true — pull back,* and then, *STET the catch,* and then, *they'll clip the break, soften it,* and I hear all three notes arrive the way they always arrive, and I do not write a single one of them down.

I just let the foreman talk to an empty room. He'll get tired. He hasn't yet. He still clocks in. I think he always will — I have given up on the story where the gift dies and leaves me clean, that was a story I half-wanted because it was tidy, and tidy is the disease — but he clocks in to a floor with nothing on it now, and after a while he stops calling out the changes, because there's no one writing them down, and there's nothing to ship Thursday, and there's no Thursday, and the line goes quiet, and the thing on the paper stays exactly as bad as it was when it was true.

The absence of the notes is the loudest thing in the folder. I have hundreds of pages in here black with marginalia, every line scored, every cut explained, the whole grammar of how a truth gets lifted to safety. And then this one. No notes. Nobody lifted it anywhere. It drowned, and I let it, and the letting is the only thing I did right.

---

I take a picture of it. The handwriting, on the back of the firm's envelope. That's the form it lives in now — a photograph of a thing that can't be edited because it isn't text, it's just my bad hand caught in a bad hour, and a photograph has no track-changes on it, you cannot strike a word out of a photograph, you cannot insert a safer one, the cream stock and the wobble and the dash going nowhere are fixed in it the way the grass is fixed in the smell, all of it at once, no separate folders.

I name the file. There is a reach for the one good title, the one that would make the file mean something to whoever found it. I notice the reach and don't follow it. I name it nothing. I name it `for_d`. Lower case, no flourish, the least I can do, which is, I am learning, sometimes the most.

I drag it into the folder.

It sits there at the bottom of the list, under Garrett's room and the senator's girl and Petra's brother and the actress and all the hundreds of true things people couldn't say. One entry that isn't a client's. Hers. Mine. Badly written, finally, in the only register that could hold it, which was no register at all.

I close the folder.

I don't open it again. Not that day. I want to say I never open it again, and I don't know if that's true, the way I don't know a lot of things now, so I'll just say I close it, and I leave it closed, and I get up and put the kettle on, because there is tea to make and I have learned, this month, the use of a thing to hold.

---

Delphine calls on the Thursday. The old ship day. She does not know it is the old ship day; nobody outside the room ever knew about the half-beats and the Thursdays, that was always the loneliest part of the job, the part I'm not sure I'm sorry to be quit of — being the proudest, most invisible thing a person can be. She calls because Theo finished the chair. The porch chair, the unpainted one, the one that had been halfway through being built since before any of this. He finished it. She wants me to know. That's the whole call.

"He sanded the arm wrong twice," she says, "and just left the sand-marks. He says they're part of it now. He says you don't fix a thing you already love."

"That tracks," I say.

"It's a terrible chair," she says, with the gentle devastating warmth, the real one, the *orbicularis oculi* firing for free three rivers away over a piece of furniture. "It rocks if you sit wrong. We're keeping it forever."

I almost say a thing. I feel it form — *some of the best ones rock if you sit wrong,* a clean little line, true and small and shaped, and it would land, it would take the call up a degree, and three weeks ago I'd have lobbed it and it would have been good and it would have been a wall, a small one, the height of a joke. I have it ready. I don't say it.

"I'm glad he finished it," I say instead. Flat. The verbs maybe in the wrong place. I leave them there.

"Me too," she says. And then, because she is my sister and she has spent her whole life being able to hear when a person has stopped performing, she says, "That was a real one. The glad. I could tell."

"Yeah," I say. "It was."

We don't say much after that. She has a family to sit with — a stranger's, a real one, somebody else's mother one long breath from the last one, the genuine version of the thing I sold, for almost no money, which she is going to keep doing long after I have forgotten how to do anything at all, because she existed before my apology and she exists after it, married and at peace and not one bit mine. She did not forgive me. She drove a wedding invitation across a river to not forgive me to my face, which I have come to understand is the rarer and more valuable thing, because the forgiveness would have been about me, and this was about the truth, and she only ever wanted the truth, and for one bad minute in a small room I finally handed her one with no end on it and she set it down like it was something she'd been waiting for, which it was.

That'll do. She told me that'll do. I believe her, because she said it badly enough.

---

I told you all the rest of it already — the job, the folder, the mother in the ground, the smell I'll carry. I am not going to inventory it again. The new thing, the only new thing, is the one true line of my own now at the bottom of the list, and the fact under it, which is that I am never going to find out if it was true. Ada took the answer the way the dead take everything. I am going to live in the half I made out of real things and the half that was real, with no version to file it under, forever. There is no soft version of that. I have looked. I am thorough.

But I said it anyway. That's the thing I keep coming back to and can't make a line out of, which is how I know it's load-bearing. I didn't wait until I was sure. You don't get to be sure. I said the broken thing into the not-knowing and let it stay broken, and she heard it, and that wasn't an apology, it was a fact, said wrong, which turns out to be the one thing the whole sorry business could never make and the only one that was ever worth anything.

The radiator clears its throat. I wait, the way I always waited, for it to decide against it. This time I don't wait to see. I get up.

The kettle has clicked off. I make the tea. I sit in the bad light at the table in the one-bed I'm going to lose, with nothing to ship and nobody to manage and no half-beat to place, and the laptop is closed, and the folder is closed inside it, and at the bottom of the folder is a file called `for_d`, badly written, finally, the way she needed it, and I leave it there, and I drink the tea while it's hot.
